Far from the promised revolution, we appear to be witnessing an unprecedented experiment in cognitive attrition.The DataThe strength of The Digital Delusion lies in its systematic marshalling of evidence. Horvath draws on international assessments, meta-analyses, and domain-specific studies to build a case that is difficult to dismiss.Book cover of "The Digital Delusion: How Classroom Technology Harms Our Kids’ Learning—And How To Help Them Thrive Again" by Jared Cooney HorvathThe Digital Delusion: How Classroom Technology Harms Our Kids’ Learning—And How To Help Them Thrive Againby Jared Cooney HorvathLME Global, 2025, $18.99, 360 pagesThe international picture he paints is bleak. On PISA assessments, students who use computers more than six hours daily score 66 points lower than nonusers—a gap equivalent to moving from the 50th to the 24th percentile, or roughly two letter grades. The 2022 PISA results showed a 15-point drop in mathematics, the largest single-cycle decline in the assessment’s history. TIMSS 2019 data reveal similar patterns: Daily computer users scored 41 points lower in mathematics and 51 points lower in science compared to infrequent users.The meta-analytic evidence is equally sobering. Horvath synthesizes 398 meta-analyses covering over 21,000 individual studies, finding an overall effect size of +0.29 standard deviations for education technology. This sounds modestly positive until one recognizes that meaningful educational gains typically require effect sizes of +0.40 to +0.50. Only intelligent tutoring systems (+0.52) and interventions for learning disorders (+0.61) consistently exceed this threshold. One-to-one laptop programs, that great hope of education reformers, show an effect size of just +0.16. As Horvath dryly notes, investing in air conditioning has a more beneficial impact on learning than investing in a laptop for every student.Three specific findings deserve attention. First, reading comprehension is consistently worse on screens than on paper, with effect sizes of –0.15 overall and –0.29 for expository text. Screens lack the spatial anchoring that supports memory formation. Second, handwritten notes produce better learning outcomes than typed notes, with effect sizes of –0.19 for immediate recall and –0.42 when reviewing notes. Typing enables shallow transcription, while handwriting forces the brain to process and condense information. Third, handwriting itself builds fine motor skills linked to reading circuits, providing embodied engagement that typing cannot replicate.The MechanismsWhat elevates this book above a mere catalogue of failures is Horvath’s attention to mechanisms. It is not enough to show that screens harm learning; we must understand why. Here, Horvath identifies three intractable problems rooted in the architecture of human cognition.The first is attention. The lateral prefrontal cortex can hold only one ruleset at a time. Humans cannot truly multitask, only switch between tasks. Each switch incurs costs: time loss, reduced accuracy, and weaker memory formation. Digital environments are specifically engineered for rapid task switching. They are, as Horvath puts it, “trying to serve two masters: learning requires stillness; profit requires attention capture.” Children now spend over 2,500 hours annually using devices for rapid-fire media consumption, compared to roughly 450 hours engaged in formal learning. The attentional habits formed in the former context inevitably contaminate the latter.The second mechanism is empathy. The student–teacher relationship produces one of the largest effect sizes in educational research (+0.57), and affective empathy contributes similarly (+0.68). But empathy, Horvath argues, is not merely an emotion; it is physiological synchrony between biological systems. When two people interact, their brain activity, heart rate, and breathing align. Digital tools lack biology, making genuine empathy impossible. The approximately 85 percent dropout rates typical of online learning, echoed during pandemic-era remote schooling, offer a stark illustration of what happens when this biological connection is severed.The third problem is transfer. Where we learn becomes part of what we learn; context is encoded alongside content. Variety in learning environments enables flexible retrieval, while the narrow uniformity of digital contexts creates skills that are far less likely to transfer to complex real-world settings. Horvath draws an important distinction between subtractive and additive transfer. Moving from a more demanding context to a less demanding one (subtractive transfer) is relatively easy, but the reverse (additive transfer) is not. Students who learn primarily on screens develop skills calibrated to that environment; when context changes, particularly in high-pressure situations like examinations, they find themselves stranded.The MythsPhoto of Jared Cooney HorvathJared Cooney HorvathPerhaps the most valuable contribution of The Digital Delusion is its systematic dismantling of the foundational myths that sustain education technology. Horvath identifies five.First is the claim that education is broken. It was not, Horvath argues, until EdTech arrived and began measuring its success against metrics it had itself created. Second is the belief that multimedia enhances learning. Entertainment and learning are not the same thing; the confusion between them produces what Horvath calls “Duolingo learning”: all engagement, little retention. Third is the notion that free choice leads to better learning. The fluency illusion leads students to prefer what feels easy rather than what actually works; ease of processing is mistaken for depth of understanding. Fourth is the assumption that children learn best on their own. The brain stops automatic acquisition of complex knowledge around age five. Thereafter, guided instruction consistently outperforms trial-and-error discovery. Fifth and finally is the promise that intelligent tutors make children more intelligent. In narrow applications, perhaps this is true, but there is no evidence of meaningful transfer beyond the specific skills trained.The StakesNo book of this scope is without limitations, and at times the evidence Horvath presents risks overclaiming. The reversal of the Flynn Effect is a real phenomenon, but its causes remain contested; attributing it primarily to education technology, while plausible, requires more careful causal analysis than meta-analytic correlations can provide.Some of the international assessment data also conflate different types of computer use. Not all screen time is equal, and the harmful effects appear concentrated in particular applications. A student using a well-designed intelligent tutoring system for spaced retrieval practice is doing something categorically different from a student clicking through a gamified quiz app designed to maximize time on platform.END CONTEXT FOR REVIEWPA RT O N E?e Problem“People will come to adore the technologies that undo their capacities to think.”–NEIL POSTMAN, discussing Brave New WorldOceanofPDF.com     | 1 | Wrapped in Lies?e Five Myths ?at Built EdTechMy daughter was given a tablet in year three (3rd Grade). At the time, I thought she was a little young for so much screen time—but the school assured us this was how kids learn best now, so I went along with it. She’s now in year six (6th Grade), and to be honest, I don’t think she’s ready. Her thinking feels scattered, she struggles with writing, and she has a really hard time staying focused, even on things she used to love. It’s like nothing sticks. I wonder if we made a mistake with the technology, but it all sounded so promising back then.–TABITHA, Mother of an 11-Year-Old (Australia)    abitha’s story is increasingly common. Across the world, parents were promised	that	digital	tools	would	unlock	potential,	increase engagement, and make learning fun. Finally, our children would be free toexplore the world on their own terms.But two decades into this digital experiment, the cracks are starting toshow. We’ve watched our children grow ever more anxious, distracted, and falsely conﬁdent in abilities they’ve yet to truly develop. Many of us have been left asking the same question as Tabitha: did we make a mistake??e answer is yes. But our mistake wasn’t in trusting schools or teachers.It was in trusting the seductive promises sold to us by EdTech developers.  Most parents want the same things for their children: to think clearly, read deeply, write persuasively, and develop the habits of mind needed to contribute meaningfully to the wider world. We want kids who are not only curious, but also capable of acting on that curiosity. And we’ve long understood that this journey begins with a solid education.When modern technology entered schools in earnest in the early 2000s,it didn’t challenge these values. On the contrary, it claimed to champion them. EdTech positioned itself as the savior of learning, promising to deepen creativity, expand knowledge, and better prepare students for an ever-changing world.And so we bought in—especially across the West.  In the U.S., 88% of public-school districts now operate one-to-one device programs. In the UK, schools spend over three times more on digital technologies each year than on teacher development. In Australia, there are roughly 450,000 more computers in classrooms than students to use them.  But what if it was all hype? What if EdTech was always destined to harm the very things it promised to improve?   ?ere’s a kid’s party game popular across Europe called Pass the Parcel. Here’s how it works: A large package wrapped in layer upon layer of decorative paper is passed around a circle of children. Each child peels o? one layer, only to reveal… another layer. ?e unwrapping continues, round after round, until one “lucky” child reaches the gift at the center.I say “lucky” because the prize is almost always a letdown: a ﬂimsy toycar or a cheap plastic ring. What looked big and exciting turns out to be hollow—a trinket hidden beneath layers of glitz.?at’s EdTech in a nutshell.  On the surface, digital tools look impressive and sound transformative. But once you strip away the sleek marketing and persuasive language, you’re often left with something woefully underwhelming—even harmful.   Like any good illusion, EdTech’s power lies in belief. Over the past three decades, ﬁve seductive assumptions have taken hold—ideas that we’ve been led to believe are true, but simply aren’t.?ese are the myths that built EdTech:• Education Is Broken• Multimedia Enhances Learning• Free Choice Leads to Better Learning• Kids Learn Best on ?eir Own• Intelligent Tutors Make Kids More Intelligent   We’ve heard these claims echoed for years: by the media, tech gurus, and even by schools themselves. But repetition without scrutiny is how a myth becomes a mandate.  So, let’s unwrap this parcel, one assumption at a time, and see what’s really sitting at the core of EdTech.Myth #1: Education Is BrokenCORE MISCONCEPTION: Traditional schooling is failing, and only EdTech can save it.?is narrative has been repeated so many times in op-eds, on TED stages, and in keynote speeches, that it almost feels self-evident: “schools are outdated and in desperate need of a digital revolution.” But for all itspopularity, this claim is heavy on rhetoric and light on evidence—and that’s no accident.   Crisis sells. By portraying schools as archaic and ine?ective, EdTech not only justiﬁes its existence, but also manufactures demand. After all, it’s hard to sell a “solution” when no real “problem” exists. But when we step back and look at the broader picture, it’s clear the education “crisis” has largely been manufactured.By nearly every meaningful international metric, schools are doing justﬁne. More children have access to education, more are graduating, racial and ethnic disparities are shrinking, gender gaps have closed, bullying is down, and both parents and students continue to report steady levels of educational satisfaction.  Schools may not be perfect—but they aren’t broken. Or at least, they weren’t until Silicon Valley set out to ﬁx them.   Since the early 2000s, two deeply concerning trends have emerged across education: student mental health has sharply declined, and academic performance has signiﬁcantly dropped across much of the developed world. But here’s the twist: both of these trends track closely with and can largely be attributed to the growing presence of digital technology in children’s lives. In other words, the very problems most often used to rationalize EdTech in classrooms are, in no small part, being caused byEdTech itself.  If schools are “broken,” then it was the inﬂux of digital tools that broke them.  One especially common variation of this myth is that schools kill creativity. As the argument goes, students spend too much time in school memorizing facts and not enough time thinking freely. Even ScientiﬁcAmerican has suggested, “Maybe we should let smartphones call up facts as necessary and let students focus on developing analytical skills.”But creativity isn’t the opposite of knowledge—rather, it emerges fromit.In 2022, more than half a million ﬁfteen-year-olds across 63 countriessat for two assessments: one testing academic knowledge (math, science, andreading), and the other creative thinking. Somewhat unsurprisingly, Singapore—a country famous for academic rigor—scored highest on the knowledge exam. But what shocked many observers was that they also scored highest on the creativity exam. In fact, across all countries, the correlation between knowledge and creativity was an astonishing 0.92: that’s nearly perfect.   ?e takeaway is clear: in order for students to think ﬂexibly, they ﬁrst need something to think with.   I once worked with a literature teacher who asked her students to write a modern retelling of Romeo and Juliet. It was immediately clear whichstudents read the play and which hadn’t. ?ose who had engaged deeply with the text produced clever and nuanced updates: one set on a football ﬁeld, another in a divided lunchroom. Meanwhile, students who merely skimmed the text or relied on basic summaries fell back to vague clichés about “forbidden love.” ?eir stories fell ﬂat; not because they lacked creativity, but because they lacked knowledge.And here’s the key: for knowledge to support creativity, it must be storedin long-term memory. As we’ll explore more deeply in Chapter 6, mere access to information is never enough. Using a computer to “look things up” won’t build creativity any more than staring at a photo of food will satisfy hunger. Until knowledge lives inside you, it can’t do any meaningful work.   ?e real danger isn’t that schools are broken. It’s that we’ve been persuaded they are—usually by the very people selling the solution.Myth #2: Multimedia Enhances LearningCORE MISCONCEPTION: ?e more stimulating the experience, the better the learning.If you’ve ever watched one of those stunning David Attenborough documentaries, then you understand the captivating power of rich visuals and immersive audio. As we watch a blue whale breach the surface of a stormy gray sea in slow motion, it’s hard to imagine we could ever forget that image.But of course, we do—just as surely as we forget almost everything else.   ?is underscores a simple but crucial point: entertainment and learning are not the same thing. What it takes to capture attention is very di?erent than what it takes to build deep understanding and long-term retention.   EdTech developers see children glued to apps like YouTube and TikTok and assume their tools should do the same. So they load their platforms with bells, whistles, and digital ﬂourishes designed to hold attention at any cost. ?e assumption is that if eyes are on screens, minds are engaged.  But decades of research reveal that multimedia on its own doesn’t enhance learning. What matters is the teaching that surrounds it: good teaching with multimedia yields the same results as good teaching without it. It’s not the technology that makes the di?erence; it’s the pedagogy.   At ﬁrst glance, this might make multimedia seem harmless. If it doesn’t help but doesn’t hurt, then what’s the problem?  Unfortunately, poorly designed multimedia has long been shown to actively undermine learning. By distracting students and diverting attention away from core material, unnecessary audiovisual elements often interfere with understanding rather than deepen it.Here’s a personal example.When I was in ﬁrst grade, I spent hours playing a computer game called?e Oregon Trail. ?e goal was to guide a wagon party across 19th-century America while keeping at least one member alive. To this day, I can tell you which keys to press to shoot a bear, the odds of successfully fording a river, and the likelihood of dying from dysentery (100%).   I only recently—nearly 40 years later—learned something surprising: ?e Oregon Trail was meant to be a history lesson.   ?ere’s no question I was deeply engaged when I played—but because my attention was ﬁxed on the mechanics of the game, I retained almost nothing about the actual Oregon Trail. I don’t know where the trail began, how many people traveled it, or why dysentery was such a serious threat two centuries ago. ?e multimedia didn’t enhance the lesson; it completely misdirected it.?is same pattern plays out in classrooms every day. Many EdTech toolsuse points, badges, timers, and other game-like features to make learning “fun.” While students may be excited and absorbed by these programs, their focus is often diverted from the content. In the end, they’re more likely to remember the score they earned than the material they were meant to learn.Consider DuoLingo.  If you (like more than half a billion people worldwide) have used this app, you know how addictive it can be. But in a recent independent study, DuoLingo users scored lower on a standardized language test than users of Babbel, despite spending signiﬁcantly more time on the app.   ?e reason? Engagement is not the same as learning. DuoLingo is designed to pull users in and keep them coming back. Babbel, on the other hand, takes a more traditional approach by prioritizing explicit instruction and pedagogical substance. It may be less ﬂashy than DuoLingo, but it’s far more e?ective.   Real comprehension is often e?ortful, and rarely entertaining; there are no shortcuts. If our goal is deep, durable learning, then our time, energy, and resources are better spent improving teaching—not upgrading multimedia.Myth #3: Free Choice Leads to Better LearningCORE MISCONCEPTION: Learners always know what works best for learning.Let’s start with a quick survey:You have questions about an important medical procedure. Do you…A) Look at a diagram outlining the procedureB) Have a detailed discussion with your doctorC) Use interactive 3D models to walk through the procedureWhen learning a new board game, do you…A) Use diagrams that explain various game strategiesB) Listen to someone explain the game while you ask questionsC) Play the game and ﬁgure it out as you goWhen using the internet, do you most often…A) Watch videosB) Listen to podcastsC) Play interactive games   If you answered primarily A, you’re a visual learner. Primarily B, you’re an auditory learner. Primarily C, you’re a kinesthetic learner.And now, the twist: none of that really matters.  Although the idea of matching instruction to a learner’s preferred style feels right, research has consistently shown that it has no meaningful impact on learning outcomes. To paraphrase cognitive psychologists Doug Rohrer and Hal Pashler, do we really believe that students who prefer reading would learn anatomy better if we removed all diagrams from their textbooks??e truth is, instruction works best when it’s aligned with the contentrather than the learner. If we teach geography using maps, music history by listening to operas, or dance by moving our bodies, everyone learns more e?ectively—regardless of their “learning style.”  A second dimension of personalization concerns learning strategies. While it may feel validating to let students freely choose their own study techniques, decades of research show people are surprisingly poor judges of what actually supports learning.   ?is is known as the ﬂuency illusion: a well-documented cognitive bias whereby we mistake ease for understanding. When a learning strategy feels smooth and e?ortless, we tend to believe we’re learning more than we truly are.  A seminal study from 2006 illustrates this idea well. One group of students was asked to re-read a passage four times: a strategy that feels easy and ﬂuent. Another group was asked to read the same passage once, then complete three recall-based quizzes: a strategy that feels much harder and slower.When asked to predict how much they’d remember, the re-readinggroup estimated 69%, while the recall group estimated only 57%. But on a memory test one week later, the re-readers scored just 40% while the recallers scored 61%.   ?e group that struggled learned more without realizing it. ?e group that coasted learned less without noticing.   ?is cognitive illusion plays out frequently with EdTech. A teacher I once worked with in Australia told me that her school had adopted a set of highly customizable learning apps because, as she put it, “the students likedthem more than real teaching.” ?e programs let students choose fonts, colors, pace, and content order—all of which felt empowering. Unfortunately, when her class sat for the ﬁnal exam, their scores dropped 18% compared to the previous year.  Even so, her end-of-year teacher review was absolutely glowing. Students reported enjoying the class more than ever, and her evaluation metrics hit an all-time high. It seems the students really did prefer the apps to real teaching, despite learning considerably less.   ?is is one of EdTech’s great miscalculations. Many developers assume students are better served when they can adapt instruction and strategies to ﬁt their preferences. But in practice, this type of personalization often breeds a false sense of mastery while quietly undermining real learning.Myth #4: Kids Learn Best on Their OwnCORE MISCONCEPTION: Increased student autonomy will yield betteroutcomes.If you give students a laptop and let them freely roam the internet in the name of “self-directed learning,” what do you think they’re most likely to do?A Washington teacher once reﬂected:During the COVID pandemic, I taught seventh grade. Every day, I sent students a list of videos, websites, and online activities to complete. ?e idea was that they’d learn the material independently, and we’d use a one-hour live session every other day to dive deeper into the core topics.But that hour never went very deep. Most students didn’t do the work, and the few who did were usually more confused than when they started. ?at one precious hour became a frustrating scramble to reteach everything from scratch.  We put kids in charge of their own learning, then acted surprised when it didn’t work. Looking back, it’s kind of embarrassing we ever thought it would.   ?e belief that children learn best when they’re in charge has become the spiritual blueprint for much of EdTech culture. Unfortunately, it relies on a fundamental misunderstanding of how learning actually works.   Very young children seem to learn e?ortlessly. ?is is because during our earliest years, the brain is wired to absorb information through exposure alone. ?at’s why toddlers can pick up one, two, or even three languages without formal instruction.   Many EdTech developers see how e?ciently toddlers learn and assume all learning should be that easy. But they overlook one critical fact: around age ﬁve, the brain begins shifting from broad, exploratory processing towardmore specialized, e?cient processing. From this point forward, while we can still absorb some information passively, deep learning requires conscious e?ort and deliberate practice. ?at’s why adults can’t simply “pick up” a second language —we need years of repetition and focus.   Once the brain enters this more specialized state, humans typically learn in one of two ways:   1) Trial-and-Error: stumbling through mistakes to discover patterns on our own.  2) Guidance: learning from someone who already understands the material.   Trial-and-error can work, but it’s incredibly slow and unreliable. If you needed heart surgery, you probably wouldn’t trust a doctor who skipped medical school and relied on YouTube videos and self-exploration.   ?is is precisely why schools exist: to provide expert guidance and make learning beyond toddlerhood faster, deeper, and more accurate. Good teaching doesn’t get in the way of e?ective learning—it enables it.  When students are left to their own devices (both literally and ﬁguratively), they often ﬂounder through trial-and-error. Some may thrive, but the majority will struggle. ?ey’ll click through lessons, play with interactive elements, and answer practice questions—but when tested later, they’ll show little retention and even less understanding.Teachers aren’t cheerleaders; they’re coaches. ?ey’re not in theclassroom to o?er sideline support; they’re meant to actively guide. Great teachers focus attention, correct misconceptions, and help students go further and faster than they ever could on their own.Real learning still depends on real teaching.Myth #5: Intelligent Tutors Make Kids More IntelligentCORE MISCONCEPTION: Adaptive algorithms are more e?ective than human teachers.In the early 1930s, psychologist B. F. Skinner placed a hungry rat inside a box with a small lever. When the rat pressed the lever, it received a food pellet. Before long, it learned to press the lever whenever it wanted a snack.   Two decades later, after visiting his daughter’s fourth-grade classroom, Skinner had a revelation: why not bring the same approach to schools? So, he refashioned his rat box into a full-ﬂedged “teaching machine.” Students would answer a question, press a lever, and receive immediate feedback—either right or wrong.Soon after, inventor Norman Crowder added a twist. He built a machinethat didn’t just give feedback; it adapted. Get a question right, and the next would be harder. Get it wrong, and the next would be easier. ?is marked the birth of the intelligent tutor—and nearly 70 years later, the core logic remains unchanged. Modern apps like Lumosity, Simply Piano, and Freeleticsmay swap levers for touchscreens, but the model remains identical: practice, adapt, repeat.Of all the myths driving EdTech, this one comes closest to the truth.?ere’s solid evidence that adaptive digital tutors can support learning—but only under speciﬁc conditions.   ?ese tools tend to work best in narrow domains with clear right-or-wrong answers, like arithmetic or spelling. But they struggle with subjects that demand open interpretation or complex reasoning, like literature or science.  Another key issue is transfer. While students may improve within the tutoring program, they often fail to apply that learning outside of it. ?is has been a persistent challenge for over a century. As Sidney Pressey, inventor of the ﬁrst teaching machine, lamented back in 1926, “If the [learning material] was rephrased or presented in a di?erent context, almost nothing seemed retained.”Beneath all this lies a deep irony. EdTech vendors often tout adaptabilityas the greatest strength of digital tools. But you know what else is adaptive? Teachers. And teachers adapt in far more powerful ways.   Algorithms can identify wrong answers, but they can’t sense confusion, interpret tone, or tell the di?erence between deep understanding and lucky guessing. Human teachers do this every day. ?ey read facial expressions, observe hesitation, and ﬁeld spontaneous questions. Digital tools may be able to adapt instruction, but only teachers can humanize it.What’s more, whereas adaptive systems are conﬁned to individual users,human teachers can adapt to entire groups. ?ey shift pace when the room goes quiet, pause when heads tilt sideways, and follow the energy of the class to guide instruction.Or at least, they used to.  A veteran teacher recently told me she feels increasingly disconnected from her students. “Half the time, I’m just looking at the backs of screens,” she said. “Instead of eyes, I see the glowing Apple logo.” Without real-time feedback from her class, she struggles to read the room—she can’t tell who’s lost, who’s distracted, or where the group needs to go next.Capitalizing on this growing disconnect, some now argue that artiﬁcialintelligence will soon be able to mimic everything a teacher can do. And while that may be true in a limited, technical sense, one crucial human ingredient will always remain out of reach: empathy. We’ll explore this more deeply in Chapter 3, but for now it’s enough to say this: without empathy, intelligent tutors will always fall short, especially for struggling students.In the end, adaptive systems can support learning. But that learningtends to be narrow and rarely transfers beyond the platform. As MIT professor Justin Reich puts it, “…adaptive tutors have some applications in learning to read, but very limited applications in reading to learn.”So Now Then…Most parents want their children to grow up happy, healthy, and well-equipped to face whatever challenges may come their way. And for centuries, a strong general education served as the foundation for preparing our kids to engage meaningfully with the wider world. No system is perfect, but traditional schooling has helped hundreds of millions of students grow into capable, competent adults.EdTech tapped directly into these values.   It promised to supercharge school by making learning faster, easier, and more fun. Wrapped in the language of empowerment, EdTech sold us the dream of raising a generation smarter than any that came before.  But as we’ve seen, once you strip away the marketing gloss, the ideas driving EdTech are naïve at best—and manipulative at worst. Which leaves us with a di?cult question: were we duped by clever salesmanship, or did EdTech developers simply never bother to understand how learning actually works before ﬂooding classrooms with devices?Honestly, I’m not sure which is worse.   But here’s what I do know: the myths that built EdTech are just that—myths.   ?e tools now embedded in schools around the world rest on shaky assumptions, with little evidence to justify their widespread use. And if the foundations are faulty, then it’s reasonable to expect these tools are doing more harm than good.   If we truly care about our children’s development, then we can no longer a?ord to be fooled by what merely looks like progress. It’s time to stop trusting hype and start demanding proof.  In the next chapter, we’ll do exactly that. Let’s examine the data to see what impact EdTech is truly having on our children’s learning.OceanofPDF.com    | 2 | Proof of FailureWhat the Data Really Say About EdTech’s Impact on Learning?e question isn’t whether students are still learning with all this digital technology. Of course they are—kids always learn; they can’t help it. ?e real question is whether they’re learning as much and as deeply as students did before. After 28 years in the classroom, I can say without hesitation: no, they’re not. Not even close.–DENNIS, Teacher since 1998 (Hong Kong)magine standing in front of a classroom. Where once you saw rows of eager faces, now you see bowed heads lit by glowing tablets. You hesitateto ask a question, knowing how hard it is to pry attention away from the screens—and whatever answer you get is likely to be monosyllabic anyway. You care deeply for your students, but the pattern is increasingly hard to ignore: thinking is shallower, writing is weaker, and even your top performers struggle to retain what you teach.?is isn’t what you signed up for.  Unfortunately, it’s the new reality many teachers face every day. Classrooms have never been perfect, and nostalgia can blur the truth—but veteran educators don’t need a randomized control trial to know that something has changed. ?ey’ve watched the slow erosion unfold over decades, all in the name of “progress.”When schools began embracing EdTech en masse, it was under thebanner of modernization. Teachers were told these tools would unlock unprecedented levels of understanding and creativity; that students would thrive as never before. And so, they leaned in: teachers eager to evolve their practice worked hard to integrate digital devices in ways they believed would help students succeed.But now, many of those same teachers are asking: why does it feel like kidsare regressing? Some even ﬁnd themselves missing the “good old days” of blackboards and paper. More and more are starting to wonder if things were actually better back then.  Of course, this isn’t the ﬁrst time a tool has been widely embraced without anyone stopping to consider what it might ultimately cost.  In 1935, Portuguese neurologist Egas Moniz proposed that mental illness came from “ﬁxed neural circuits” in the brain. His solution? A surgical procedure designed to sever those circuits, which he called the leukotomy.   Several years later, American neurologist Walter Freeman took this idea further. He simpliﬁed the surgery into a ﬁve-minute operation that required no anesthesia and no formal medical training. He called his new procedure the lobotomy.   Freeman’s creation caught ﬁre. Between 1940 and 1970, roughly 90,000 people around the world, some as young as four years old, underwent lobotomies.   ?ere was just one problem: the surgery never truly worked. While lobotomies sometimes reduced the visible symptoms of mental illness, they ignored the root causes—and came at a devastating cost: emotional ﬂattening, cognitive impairment, and loss of personality. It was like cutting the wires to a ﬁre alarm. Sure, the noise stops, but the ﬁre is still burning.Eventually, the science caught up. Researchers discovered the brain isn’tﬁxed and that it can, in fact, be reliably reshaped through time, therapy, and medication.I share this story for two reasons.  First, because it shows that every tool lives or dies by the assumptions it rests on. If those assumptions are wrong—as they were with “ﬁxed neural circuits”—then the tool is destined to fail, no matter how widely it’s adopted.  Like the lobotomy, EdTech was embraced with astonishing speed and very little skepticism. It was hailed as the future of education, promising transformation and personalization at scale. But as we saw in the previous chapter, those promises were built on ﬂawed assumptions, leaving little reason to believe they’ll ever be realized.Second, when a tool fails, it rarely goes quietly. It causes damage. Itleaves scars. Someone always pays the price for false promises.  Digital technologies are not neutral; they reshape how students think, behave, and relate. And when EdTech fails, the cost isn’t measured in dollars—it’s measured in the learning and development of our children.   We’ve exposed EdTech’s faulty foundations. Now it’s time to assess the damage.International Testing“It is very di?cult to ﬁnd conclusive and consistent evidence tosupport the hypothesis of a positive impact of [digital technology] use on student performance as measured by standardized tests.”–EdTech and Student Performance: A Systematic Review (2022).EdTech companies love to highlight narrow success stories. But if digital tools truly improved learning, we’d expect to see positive gains at scale.?at’s why international testing matters: it reveals whether local hype holds up on the global stage.PISAPISA (the Program for International Student Assessment) is the world’s largest standardized test. Every three years, hundreds of thousands of 15-year-old students across dozens of countries complete the exam, which assesses knowledge across math, reading, and science.  In 2012, 2015, and 2018, PISA asked students how much time they spent using digital devices during a typical school day. When those answers were compared with test scores, the results told a clear and troubling story.   ?e more time students spent on screens at school, the further their scores fell. On average, those who used computers for more than six hours per day scored 66 points lower than their peers who didn’t use them at all.?at’s the di?erence between the 50th and the 24th percentile—equivalent to a two letter-grade drop.   And this wasn’t just a developing-world issue. Among wealthy OECD countries, the average drop was even steeper: 67 points.  Some keen-eyed readers might notice one wrinkle: beginning in 2015, students who used computers for 1–60 minutes per day slightly outperformed those who didn’t use them at all. Tech proponents often cite this as proof that moderate screen time can boost learning.Not so fast.   What most people don’t realize is that in 2015, PISA transitioned from a paper-based to a computer-based test. ?is shift didn’t boost performance for students proﬁcient with digital tools—but it did lower scores by 18 points for students who never used computers. In other words, the supposed beneﬁt of moderate EdTech use wasn’t a gain at all; it was a penalty paid by the uninitiated.But the story gets worse.  Researchers have long known about a phenomenon called the mode e?ect: the tendency for students to perform worse on computerized tasks than on pen-and-paper ones. ?is is why so many students struggle when schools move homework and assignments online. As one parent told me, “My son was never a weak reader, but once his school switched to digital textbooks, ideas just stopped sticking.”So why didn’t the mode e?ect show up in 2015 when PISA went digital?Because the test makers buried it.  When overall performance came in lower than expected, the OECD quietly excluded 91 questions from the ﬁnal results. ?at’s right—a full 34% of the exam was simply erased to preserve the illusion that nothing had changed.   Fortunately, a team of independent researchers gained access to ﬁeld trial data and uncovered what the OECD had worked so hard to conceal: the shift from paper to digital testing reduced scores by 14 points across the board. Everyone did worse—even the heavy tech users. So much for the claim that more time with EdTech leads to better digital performance.?e most recent PISA was administered in 2022. Although students areno longer asked about school-based tech use, global EdTech adoption has only grown year on year. As such, it should come as no surprise that average scores dropped a further 15 points in math and 9 points in reading—the largest single-cycle declines in the exam’s history.For those willing to look, the lesson is obvious.TIMSSTIMSS (the Trends in International Mathematics and Science Study) is the longest-running global assessment of student performance in math and science. Every four years, hundreds of thousands of 4th and 8th graders from dozens of countries take part.  In 2019, TIMSS asked students how often they used digital devices during math and science lessons. Once again, the pattern was unmistakable: more tech use at school led to lower scores.  On average, students who used computers nearly every day in class scored about 41 points lower in math and 51 points lower in science than students who rarely used them. ?at’s a drop from the 50th to the 32nd percentile—equivalent to one and a half letter grades. And as before, this drop wasn’t limited to lower-income nations. Among OECD countries, the average decline was 42 points in math and 48 points in science.PIRLSPIRLS (the Progress in International Reading Literacy Study) is the only long-term international exam focused exclusively on reading skills in younger children. Every ﬁve years, hundreds of thousands of 4th grade students across dozens of countries participate.   ?e only time PIRLS asked about general in-school computer use was way back in 2006.   Whereas moderate computer use showed some beneﬁt, frequent use led to signiﬁcantly worse performance. Students who used computers at school nearly every day scored 46 points lower overall (44 points in OECD countries) than those who rarely used them.  But this data is nearly two decades old. More recent ﬁndings are far more troubling.  A 2023 study of nearly 150,000 fourth grade students across the U.S. found that, “…even small daily amounts of use of digital devices in the classroom (here, 30 min) are negatively related to scores on a reading comprehension test.”  Furthermore, in 2021 PIRLS made the shift from paper-based to computer-based testing. ?at year, average scores dropped by 27 points across the board. While COVID disruptions likely played a role, PIRLS designers acknowledged that the aforementioned mode e?ect signiﬁcantly contributed to the decline.As a ﬁnal note, in 2021 the United States administered both versions ofthe PIRLS: some students took the exam on paper, while others took it on a computer. Although the questions were identical, performance on the digital version was so poor that the U.S. chose not to release those scores; only the paper results were made public.Although the exact size of the digital gap has never been disclosed, thedecision to bury that data speaks volumes.But What About…One statistic frequently cited by EdTech advocates is that access to digital tools correlates with higher test scores, often around +0.40. On the surface, this makes it seem like more technology leads to better learning.While technically correct, this number is deeply misleading.   ?at +0.40 correlation doesn’t reﬂect the power of technology; it reﬂects socioeconomic advantage. Students in wealthier countries tend to have greater access not just to computers, but to everything: textbooks, tutors, after-school programs, well-trained teachers, healthy meals, and stable homes. In other words, it’s not the devices boosting scores; it’s the environment that comes with them.Once researchers control for national wealth and baseline achievementlevels, the correlation doesn’t just shrink—it reverses direction. In fact, the adjusted association between EdTech access and performance drops to-0.57.  As the OECD itself noted, “…countries that have invested less in introducing computers into school have improved faster, on average, than countries that have invested more. Results are similar across reading, mathematics, and science.”Academic Research“?ere’s clear scientiﬁc evidence that digital tools impair rather than enhance student learning.”–?e Karolinska Institute (2023)I recently spoke with a parent group debating which EdTech programs schools should maintain, and which they should abandon because they stiﬂed learning. ?e problem was that no matter how poorly a toolperformed overall, there was always one or two parents eager to defend it because their child had done well on it.   ?is is the danger of anecdote. Fifteen families might report harm, but a single counterexample is often enough to derail any conversation and stall progress. As Cicero might remind us, no opinion is so foolish that it lacks defenders.   So what happens when we look beyond personal experiences? When we push past isolated cases and examine the broader body of academic evidence?  To do this, we’ll use meta-analysis: a method of combining data from hundreds (or even thousands) of individual research studies to generate a more accurate estimate of a tool’s general impact. ?e results are typically expressed as an e?ect-size: a single number showing how much an intervention moves the needle. Positive values suggest beneﬁt, while negative values suggest harm.What do meta-analyses say about EdTech (see top of p. 32)?  At ﬁrst glance, things look promising. Every e?ect size is positive, so digital tools must beneﬁt learning, right?Not quite.  In 2023, statistician John Hattie analyzed over 350,000 studies of learning. His ﬁnding was shocking yet somehow totally predictable: 95% of all educational interventions show a positive e?ect size.  In other words, nearly everything “works” in education—and the few exceptions are associated with obvious harms like chronic illness (-0.51), anxiety (-0.40), and bullying (-0.28).   Educational research tends to statistically over-inﬂate even the weakest of interventions. ?e reason for this concerns heterogeneity of comparison.  When enthusiasts boast that EdTech has an e?ect size of +0.29, a very important question almost always goes unasked: compared to what? For that number to mean anything, something has to be sitting at zero—so what’s the baseline?  Most people assume the comparison must be teacher-led instruction. But meta-analyses show that direct instruction averages an e?ect size of+0.56, so that can’t be the baseline. Maybe it’s student-led approaches. But discovery learning averages +0.27, so that can’t be it either.What exactly is EdTech being measured against?   ?at’s the problem: there isn’t any consistent comparison. Unlike research in medicine or physics, where control conditions are standardized, educational studies often compare interventions to wildly di?erent things(with a surprising number using no baseline comparison at all). ?is explains why almost every educational study reports some degree of beneﬁt, and why we can’t treat all positive e?ect sizes as meaningful.So, if we can’t use zero as our reference point, what should we use?  Most researchers agree that meaningful educational gains begin somewhere between +0.40 and +0.50. To give EdTech every beneﬁt of the doubt, let’s use the least conservative benchmark of +0.40. Now, go back and take another look at the earlier table. Suddenly, the picture looks very di?erent.In nearly every context, EdTech doesn’t even come close to theminimum threshold for meaningful learning impact. And in key areas, like 1-to-1 computing, distance education, and digital support for disadvantaged students, the shortfall is especially stark.  Only two domains show meaningful beneﬁt: intelligent tutoring systems and interventions for learning disorders. And that shouldn’t come as a surprise. As noted in the previous chapter, adaptive tools can support surface-level skill development in narrow, well-deﬁned domains. When it comes to remediation, the same logic applies. As childhood learning disorders expert Bruce Pennington explains:“Children with [speciﬁc learning disorders] do not appear to require aqualitatively di?erent instructional approach from typical learners; they may just need more of it, broken down into smaller steps, and with more opportunities for review…”   Put simply, digital tools that aid in building foundational skills can also beneﬁt students who require more time and structure for mastery.  But intelligent tutors are not all-purpose solutions. ?ey succeed only because they are tightly constrained, highly repetitive, and focused on isolated skills. General education, by contrast, aims higher. Schools strive tofoster adaptability, creativity, and deep understanding—and that’s precisely where digital tools fall short.  So yes, students can learn with EdTech: the e?ect sizes aren’t negative. But this learning is almost always slower, shallower, and less durable than what’s achieved through human-centered methods like explicit instruction (+0.64), guided discussions (+0.82), and jigsaw teaching (+1.16).   ?e data is clear: if we care about deep, lasting learning, then we already know what works—and it isn’t a screen.Three Specific Issues Reading: Paper vs ScreenIn a revelation that will come as no surprise to avid readers, more than twodecades of research has shown that people comprehend better and retain more when reading from paper rather than from screens.   ?is screen inferiority e?ect is especially pronounced when students read to learn (e.g. studying a textbook) rather than for enjoyment. Furthermore, this e?ect appears to be intensifying. Analyses show a steady decline of-0.01 per year, suggesting that rather than improving at digital reading, students are somehow getting worse.  Why are screens so poor for reading? Have you ever tried to return to something you’d read previously in a digital document, only to discover you can’t ﬁnd it? You know it was there a few scrolls ago, but now it’s gone.?at’s because your brain is searching for a place—but on screens, place doesn’t exist. Screens strip text of any static location. Unfortunately, location is a key anchor of human memory.   Much like a GPS, the hippocampus (our brain’s memory center) builds a continuous mental map of the world around us. And each time we learn something new, that memory becomes tied to a speciﬁc three-dimensional location within that map.   When we read from paper, each word occupies a ﬁxed, physical location. If you’re holding a print version of this book, for instance, then this sentenceexists right here—and this spatial position will become part of the memory you’re currently forming. ?is spatial anchoring is why readers can often recall where in a book something appeared (about 20 pages in, left-hand page, near the top), even if they can’t remember the exact words.   Unfortunately, digital texts lack a ﬁxed spatial layout. If you’re scrolling through a PDF of this book, then this sentence ﬁrst appeared at the bottom of your screen, is now sitting somewhere near the middle, and will soon vanish out the top. With no stable location, the spatial sca?old that supports memory formation collapses.As a result, reading from screens often triggers an unconscious shiftfrom deep comprehension to shallow skimming.   ?is helps explain why reading scores on the PISA and PIRLS exams dropped so sharply after these tests moved online. It also sheds light on some ba?ing decisions made by modern test developers.   Consider the SAT. In 2024, this U.S. high school exit exam transitioned from paper to digital. Along with this switch came a major overhaul of the reading comprehension section.  In previous versions, students read ﬁve complex passages of 750 words each and answered ten questions per passage. In the new online version, they are tasked with reading 54 short passages of just 75 words and answer a single question about each one.  Rather than assessing the skill of deep reading as it’s traditionally been understood, the test developers simply redeﬁned the skill itself to better suit the digital medium. In other words, for our children, reading comprehension now means the shallow extraction of a single idea from a bite-sized snippet of text.We’re not adapting tools to ﬁt our kids—we’re reshaping our kids to ﬁtthe tools.?at’s not innovation. ?at’s surrender.Notetaking: Pen vs Keyboard?e data are quite clear on this point: taking notes by hand leads to signiﬁcantly better learning than typing notes on a computer. Furthermore, this longhand superiority e?ect more than doubles when students review their notes before an exam.  To put this into perspective, imagine a class of 2000 students taking notes—half by hand, and half using a laptop. Everything else is identical: same lectures, same assignments, same discussions.  Assuming a class average is 75 (out of 100) and a standard deviation of ten, we’d expect 114 As in the handwriting group, but only 84 in the typinggroup—a 26% di?erence. At the other end, we’d expect 58 Fs among the typists, but only 36 among the handwriters—a 38% di?erence.  When my oldest niece started at a one-to-one digital high school, she gave herself a new nickname: the stenographer. At ﬁrst, I thought she was joking. But the more she explained, the more it made sense. “I don’t really have to think during class,” she told me. “I just type out whatever the teacher says.” Whenever I asked her what she’d learned that day, she’d always shrug and say, “I don’t know—I’d have to go look at my notes.”In that single sentence lies the reason pens beat keyboards.   ?ere are two basic kinds of notetaking: shallow and deep. Shallow notetaking is concerned with transcribing as many words as possible. Did I hear every word and write it down correctly? While this may capture a lot of content, it undermines learning by directing attention only onto the sound and sequence of words.Deep notetaking, by contrast, emphasizes actively understanding andorganizing ideas. Do I grasp the concept being discussed? While this may result in fewer notes overall, it enhances learning by directing attention to the meaning of and relationship between ideas.?e ultimate issue, then, is one of speed.  Typing is fast. Many students (my niece among them) can type as quickly as a teacher speaks, making shallow transcription seem like a logical choice. In fact, studies consistently show that typed notes include more verbatim text and fewer transformed ideas: a hallmark of superﬁcial engagement.   Handwriting, on the other hand, is slow. No one can write as quickly as a teacher speaks, so pen-and-paper notetakers are forced to focus on meaning.?ey must decide what truly matters as they summarize, reorganize, and even reframe material mid-sentence: classic signs of deep cognitive engagement.  Slower writing promotes deeper thinking, and there’s no shortcut for that.Literacy: Handwriting vs TypingIn an increasingly digital world, should young children still learn to write by hand? Or would it be wiser to focus early instruction on typing?   ?e answer to this is more signiﬁcant than most people realize. Beyond notetaking, there are three critical reasons why handwriting remains essential, especially in the early years.1) Handwriting Builds Fine Motor Skills  Whereas typing involves broad and repetitive ﬁnger movements, handwriting requires precise and dynamic control. Every handwritten character demands constant micro-adjustments in pressure, angle, and motion—movements that strengthen ﬁne motor coordination.  But this is about more than penmanship. Writing and reading draw on similar neural systems within the brain, and strengthening one helps develop the other. ?is means ﬁne motor skill is closely linked to literacy. Children who can write ﬂuently by hand are more likely to read ﬂuently by eye.Recent brain imaging backs this up. When pre-literate children areasked to write, trace, or type letters, only handwriting activates the brain’s reading circuit—the same network they will later use to decode printed text. As	neuroscientist	Stanislas	Dehaene	explains:	“?ere	is	a	corerecognition of the gesture in the written word—a sort of recognition by mental simulation in your brain—and it seems that this is contributing in unique ways we didn’t realize. Learning is made easier.”2) Handwriting Beneﬁts from Variation that Typing Lacks   In the next chapter, we’ll explore the concept of transfer—our ability to apply learned skills in new situations. For now, one key principle matters: the more varied the learning experiences are, the more adaptable the resulting skill will be.   ?is is why you can easily spot a family member from across a crowded street: you’ve seen their face in dozens of di?erent contexts. But when you run into a coworker at the grocery store, you might walk right past them. Outside of the o?ce context, your brain lacks the usual cues, and recognition falters.?e same principle applies to writing.   Handwriting is inherently varied. Each tool (pencils, crayons, markers) feels di?erent in your hand. Each surface (paper, cardboard, blackboard) o?ers unique resistance. Each letter (uppercase, lowercase, cursive) demands slightly di?erent motor patterns. ?is variability makes handwriting a deep, ﬂexible motor skill; one that adapts well across settings.Children who learn handwriting ﬁrst tend to pick up typing incrediblyquickly. ?eir ﬁne motor control transfers swiftly from pen to keyboard.   Typing, on the other hand, is inherently uniform. While keyboards may di?er slightly in size or layout, the basic action of pressing a button never changes. Whether you tap the keys lightly or pound them with force, the result is always the same. ?is consistency makes typing a shallow, rigid motor skill; one that doesn’t adapt well across settings.In fact, children from tech-heavy classrooms who become ﬂuent typistsoften show weaker handwriting and literacy skills than peers in more analogenvironments. Because typing doesn’t demand the nuanced motor control of handwriting, the skill doesn’t transfer in the same way.Put simply, handwriting builds a foundation that typing cannot replicate.3) Handwriting Activates ?inking in Ways Typing Can’t   ?is may be the most abstract point in the chapter, but it’s also the most profound.  Many people believe that thinking happens entirely in the brain, as if we’re just gray matter hitching a ride inside a body. But this misses something essential: we don’t merely have bodies—we are bodies. Learning doesn’t arise from the brain alone; it emerges from the rhythms, movements, and sensations of our entire physical selves.Typing is e?cient, but disembodied. It treats writing as the mechanicaltranscription of already-formed ideas. Every keystroke is the same, the output uniform, and the thinking often ﬁnished before our ﬁngers ever move.   Handwriting is something else entirely. It’s slower, more deliberate, and fundamentally embodied. When we write by hand, we don’t just record thoughts—we shape them. ?e arc of the wrist, the weight of the pen, the rhythm of our breathing: all of this extends cognition beyond the brain.   In essence, handwriting is thinking. Whereas digital tools may be useful for reﬁning ideas, only handwriting reliably helps us create them.  Handwriting is also deeply personal. Whereas typed words are sterile and interchangeable, handwriting is expressive and ever-changing. Your penmanship is as unique as your ﬁngerprint. Watch a child write their name for the ﬁrst time—the furrowed brow, the shaky letters, the proud smile.?ose marks on the page are more than text: they are a declaration of self.?is is me.So Now Then…A school principal in the U.S. recently shared that her sta? quietly reintroduced paper worksheets and handwritten assignments after 12 years of one-to-one laptops. She stressed “quietly” because she feared backlash from parents who expected their children to be using the latest digital tools.But the backlash never came.   Over the next two years of embracing analog work, student engagement and learning increased, behavioral and wellbeing issues declined, and parents (by and large) were relieved.   She reached out to me with the real question: Why? Why did everything improve so quickly and so clearly?   We’ve looked at the data and seen that outside of a few narrow domains suited to adaptive repetition, EdTech impairs learning. But data can only tell us that a tool is not working. If we want to change minds and help ourchildren’s schools make smarter choices, we need to understand why these tools keep falling short.   Fortunately, tools don’t fail at random; they fail for reasons. In the case of EdTech, those reasons are rooted in the architecture of the human mind. Digital tools clash with the way human beings are built to learn.   Yes, the small mechanisms described in this chapter help explain speciﬁc outcomes—like why digitization weakens reading and notetaking—but they only scratch the surface. To truly understand the problem, and how to ﬁx it, we need broader mechanisms: ones that can explain global trends.   In the next chapter, we’ll explore three biological barriers that no digital tool can ever overcome; three fundamental truths about how deep learning works, and why our children can’t a?ord any more false promises.OceanofPDF.com     | 3 | Against the Brain?e ?ree Intractable Problems with EdTechAccording to my son’s online report card, he’s doing great. All his scores are up and it looks like he’s improving in every subject—but in real life, I don’t see it. Our conversations are shallow and his writing reads like a third grader’s. Honestly, I have no idea how he’s learning anything, because every time I see him on the computer he’s just gaming. I know I sound cynical, and I wasn’t a great student myself, but I swear we learned more with paper and pencil than they do with all this tech. I can’t prove it, but I know something is o?.–JAMES Father of a 13-Year-Old (Canada)hen I ﬁrst met James, he was pacing quietly in the back of a crowded school board meeting. He wasn’t there to complain, but toﬁght for his son. He explained that he had spent months writing letters, speaking at meetings, and talking with teachers about reducing reliance on technology, but nothing changed.In my naivete, I thought the answer was data. If he shared the numbersfrom the last chapter with his son’s school, I was sure that would make all the di?erence. And so he did.  One month later he emailed me to say nobody cared: the evidence didn’t make an ounce of di?erence. In hindsight, I shouldn’t have been surprised.  In 1847, Hungarian physician Ignaz Semmelweis was alarmed by the number of new mothers dying from puerperal fever. ?e death toll was staggering, but no one could explain what was causing it.  To solve the mystery, Semmelweis collected data from two maternity wards—one sta?ed by male doctors, the other by female midwives. His ﬁndings were stark: mothers in the doctors’ ward were dying at nearly ﬁve times the rate of those in the midwives’ ward.What explained this gap?   After observing practices, Semmelweis identiﬁed one crucial di?erence: doctors often conducted autopsies in the morning before delivering babies—something midwives never did. He hypothesized that something invisible was being transferred from the cadavers to the mothers, and instructed doctors to start washing their hands with chlorine before deliveries.   ?e results were immediate. Maternal deaths fell from 18% in April to just 2% by August.  You’d think this breakthrough would be celebrated and immediately acted upon. It wasn’t. Semmelweis was ridiculed, rejected, and ultimately lost his job. ?e man whose idea could have saved thousands ended up dying alone and discredited in a Viennese asylum.  And yet today, the idea of doctors not washing their hands before surgery is unthinkable. So what changed? Mechanism.  In 1861, French chemist Louis Pasteur proposed germ theory: the idea that disease is caused by microscopic organisms passed from person to person. Suddenly, Semmelweis’s observations made sense: germs explainedhow illness could spread from corpses to mothers. With the mechanism now understood, handwashing quickly became standard practice worldwide. Time and again, history shows that data alone rarely changes minds. It’s only once we understand how a process works that meaningful actionfollows.   Builders exposed to asbestos long showed higher cancer rates than those working with other materials—but this data was ignored until scientists proved that microscopic ﬁbers could lodge in the lungs triggering tumor growth. High-contact athletes long showed greater cognitive decline than their non-contact peers—but this data was dismissed until researchers linked repeated head trauma to the buildup of tau protein in the brain.?e same pattern is now playing out with EdTech.  We have more than enough data showing that digital tools harm learning. But without a clear why, the numbers are easy to dismiss. However, once we understand driving mechanisms—how screens disrupt learning —the debate changes.   We’ve already touched on a few narrow mechanisms, but now it’s time to confront the big ones: three core principles from cognitive science that are hardwired into our very biology. Once you see them clearly, the ﬂaws in digital tools will become impossible to ignore.Mechanism #1: Attention?e Set-Up?e world is a chaotic place.  As I write this, I’m sitting in a busy airport. Hundreds of people are streaming past, a garbled voice is blaring gate changes over a loudspeaker,and two kids are getting scolded for sending their bags on a solo journey down the automated walkway.And yet, I’m able to tune it all out and focus on typing these words.?at’s attention at work.   ?e simplest way to think about attention is as a ﬁlter. Just like old 3-D glasses ﬁltered out certain wavelengths of light, attention ﬁlters out cognitive noise—allowing only relevant information to reach conscious awareness.So what makes something “relevant”?  Every task we undertake—writing an e-mail, driving to work, paying bills—relies on a set of internal rules that deﬁne success. For example, to read this paragraph, your brain is following a clear “reading ruleset”: tracking lines, holding words in memory, ﬂipping between pages.  Within the brain, these rulesets must be loaded into a small area called the lateral prefrontal cortex (LatPFC)—the command center for goal-directed behavior. Whichever ruleset the LatPFC holds determines what your attention ﬁlter blocks out, and what it lets through.   Unfortunately, the LatPFC has a hard limit: it can only hold one ruleset at a time.   ?is means any time we try to perform two conscious tasks at once—say, ﬁlling out a tax form while replying to a text—the brain can’t handle both simultaneously. ?e best it can do is rapidly switch back-and-forth between them, swapping out the ruleset in the LatPFC each time.   In other words, humans cannot multitask. We literally don’t have a brain capable of it—and the cost of trying is steep. When we force the brain to switch between tasks, three things happen:1) Time LossTask switching isn’t instantaneous. Each time the LatPFC resets, there’sa brief moment when attention shuts down—like a mental “blink.” Although each blink lasts only about 0.15 seconds, this cognitive dead-time compounds quickly if you’re switching dozens or hundreds of times.2) Lower Accuracy   Task switching isn’t clean. Each time the brain swaps tasks, there’s a brief period when the two competing rulesets bleed together making it much more likely you’ll commit an error. If you’ve ever tried to write an e-mail while having a conversation and accidentally typed out a word you meant to speak aloud, then you’ve experienced this interference e?ect.3) Memory  When we’re focused, the brain uses the hippocampus to build strong, lasting memories. But when we regularly shift tasks, it leans instead on the striatum: a brain region specialized for automatic routines rather than deep learning. As a result, what we “learn” while multitasking is often stored as a habit, making it harder to recall and apply later.Make no mistake: multitasking is one of the worst things we can do forlearning. It slows us down, screws us up, and kills our memory.But what does any of this have to do with EdTech??e Twist?e primary function of a tool is deﬁned by how it’s most often used.  Consider the hammer. ?ere are myriad uses for a hammer: doorstop, bottle opener, backscratcher, screwdriver, paperweight. But most people, most of the time, use this tool for one thing: hitting. As such, if I handed you a hammer right now, your ﬁrst instinct would probably be to look for something to hit.Now consider the computer. When a student sits in front of a screen,what’s the behavior that instinctively comes to mind?  Surveys tracking the digital habits of kids ages 8 to 18 give us a clear answer. Here’s how much time they spend each week on various screen-based activities:• 30 hours 37 minutes playing videos / music / video games• 15 hours 8 minutes scrolling the internet / social media / chats• 12 hours 30 minutes doing schoolwork / homework• 2 hours 44 minutes reading / writing for pleasure   Given that schools only run an average of 36 weeks per year, this means our children spend nearly 450 hours annually using digital tools for learning—and over 2,500 hours using them to jump between rapid-ﬁre media content.So, what’s the primary function most children associate with devices? Multitasking.  And what did we just establish is one of the worst things we can do for learning??e ConclusionLast year, after listening to my nieces grumble about their mountain of homework, I sat down at the dinner table to watch them tackle it on their laptops.  Not ﬁve minutes went by before they started checking Instagram, scrolling YouTube, and messaging their friends. By my estimate, they spent over an hour on homework that should have taken ﬁfteen minutes, tops. ?e rest was wasted time. Worse, when I asked what they’d actually learned, neither could tell me anything meaningful. It was as if the screen had eaten their memory.As you can guess, my nieces aren’t unique.Research suggests that when studying on a screen, the average studentlasts less than six minutes before drifting into unrelated digital distractions. When using a laptop in class, students spend 24 to 38 minutes of every hour o?-task. And even in tightly controlled laboratory experiments, where students are literally paid to stay focused, nearly 40% still can’t resist the urge to multitask.?is isn’t a matter of weak willpower or broken brains—it’s a matter ofpractice. Our kids spend thousands of hours training themselves to use digital devices in ways that undermine learning. If it takes years of practice to master any skill, then by the end of elementary school, many kids have already mastered one thing: screen-based multitasking.   And that’s the central irony of EdTech—it’s trying to serve two masters. On the one hand, developers say they want to support learning. On the other, they need to support proﬁts by capturing attention and maximizing screen time. Unfortunately, these two goals are fundamentally incompatible. To hold attention, digital tools must move fast: ﬂashing visuals, short videos, inﬁnite scrolling, constant rewards. But real learning requires the opposite: stillness, stability, and sustained thought. ?e very features that keep students glued to their screens are the same ones that make deepthinking impossible.   Whenever a tool tries to serve two masters, one will always win out. And in the case of EdTech, the winner is clear: money trumps memory. ?at’s why the EdTech industry generates billions in proﬁts while student outcomes continue to decline.   And sadly, there’s no going back. Had digital devices stayed conﬁned to classrooms, we might have been able to mitigate some of their harm. But once they entered the home, the bedroom, and the pocket, the war was over.   ?e habits are built, and the muscle memory is set. In the battle between education and entertainment, entertainment won.Mechanism #2: Empathy?e Set-UpIn 1966, MIT scientist Joseph Weizenbaum built ELIZA: the world’s ﬁrst digital therapist. Users would type in a prompt (“I’m feeling sad”) and the computer would respond with an open-ended question (“Why are you feeling that?”). Weizenbaum, who viewed ELIZA as a simple coding exercise, was shocked when people began taking his program seriously—with some even predicting that computers would soon replace humans in the therapeutic relationship.More than ﬁve decades later, ﬂesh-and-blood therapists continue tothrive. In fact, studies show that their impact on psychological symptoms is three to ﬁve times greater than that of even the most advanced digital programs.So, why have digital tools performed so poorly in the ﬁeld of mentalhealth?   Because the operative word in therapeutic relationship isn’t therapeutic. It’s relationship. Decades of research consistently show that the quality of the patient-therapist relationship is the single strongest predictor of success. Some estimates even suggest that up to 80% of therapeutic outcomes depend on a strong empathetic bond.?e same holds true in education.  A positive student-teacher relationship is one of the most consistent predictors of learning, with an e?ect size of +0.57— well above the +0.40 benchmark for meaningful impact.?e power of strong relationships in the classroom is two-fold.  First, they support understanding. When we care about someone, it becomes measurably easier to comprehend their thoughts, intentions, and explanations. In other words, it becomes easier to learn from them.   Second, they fuel motivation. Learning is hard, and when frustration sets in, it’s often the relationship (rather than the pedagogy) that keeps us going. We push through challenges because we trust the person guiding us.  So yes, relationships matter. But what aspect of the student-teacher relationship matters most?   Just like in therapy, the critical ingredient appears to be a?ective empathy: the ability to genuinely feel what another person is feeling. In teaching, its e?ect size is a remarkable +0.68. ?at’s higher than genuineness (+0.28), adaptability (+0.41), and even encouragement (+0.48).   During the 2020 pandemic, a friend of mine described how her son had completely checked out of remote learning. Even though the teacher was technically “there” for a few hours each day, the endless diet of digital worksheets, pre-recorded videos, and adaptive tutors eventually took its toll.And he wasn’t alone.  According to UNESCO, an estimated 24 million children around the world dropped out of school entirely during the COVID shutdowns. And among those who remained, engagement plummeted, with some surveys ﬁnding that nearly 80% of students felt signiﬁcantly less motivated.  Many factors contributed to this collapse, but much of it boils down to the loss of human connection. As my friend’s son put it in a message to me, “I’ll wear a mask, get vaccinated, do whatever it takes to go back to school and see my friends. I never thought I’d say this, but I actually miss my teachers. Sitting at home sucks, learning games suck, and online school sucks.”?e lesson is hard to miss: without empathy, learning falters.?e Twist?e most interesting thing about empathy is this: you don’t have it. In fact, no one does.  Despite what many people believe, empathy is not an emotion. Emotions are individual: we feel them within ourselves. Empathy, on the other hand, is relational: it exists only between people.  Consider magnets. A magnetic ﬁeld doesn’t exist in the north or south pole alone; it only emerges when both poles come together. Empathy works the same way. It’s not something one person possesses—it’s something that arises only through biological interaction.   ?is interaction is technically called physiological synchrony: a coordination—or matching—of biological signals between individuals. When two people empathize, their brain activity, heart rates, breathing patterns, skin conductance, and even eye blinks begin to align.   ?ink back to your favorite teachers. Odds are they weren’t the funniest or the most organized. ?ey were the ones you resonated with. ?at“resonance” isn’t some abstract, mystical feeling; it’s a measurable biological rhythm in your head, your heart, your muscles, your skin.  With this in mind, the two key beneﬁts of strong student-teacher relationships make even more sense.1) Understanding  When we say empathy supports understanding, we don’t mean surface-level comprehension; we mean deep, intuitive grasp. When a student and a teacher are in synchrony, the student doesn’t just learn from the teacher—they begin to think with them. ?ey follow reasoning, anticipate ideas, and respond to feedback because their brain and body are physically mirroring the teacher’s.2) Motivation  When we say empathy fuels motivation, it’s not just because the teacher notices when a student is struggling; it’s because they feel it too. ?at shared sensation allows the teacher to meet the student exactly where they are in order to guide them forward. Empathy doesn’t just sit with frustration—it helps reveal the path through it.Across classrooms, the data tell a remarkably consistent story:physiological synchrony predicts everything from learning outcomes and engagement levels to feedback comprehension and group-task performance.So, what does this have to do with EdTech??e ConclusionWhat’s the one essential ingredient for a?ective empathy?Biology.And what’s the one thing digital technologies fundamentally lack? Biology.?is means forming an empathetic relationship with a computer isn’tmerely di?cult—it’s impossible.   Sure, students can laugh with computers, sympathize with them, maybe even feel a crude form of a?ection. But these are internal emotions that begin and end within one body. ?ey don’t require anyone else to be present.   Empathy is di?erent. It requires two biological systems, locked in mutual awareness, exchanging physiological signals in real time.   ?is helps explain why the dropout rate for online learning hovers around 85%; why virtual schools typically underperform even the most underfunded brick-and-mortar ones; and why so many students tuned out during COVID-era remote learning. Without empathy, learning becomes harder. And without human connection to carry us through struggle, many simply give up.Some students have started reporting “connections” with AI-basedtutors. ?ey say the tool understands them, motivates them, and even cares about them.   ?is was exactly what Joseph Weizenbaum feared. When users began claiming that his ELIZA program “understood” them, he knew better. ?e program wasn’t empathizing, it was just pattern matching. It had no awareness, no comprehension, and no self. He spent the rest of his life warning us not to confuse intrapersonal emotion (what I feel alone) with interpersonal empathy (what we feel together). To blur that line is to diminish both.And that’s the real danger.  Just because a student feels a connection with AI doesn’t mean they’re experiencing biological synchrony. And without synchrony, there’s no empathetic beneﬁt. It’s like staring at a photo of the sun and expecting to get tan. Sure, the subjective feeling of warmth might be real—but the fundamental mechanism simply isn’t there.   To be clear: empathy isn’t required for learning. Students can absolutely study, solve problems, and build skills without it. But empathy is like Miracle-Gro: it makes everything faster, stronger, and more resilient. It turns instruction into connection, and that’s something no machine can replicate.?at’s why teachers still matter—not just for what they know, but forwho they are. Because in the end, learning isn’t just cognitive; it’s deeply human.Mechanism #3: Transfer?e Set-UpTransfer is the ability to take skills learned in one context and apply them in another, oftentimes unfamiliar context—and a classic 1975 study illustrates just how ﬁckle this process can be.   Researchers asked a group of deep-sea divers to memorize a list of words while submerged 20 feet underwater. ?e next day, half returned to the same depth, while the other half stayed on dry land. Each group was then asked to recall as many words as possible from the day before.   ?e result? ?ose divers who recalled their words underwater remembered 35% more than those on land.   ?is shows that where we learn something ultimately becomes part of what we learn—even when we’re not consciously paying attention to our surrounding environment.
