Psychologists call this context-dependent learning, and it’s why certain smells trigger vivid recollections, and why visiting your childhood neighborhood can rekindle long-forgotten moments.If every memory stayed chained to its original context, we’d be stuck—forced to re-learn basic ideas every time our surroundings changed. But that’s clearly not the case. ?ere must be a way for learning to break free from context and travel with us to new situations.?e secret is variety.   When we practice only in a single setting, memories do become tethered to that environment. But when we learn across di?erent contexts, our memories become more ﬂexible and easier to access anywhere.   I once taught a student who studied religiously. Every night, same time, same desk—forty minutes without fail. Her notes were immaculate and her textbook well-worn. But on exam day, sitting under harsh ﬂuorescent lights in a crowded lecture hall, she froze.   She thought it was test anxiety, but later realized the truth: her brain had been trained to recall information only at her study desk. ?e knowledge was there—it just wouldn’t surface in a new environment.   By contrast, students who rotate study locations—kitchen table, school bus, library—train their brains to retrieve knowledge anywhere. ?eirabilities become more “portable.” As my former student later reﬂected, “I still study at my desk, but I take practice quizzes wherever I can. And I’m deﬁnitely seeing a di?erence on exams.”  Variety is the ﬁrst step toward transfer—but not the last. Even ﬂexible knowledge doesn’t transfer automatically. ?ere’s still another hurdle to clear.?e TwistTransfer comes in two distinct forms: subtractive and additive.  Every new environment demands that we perform skills a little di?erently. ?ink of cooking in a fully equipped ﬁve-star kitchen versus a studio apartment with a single hot plate. ?e core skill of “cooking” stays the same, but the tools, timing, and constraints vary dramatically. Each context calls for unique adaptations.Sometimes, adapting means doing less—removing steps from a processyou already know. ?is is subtractive transfer, and it’s relatively easy. If you learned to drive a car using a manual transmission, then moving to an automatic is simple: you’re just doing less.  Other times, adapting means doing more—introducing new steps to a process you already know. ?is is additive transfer, and it’s much harder. If you learned to drive on an automatic, then switching to a manual means building entirely new skills: clutch control, gear shifting, and timing coordination. You’re not merely tweaking what you know; you’re constructing something brand new.?e ConclusionDigital technologies trap learning in a uniquely narrow context.In the real world, students encounter and encode ideas across a variety ofsettings. A child might learn to count using beads in the classroom, their ﬁngers at the dinner table, toys at home, and any number of everyday cues.?at variety doesn’t just reinforce memory, it builds ﬂexible understanding.  But digital tools tend to tie experiences to a single, uniform environment.  When students learn only through screens, their knowledge and skills become harder to access and apply elsewhere. Much of the well-documented COVID learning loss may not reﬂect what students didn’t learn, but what they couldn’t transfer. ?ere’s every reason to believe that students acquired at least some knowledge during remote instruction, but simply couldn’t retrieve it once they returned to a live classroom.But of course, the context problem cuts both ways.  If students trained in digital environments struggle to transfer their learning to real-world settings, then students trained in real-world settings should likewise struggle to transfer their learning to digital environments, right?Which brings us to the second dimension of transfer.  One of the main selling points of digital technology is that it makes tasks easier. Typing is less strenuous than handwriting; following GPS commands is less demanding than reading a map; spellcheck requires less e?ort than editing your own prose. In the professional world, this e?ciency is an undeniable boon. But in the classroom, it is technology’s Achilles heel.?e very fact that digital tools reduce e?ort means the skills theypromote tend to be shallow. Accordingly, when students try to move these skills into more complex, real-world settings, they face the challenge of additive transfer: a hurdle most are unprepared to clear.  Consider typing. Because it bypasses the development of ﬁne motor control, students who try to shift from typing to handwriting often ﬁndthemselves starting from scratch. ?ey’re forced to build entirely new capacities that the keyboard never trained.Fortunately, the additive-transfer problem doesn’t cut both ways.   Skills developed o?ine are typically more robust, varied, and embodied. As a result, moving them into a digital environment usually requires only subtractive transfer—and simplifying a complex, well-formed skill to ﬁt a streamlined tool is relatively trivial. ?is is why students who learn to handwrite ﬁrst often become proﬁcient typists quite quickly: their ﬁne motor skills transfer easily to the keyboard.Put simply, students who learn online often become stuck; unable toexport their skills into the real world. Meanwhile, students who learn o?ine often become adaptable; able to transfer their skills quickly into digital environments.  To be fair, sometimes transfer doesn’t matter. If a skill only needs to be performed on a computer (like entering formulas into Excel), then by all means, students should learn and use it there. But transfer becomes critical when we’re dealing with the kinds of knowledge and skills meant to shape how we think, act, and engage with the world: writing, reading, mathematics, scientiﬁc reasoning, historical understanding, civic literacy, emotional comprehension. ?ese skills aren’t meant to live on a screen—they’re meant to move and evolve with us throughout life.Computers tie us down; real life frees us up. And in the end, isn’teducation meant to o?er freedom over dependency?So Now Then . . .EdTech isn’t failing because of outdated software or poor teacher training. It’s failing because it’s fundamentally incompatible with how human beings actually learn.  Learning is deliberate and e?ortful. It requires sustained focus, thrives on emotional connection, and succeeds when skills can transfer ﬂexibly between contexts. Yet by design, EdTech undermines these biological realities: it fragments attention, severs emotional synchrony, and conﬁnes learning to a context so narrow that knowledge struggles to escape the screen. And as this chapter’s title suggests, these problems are intractable:• Unless tech stops being used primarily for entertainment, multitaskingwill always remain a barrier.• Unless tech incorporates biological components, genuine empathy will always remain impossible.• Unless tech is deliberately made harder to use, transfer will alwaysremain elusive.   In short, unless EdTech companies are willing to radically rethink their programs, platforms, and business models, digital devices will never be engines of deep learning.   ?e cost of ignoring these truths isn’t just lower test scores—it’s the slow erosion of qualities every parent hopes to see in their child: the ability to think deeply, to connect meaningfully with others, and to shape the larger world. When we tether learning to screens, we risk raising a generation literate in apps, but illiterate in life.Still, EdTech vendors will keep defending their tools—but only bydistorting reality. Luckily, their script rarely changes. Once you see how advocates twist terms and warp concepts, their arguments fall apart.   In the next chapter, let’s unpack their arguments—one by one—and see exactly how to push back.OceanofPDF.com        | 4 | Addressing the ApologistsHow to Respond to Common Arguments from EdTech EvangelistsI’ve raised concerns about laptops with my son’s school, but they always respond the same way: “Students need to learn how to live with devices.” It feels like they’re dodging the issue. If I told them secondhand smoke was harming kids and they responded, “Well, cigarettes are a part of life, so students need to learn how to live with them,” people would be outraged. How is this any di?erent? I feel like I’m going insane.–ANONYMOUS Parent of 16-Year-Old (United States)     any schools go to great lengths to preserve respectability by denying the link between digital tools and declining memory, attention, and performance. But education isn’t unique in this kind of denial—industry hasbeen perfecting the art of deﬂection for decades.  In 1970, the Ford Motor Company released North America’s ﬁrst subcompact car: the Pinto. It was a?ordable, fuel e?cient, and wildlypopular. But the car su?ered from one tiny problem—the fuel tank was mounted directly behind the rear axle, meaning that even a minor rear-end collision could cause the car to burst into ﬂames.   Internal memos revealed that engineers knew about this ﬂaw, and they’d even calculated the cost of ﬁxing it to be only $11 per car. But when public backlash erupted, Ford didn’t ﬁx the problem—instead, they ﬁxed the narrative.1) “Everyone’s Doing It”  Ford’s legal team pointed out that all cars have fuel tanks, meaning any car could conceivably explode under the right conditions. ?e Pinto, they argued, was just one more potential ﬁreball on the road.2) “Hate the Game, not the Player”  Since the Pinto was technically compliant with all federal safety standards, Ford argued it bore no moral responsibility. Any blame should be placed on the government for setting inadequate standards.3) “We Know You Better ?an You Know Yourself ”  Sure, customers claimed they wanted safer cars, but Ford insisted what they really wanted was cheap speed. Altering the Pinto would make it slower, heavier, and less fuel-e?cient—and nobody truly desired that.  At no point did Ford seriously question whether their car should have been on the road in the ﬁrst place, let alone whether they bore responsibility for any harm it caused.EdTech has long been playing out its own version of the Pinto story.  Just like Ford, tech evangelists rarely deny the underlying harms of digital tools. Instead, they shift the narrative and go on the o?ensive—redirecting the conversation, dodging responsibility, and serving up a set of reasonable-sounding apologies.?e good news? ?eir excuses follow a predictable script. In fact, mostfall neatly into just eight categories—and once you know how to spot them, they’re surprisingly easy to dismantle.Apolog y #1: EdTech Has so Much Potential (or “Tech Is the Future…”)“Computer assisted learning shows considerable promise…Against this backdrop, promising uses of education technology have the potential to support massive inroads in learning.”–Poverty Research Center, Technology Evidence ReviewIt’s easy to get swept up in the possibilities of digital devices in education. Case in point: the above quote comes from a research team at MIT. After reviewing 126 studies and ﬁnding that EdTech tends to lower academic achievement, they still concluded that these tools hold great potential.  Potential refers to what something could be, or should be, or might someday become—not what it actually is. ?is means arguments from potential aren’t grounded in evidence; they’re grounded in faith and desire. To say that computers have the potential to improve learning is, in fact, to tacitly admit they aren’t doing so now.?ere are few serious domains where potential alone is an acceptablebasis for policy. Consider medicine. Many researchers believe genetic manipulation will revolutionize treatment. But until they can clearly demonstrate when, how, and under what conditions those methods outperform existing ones, governments have rightly imposed strict legal limits on what doctors are allowed to do.I’m not arguing against ambition. It’s entirely possible someone will oneday create a digital product that works better than any traditional teaching method. But that day hasn’t yet arrived, and promissory arguments are no foundation upon which to reshape education. ?e promise may be real—but so is the burden to prove it.And that’s the key to countering this apology.  It’s not the responsibility of the world to disprove someone’s belief. It’s the responsibility of the believer to provide convincing evidence for their claims. Schools should be asked to adopt digital tools only after they’ve demonstrated clear and unequivocal beneﬁts to learning—not when a handful of advocates believe they someday might.Apolog y #2: We Need More Time (or, “You Can’t Rush Genius…”)“My daughter’s school admits that performance is going down, but they say it’s because teachers are still learning how to use the new computers. I guess my daughter’s education is just the price we pay for working out the kinks.”–Renee, Mother of 9-Year-Old (Australia)When challenged on the empty promise of potential, many EdTech proponents shift gears. ?ey admit digital tools haven’t worked yet, but insist we’re getting closer. “Just give us more time and more funding,” they say. “Progress takes patience.”?e biggest threat to this apology is history.   ?e ﬁrst major meta-analysis of computers in education was published in 1977. It reviewed over 150 studies, some dating back to 1962. ?e result? An e?ect size of 0.29.  And today—after six decades of research, investment, and iteration—what’s the modern e?ect size of computers in education? Still 0.29. As researcher Dylan Wiliam put it, “Technology is the revolution still coming after 50 years.”So how much more time are we expected to give?  Even if more data is needed, the responsibility for that research surely falls on developers. Teachers should be teaching, not trialing half-baked digital tools.  And yet, that’s exactly what many EdTech advocates continue to push for. For example, after analyzing data from millions of students across three PISA cycles and concluding that computer use is associated with declining academic performance, the OECD still managed to conclude, “It is vital that teachers become active agents for change, not just implementing technological innovations, but in designing them too.”  Why is it vital that teachers serve as product designers—and students, by extension, as beta-testers? Is this really necessary for student learning? Or is it simply necessary for tech companies to salvage a failing experiment?   ?is apology is no di?erent than Ford deciding it was cheaper to let drivers ﬁgure out for themselves how likely their car was to explode. ?atkind	of	research	should	be	done	in-house, completed	in-full, and independently veriﬁed before the public is asked to embrace a tool.   No child’s education should be put on hold so EdTech can have one more decade to get it right.Apolog y #3: Digital Devices Are U biquitous (or, “They Ain’t Goin’ Nowhere…”)“Last year, our district issued each student a laptop and mandated teachers use them in every class. Our school tried to resist, but this year they’ve set even higher usage targets and are monitoring activity from the back end. Some of my teachers could actually lose their job for not having kids use the computer enough.”–Anonymous, School Principal since 2013 (United States)Driving. Health insurance. Alcohol. Taxes. Video games. Dating,marriage, and divorce. Student loans. Pregnancy. Litigation. Television. Stock investments. Table manners. Job applications. Self-defense. First aid. Pet care. Yoga.   I’m listing these not to be exhaustive, but to make a point: ubiquity alone doesn’t justify inclusion in schools. Teachers have always understood they’re not the only educators in a child’s life. Many essential life-skills are best learned through family, peers, and real-world experience—not in the classroom.?at said, there is a reasonable argument to be made that educationshould address certain ubiquitous topics. ?e idea that schools bear responsibility for preparing students for the entirety of the world they’re entering certainly has merit.  But here’s the key distinction: arguing that a topic should be taught is very di?erent from arguing that everything should be taught through that topic. ?e ﬁrst is a question of curriculum (what we teach), while the second is a question of pedagogy (how we teach).   Consider driving. You might believe schools should teach students how to drive. Fair enough. But it doesn’t follow that schools should now teach every subject in a car. What we teach and how we teach are not the same.  And this is where the apology from ubiquity collapses. ?rough a kind of linguistic sleight of hand, the claim “Schools should teach computer skills” has morphed into “Schools should teach all skills through computers.”  Some tech enthusiasts strongly believe students need to learn coding, digital navigation, and online etiquette. But that doesn’t mean we need to reconﬁgure every class—from reading and writing to math and science—around a screen. When it comes to pedagogy, we should choose the tool best suited to the job; not the most ubiquitous one.Apolog y #4: Kids Need Digital Skills To Be Competitive (or, “How Will They Get a Job…”)“Everything is about ‘work-readiness’ and ‘21st century skills’. I know my kid—he’s not even ready for high school, let alone work. Yes, he can code. So what? AI can code.”–Anonymous, Parent of 14-year-old (United States)When confronted with the distinction between what we teach and how we teach, many EdTech proponents shift tack. ?ey’ll often concede that technology may not belong in every class, but insist we need more classes about technology. ?ey argue we must devote more curriculum time to explicitly teaching technology—otherwise, how will students ever hope to be employable in the future?I’d argue that maximizing employability shouldn’t be the ultimate goal ofeducation—but even if it is, chasing digital skills is exactly the wrong strategy.  Here’s a useful rule of thumb: teach someone how to use a tool, and they’ll be able to use that tool. Teach someone how to think, and they’ll be able to use any tool.   Ironically, the best preparation for a volatile, rapidly-changing workforce isn’t tool-speciﬁc training; it’s a broad, general education. Rather than trying to anticipate every future trend, schools serve students best by cultivating the cognitive skills and habits of mind that let them adapt to whatever the world throws their way.Consider welding. A student who spends two years in a weldingapprenticeship will likely become an excellent welder. But if that student later wants to become a plumber or electrician, they’ll probably struggle.?is is simply an extension of the transfer problem explored in the previous chapter: the more speciﬁc the skill, the fewer the options.  Luckily, a general education doesn’t lock kids into a single path. It attempts to open every path.   ?is is why K-12 schooling has traditionally emphasized thinking through knowledge rather than training on tools. Students don’t study history to become historians or write essays to become novelists. ?ese disciplines are vehicles for building literacy, reasoning, mental ﬂexibility, and those cognitive habits that support long-term agility.Still, every few decades, someone will insist that schools must teach thetools of the moment.  In the past, that meant carving out curriculum time to train on Morse code, shortwave radios, and slide rules—all once considered essential for economic survival.Today, that same urgency has shifted to coding, robotics, and virtualreality. Yet despite this growing urgency, something strange is happening: as education devotes more time to digital tools, students’ actual digital literacy is declining. International assessments now ﬁnd that today’s teenagers—raised on devices and surrounded by screens throughout the school day—perform worse on basic digital problem-solving tasks than students did a decade ago. Furthermore, employers increasingly report that young people are arriving less job-ready than previous generations. More technology has not produced more technological competence; in many cases, it has displaced the broader thinking skills that genuine digital literacy depends on.Put simply, the more schools push technology, the less digitallycompetent students are becoming. And this helps explain an odd irony many people recognize but few ever vocalize: today, the loudest voices calling for explicit teaching of these tools come from adults who were, themselves, never taught them in school.   How is this possible? How did the forty-somethings now giving keynote presentations about artiﬁcial intelligence ever manage to ﬁgure out how to use such recent innovations without formal instruction?  Because their general education taught them how to think, learn, and adapt.   When we allow short-term job demands to dictate curriculum, we erode the deeper mission of education. By all means, if a digital tool deepens thinking or enriches learning, we should use it in schools. But when the tool itself becomes the focus, our kids’ learning will expire the moment the market shifts. And with digital tools, that shelf life is often incredibly short.Apolog y #5: Modern Students Learn Differently (or, “Parents Just Don’t Understand…”)“I cannot count how many times I’ve been told my kids learn di?erently than we used to. As far as I can tell, that’s just an excuse for teachers to play YouTube clips in class and let kids get their information from Wikipedia. I reckon ‘di?erent’ is just code for lowering the bar.”–Alan, Father of 17-year-old and 15-year old (Australia)Tech proponents argue that because today’s students have been immersed in digital technology from birth, they must learn di?erently than previous generations. And if students learn di?erently, then schools must teach di?erently to be e?ective. ?is typically means replacing traditional methods with digital tools designed to match how kids now engage with, process,and understand the world. Only EdTech, we’re told, can reach these so-called digital natives.?ere’s just one problem: the idea of the digital native is a myth.  More than two decades of research show that young people don’t use technology more e?ectively, don’t understand it more deeply, and don’t possess any unique digital literacy compared to adults. As the UK’s Joint Information Systems Committee put it, “…the ubiquitous presence of technology in [millennials’] lives has not resulted in improved information retrieval, information seeking, or evaluation skills.”?e reality is far less exciting—but far more important. All human beingslearn in the same way.To clarify, consider this:• All human beings digest in the same way. What we eat will vary, and the accommodations we require (like a feeding tube) may di?er, but the digestive process itself is biologically ﬁxed.• All human beings breathe in the same way. What we inhale will vary, and the tools we use (like a vape) may di?er, but the respiratory process itself is biologically ﬁxed.• All human beings learn in the same way.  No app, platform, or social media trend has changed how the brain encodes, consolidates, or transforms knowledge. Some students may require speciﬁc supports (e.g. a blind student may need braille material), but those supports don’t alter the underlying biological process of learning.   ?at said, it’s true that today’s so-called ‘digital natives’ have di?erent preferences than previous generations. But as we saw in Chapter 1, preferences are irrelevant at best—and harmful at worst.   For example, many modern students prefer short-form video content like TikToks or Instagram Reels. To cater to this, some EdTech platformspromote ‘micro-learning’ modules and 60-second ‘learning sprints’. But learning doesn’t happen in sprints—it never has. While short bursts may feel engaging, research shows they lead to weaker retention, shallower reasoning, and impaired critical thinking.   So yes, preferences may have evolved. But human cognitive architecture has not.   To overhaul millennia-old educational practices on the false notion that a new tool has somehow restructured a physiological process is to abandon reason for hype. And perhaps that explains why so much of the data we’ve seen points to EdTech harming learning. Digital tools drag us away from what works, not toward some new version of human cognition.In a strange twist, this apology may end up undermining EdTech in thelong run. As noted earlier, studies consistently show that Gen-Z typically performs no better than previous generations on many key measures of digital literacy. Why? Because when we assume kids naturally ‘get’ technology, we stop teaching them how to use it. We simply hand them a device and expect ﬂuency to follow.?e myth of the digital native doesn’t justify new approaches to learning;it simply encourages us to abandon those that actually work.Apolog y #6: People Are Using EdTech Incorrectly (or, “It’s All Your Fault…”)“One interpretation of all this…is that we have not yet become good enough at the kind of pedagogies that make the most of technology.” –OECD: “Student, Computers, and Learning Report”It seems nobody is immune to this argument. After analyzing millions of data points and ﬁnding that digital tools harm learning, the OECD somehow still managed to conclude the problem isn’t technology—it’s us. If only parents, teachers, and students would use EdTech in the way developers intended, all would be well in schools again.But intentions are meaningless.  Philo T. Farnsworth invented the television to promote global understanding. Instead, it became a vehicle for mass advertising. RobertPropst designed the cubicle to boost workplace productivity. Instead, it became a cost-cutting cage for employees. Alfred Nobel created dynamite for industrial progress. Instead, it became a tool of military destruction.   ?ere’s no doubt EdTech developers have a clear vision for how their tools ‘should’ be used. But once a tool enters the marketplace, it’s the public—not the inventors—who ultimately determine its function. And when over 80% of student screen time goes to passive media consumption, we can be certain that this (not learning) is the primary function of digital devices.  Still, let’s give this apology the beneﬁt of the doubt. If computers truly are being used ‘incorrectly’ in schools, then what does ‘correct’ use look like? According to the OECD: “[EdTech] is linked to better student performance…when computer software and internet connections help to increase study time and practice.”In case you missed it, let me rephrase that: learning improves whenstudents spend more time learning.   ?is insight isn’t unique to computers. Flashcards, workbooks, whiteboards, an empty milk jug, a box of wet ferrets, my wry Uncle Muskie. Any tool that gets kids to spend more time engaged in learning will improve performance. ?e real question is, do computers actually do this?As we’ve seen, the answer is an unequivocal no.Apolog y #7: Don’t Let Your School Get Left Behind (or, “Ever yone Else is Doing It…”)“Our school just rolled out a major new tech platform that nobody asked for and nobody knows how to use. But we’re being told that this is the only way our school can ‘stay competitive’ and maintain enrollment.”–Anonymous, Teacher since 1999 (Australia)If blaming individuals doesn’t work, there’s always a broader scapegoat: the institution.   Around the world, school leaders worry that if they don’t adopt the latest tech, they’ll seem outdated, lose prestige, and fall behind in the rankings. Success becomes a matter of branding. It no longer matters whether digital tools improve learning; only that they look good in admissions brochures.Fear of missing out isn’t just for teenagers.   Needless to say, EdTech thrives in this high-pressure environment. One-click dashboards. AI tutors. Real-time analytics. “Adopt our platform and prove to the world that your school is future-ready.” What administrator wants to be the one to say no?But what’s the cost of always saying yes?  When time-starved teachers are presented with new platforms every year, burnout climbs. When easily distracted students are given new tools in every class, engagement drops. When busy parents have to learn a new reporting portal each semester, confusion grows.  And we’ve already seen the academic cost. Since 2000, global EdTech investment has exploded—even as student outcomes have steadily declined. Granted, correlation is not causation, but the pattern is hard to dismiss.  Schools were never meant to compete in a digital arms-race. ?ey were meant to be in the business of learning. When new tools consistently fail to outperform old ones, the boldest move isn’t to stay on trend—it’s to step back. Doing what’s right for students may not be ﬂashy, but results matter more than appearances.Apolog y #8: It’s Just a Tool (or, “Don’t Blame Us…”)“Whenever I raise concerns about the digital programs we’re required to use, my department head says, “It’s just a tool—you control how it’s used.’ But that’s not true. ?e program dictates the pacing, the sequence, the problems, even the feedback. If that’s ‘just’ a tool, then I guess teachers are ‘just’ babysitters.”–Helen, Teacher since 2018 (United States)If you hear this apology, take it as a win. In my experience, it only shows up after every other argument has failed. It’s not meant to persuade—it’s a last-ditch attempt to shut down debate and make critics seem unreasonable.   And it often works because, strictly speaking, it’s true: digital devices are just tools. ?ey have no agency, no initiative, and no power without human input.But that’s the trap. ?is apology, while strictly accurate, is deeplymisleading.   Tools may lack autonomy, but that doesn’t make them neutral. Every tool carries an implicit vision of how the world should work. By promoting certain behaviors, tools shape how we think, how we act, and how we understand the world. Abraham Maslow captured this when he said, “It is tempting, if the only tool you have is a hammer, to treat everything as if it were a nail.”Consider the mechanical clock. ?is tool doesn’t simply measure time—it deﬁnes it. As historian Lewis Mumford observed, by imposing a mechanistic rhythm on daily life, the clock enabled regular work hours, standardized production, and helped forge the rise of the modern industrial mindset. ?e clock didn’t reﬂect a worldview; it imposed one.   We’ll return to this idea in Chapter 7 when we explore AI. But for now, it’s enough to recognize that digital tools are not inert. EdTech doesn’t merely support pedagogy—it is pedagogy. Whether it’s an online forum promoting social learning, a gamiﬁed tutor pushing competition, or an augmented reality system casting learning as immersive, every platform encodes a worldview in its design. Each program makes assumptions about how learning should work, how schools should function, and what role education serves.?e joke is that when we use these tools, we inevitably adopt theirworldviews.  And the punchline? EdTech was never meant to quietly support traditional teaching. Its goal has always been to disrupt and reimagine schools to better ﬁt the logic of digital tools. Watch any TED Talk or product launch—EdTech’s revolutionary intent has been on proud display for over a decade.So when developers claim their products are ‘just tools’, they’re hoping todisarm you. If they can frame your critique as an irrational attack on an inanimate object, then they can quietly shift blame back onto students, teachers, and parents.   It’s true that even the best hammer can’t redeem a bad carpenter. But the inverse matters more: even the best carpenter can’t build with a warped hammer. Bad tools don’t just fail, they distort the very task itself. And when the task is educating children, any distortion is dangerous.EdTech is a bad tool.So Now Then…I recently spoke with a parent who lamented her daughter’s struggles in a new ‘tech-heavy’ school. ?e girl swore that none of her friends liked the screens; they all felt like they weren’t learning anything—just clickingbuttons and completing digital quizzes. When this parent asked about opting her daughter out of digital device use, she encountered a particularly pernicious form of resistance. ?e principal didn’t deny her concerns; instead, he claimed the school was already too far down the digital road. Reversing course, he said, would be ‘counterproductive’ to their district’s tech goals.?ere’s nothing more deﬂating than hearing, ‘It’s too late to turn backnow.’ Many schools have already poured immense time, e?ort, and money into EdTech. So even when presented with clear evidence that learning is declining, they choose to stay the course. Going back feels daunting, or even impossible.   ?is is the sunk cost fallacy, and it may be the ultimate tech apology. ?e belief that we should continue investing in something simply because we’ve already invested so much certainly sounds reasonable. But past investment in EdTech doesn’t justify present harm—especially when the cost is being paid by students who continue to fall behind.We can’t let the fear of admitting a mistake prevent us from doing what’sright. Yes, reversion may be awkward in the short term, but it will ultimately open the door to better outcomes.What’s done is done. But that doesn’t mean it must continue.   In just the past two years, schools around the world have begun banning smartphones. Not long ago, this seemed unthinkable: phones felt too embedded, and critics warned that students and teachers would rebel.   ?ey were wrong about phones—and they’re equally wrong about EdTech.  It’s time to lead the charge. ?e best time to push back was twenty years ago. ?e next best time is now.OceanofPDF.comPART T WOSpecial Cases“?e blade itself incites to deeds of violence.”–HOMER, ?e OdysseyOceanofPDF.com| 5 | SmartphonesA Special Kind of BadMy daughter is addicted to her phone. She’s smart, funny, and used to love reading, but now she can’t make it a page without checking her notiﬁcations. We’ve tried banning the phone at home, but her school allows them “for learning”—so the addiction is fed daily, and I’m powerless to stop it. Last month I pleaded for our state school board to ban phones, but they didn’t budge. I feel like I’m bailing water out of a sinking boat, doing everything I can at home only to have it undone the moment she walks into school.–RACHEL Mother of 14-year-old (United States)or more than a decade, I’ve asked educators and parents around the world one simple question: why would a kid ever need a cell phone inschool?I have yet to receive a convincing answer. “Can’t kids learn from them?”Even if we granted that digital tools help learning (a generousconcession, as we’ve seen), there’s still no reason that learning must happen on a phone. School-owned laptops can do everything a phone can, but with the added beneﬁts of internet restrictions, platform safeguards, and school-approved software.   Smartphones, by contrast, are owned and controlled by the student. ?is means schools can’t reliably monitor which apps are installed or what students are actually engaging with—at least, not without severely violating privacy laws. So why invite distraction and risk when safer, better-suited tools already exist?“What if I need to get in touch with my child?”   Before the smartphone boom of the 2010s, parents weren’t cut o? from their children the moment they walked into school. ?ink back to your own childhood: if your family needed to reach you, they simply called the school. A sta? member—trained, trusted, and following established protocol—would ﬁnd you and escort you to the main o?ce.?at system still exists. Every school still has a phone line, a front desk,and trained personnel. Why have we convinced ourselves that bypassing a system built to maximize safety and minimize disruption is somehow a sign of progress?“What if there’s a shooting or other emergency?”   ?is is an understandable fear, but it doesn’t hold up to scrutiny. First responders routinely report that the proliferation of smartphones has made large-scale emergencies harder to manage, not easier.   During the 2018 shooting at Marjory Stoneman Douglas High School in Florida, student phone use clogged communication networks, hampering coordination among o?cials. False reports of multiple shooters spread rapidly on social media, sowing confusion among students and police alike.And hundreds of panicked parents, alerted in real-time by their children, rushed to the campus, unintentionally blocking access for EMTs.  In crises, the safest response is a small, well-trained team of adults following a clear plan—not hundreds of teenagers simultaneously broadcasting conﬂicting reports. Controlled, coordinated communication saves lives. Chaos does not.“How else will children learn to use a cell phone appropriately?”  When asked what constitutes ‘appropriate’ smartphone use in schools, most teachers give the same answer: “Students should be able to refrain from looking at or using their phone during class.”?at’s a reasonable standard, but one even adults routinely fail to meet.?e average American adult checks their phone 205 times a day—about once every four and a half minutes. In fact, 43% openly admit to being addicted to their phones. If grown-ups with fully developed brains and decades of life experience can’t responsibly manage this tool, why should we expect children and teenagers to do any better?And even if we wanted to teach ‘appropriate’ use, I’ve seen no publishedevidence that explicit smartphone-etiquette lessons produce lasting behavioral change. If neither adult modelling nor direct instruction work, then by what mechanism do we expect students to develop good academic phone habits? Osmosis?“What about medical uses?”   ?is is the one legitimate exception: students with serious medical conditions sometimes use smartphones to monitor blood sugar, control medical implants, or share health data with caregivers. But even here, two issues remain.  First, smartphones are notoriously unreliable. ?ey crash, update unexpectedly, and frequently get bogged down by background apps. Wouldyou really entrust a child’s medical safety to the battery life of a device they spent all morning gaming on? ?at’s why many doctors require dedicated, single-purpose medical devices: they’re more stable, more accurate, and less vulnerable to interference than general-use phones.   Second, the number of students with medical conditions requiring real-time monitoring is incredibly small—well under 1% globally. In those rare cases, medically mediated phone access can be handled individually. ?is is no justiﬁcation for giving every student unrestricted access throughout the school day.The DataDespite the lack of convincing arguments, most schools still default to allowing smartphones. Rather than demanding proof that these devices improve learning before allowing them, they ﬂip the burden of proof: “Show us the harm, and we might consider restricting them.”Fortunately, the harm is easy to ﬁnd.Mental HealthMost parents have seen it: the glazed-over stare while scrolling, followed by the meltdown when the screen is taken away. ?e relationship between children and their phones is often dysfunctional, and research is now conﬁrming what families have long suspected.  Over the past decade, study after study has shown that the more time children spend on smartphones (especially social media), the more likely they are to experience anxiety, depression, and emotional volatility. In schools, heavy phone use has been linked to increased stress, lower happiness, and more disruptive behavior both inside and outside the classroom.Put bluntly: smartphones harm mental health.?ere are several reasons for this, but one in particular stands out.   When humans interact face-to-face—laughing, sharing, listening—the brain releases a chemical called oxytocin. Often called the ‘bonding hormone’, oxytocin plays a central role in human connection. But when communication happens via text, the brain is more likely to release a peptide called tachykinin 2 (TAC2). TAC2, a biological marker of social isolation, activates brain regions involved in threat response.In plain terms: the brain doesn’t recognize text-based communication asgenuine social interaction. And when digital ‘connection’ fails to meet our physiological need for bonding, the brain is often driven to a state of hyper-vigilance (anxiety) and defensive aggression (problematic behaviors).   It’s common for children to brag about (or even measure their self-worth by) the number of connections they have on social media. A father from Brisbane once told me that the only time his phone-obsessed daughter seems happy at home is when she gains new followers. “Most of the time she’s staring at her phone with a furrowed brow,” he said, “but every so often she’ll look up, smile, and proudly announce she got a new subscriber. I know I shouldn’t celebrate this, but it’s the only time I feel like we connect—so I do.”Unfortunately, due to the oxytocin/TAC2 dichotomy, digital ‘followers’aren’t the same as ﬂesh-and-blood friends—and the more children build their social lives around the former, the more likely they are to slip into despair. Studies consistently show that as the number of online connections increases, so too do symptoms of depression. As the Brisbane father put it, “She’s constantly messaging her friends, but it doesn’t seem to make her any happier. I always o?er to drive her over to someone’s house, but she’d rather just keep typing.”He comforted himself with the thought of school: “At least she still getsto run around with her friends at recess and talk with them at lunch.” Unfortunately, that turns out not to be the case.  In-person social interaction is becoming increasingly rare in schools. When students have access to their phones, many choose to scroll or surf rather than engage with the people around them. ?is behavior is so widespread that it now has a name: phubbing—snubbing others in favor of a phone. In one large-scale study, 43% of children admitted to phubbing speciﬁcally to avoid in-person interaction at school.So what happens when phones are removed from school? Exactly whatyou’d hope.  Students report feeling more connected, their wellbeing improves, problematic behaviors decline, stress levels drop, and overall satisfaction with school goes up. Furthermore, breaktime habits shift dramatically. Instead of sitting alone with screens, students walk with friends, read physical books, and play active games with real people. ?ese moments of genuine interaction reignite a deeper sense of human connection; the kind that leads to genuinely happy kids. And isn’t that what every parent wants?Physical HealthTime spent on a device is, by deﬁnition, not time spent running, jumping, or playing. In schools, the shift from playgrounds to phones has been linked to increased fat mass, decreased muscle mass, and a rise in potentially serious health conditions like early-onset diabetes and elevated cholesterol.  One popular response is to make phones more ’active’. Apps like Pokémon GO and other augmented reality games aim to get kids moving without ever needing to look up from the screen. But this is a textbook case of mistaking the poison for the cure. Yes, you could use screens to combat the damage caused by screens…or you could simply remove the screen.  Another response is to point out that many academic activities are also sedentary. Reading, writing, and arithmetic aren’t physically demanding, so why treat smartphone use any di?erently?Because not all sitting is created equal.  Clinicians often distinguish between negative (cognitively passive) and positive (cognitively active) sedentary behaviors. Both can a?ect physical health, but only the latter has been linked to improved cognitive and emotional development.  So yes, studying may be sedentary—but it strengthens focus, builds motivation, and boosts learning in ways that scrolling never will.Another consideration is sleep.  High-quality sleep is essential for maintaining physical health. Unfortunately, screens suppress melatonin (a key sleep hormone) and stimulate the brain in ways that reduce not only sleep duration but also sleep quality. ?is compounds the problem: screens promote inactivity while also disrupting the recovery process the body needs to o?set it. By contrast, positive sedentary behaviors (like reading before bed) tend to promote beneﬁcial, restorative sleep.Fortunately, when phones are removed from schools, students engage infar more physical activity during breaks—especially when screens are replaced with equipment like balls and jump ropes. Although no long-term study has yet tracked the downstream health e?ects of these shifts, the logic is sound: more movement and less harmful sedentary behaviour is unlikely to be anything but a gain for student wellbeing.LearningAs smartphone use rises, academic performance declines on both daily assignments and high stakes exams. In fact, a recent meta-analysis estimatedthe e?ect size of phone use at -0.33. ?at’s a seriously negative impact, on par with depression (-0.30), bullying (-0.28), and even childhood traumatic brain injury (-0.40).   ?e clearest culprit is distraction. As discussed in Chapter 3, multitasking is neurologically impossible and one of the worst practices for learning and memory formation. Yet smartphones are designed to lure students into a fractured state of attention. Even children who aren’t actively using their phones but know their device is nearby (such as in a backpack or locker) show divided focus and diminished performance.As one teacher from Melbourne told me, “I used to arrive at school tothe sounds of dozens of kids screaming and playing, then spend my day learning with my students. Now I hear nothing but typing and video game noises, and spend my day trying to compete with screens. Honestly, probably half of every class is wasted telling kids to put away their phones and getting everyone back on task.”Less than a year later, she left the profession. “I wanted to be a teacher,”she said, “not the phone police.” Her experience is far from unique—but there is another way.  When schools remove phones entirely, learning improves. Large-scale studies from the UK, Spain, Norway, and Sweden have found that going phone-free produces academic gains equivalent to as much as eleven additional hours of instruction per week. Phone bans also improve teacher satisfaction and retention by reducing discipline problems and returning valuable class time to instruction.When confronted with this evidence, many tech advocates call for‘caution’—arguing that the research is too inconsistent to support ﬁrm conclusions. But that so-called ‘inconsistency’ only ever moves in one direction: banning phones either improves learning or has no e?ect. I haveyet to see a single large-scale study or meta-analysis showing that removing smartphones harms academic outcomes.  Refusing to act when the worst-case outcome is nothing and the best-case is signiﬁcantly better learning isn’t caution; it’s a stalling tactic. Can our children a?ord the delay?To Ban, or Not To BanDespite overwhelming evidence of mental, physical, and cognitive harm, many schools remain reluctant to impose a full bell-to-bell ban on smartphones. ?is hesitation is often framed as a matter of principle: banning is seen as authoritarian, and shielding kids from the ‘real world’ is viewed as counterproductive.But this ignores a basic truth: school is, by design, one giant ban. It existsprecisely because children aren’t yet mentally or emotionally equipped to navigate the ‘real world’ on their own. Schools have always served to ﬁlter reality—exposing students to carefully selected experiences in a slow, controlled, and developmentally appropriate way.  Some bans are obvious: drugs, weapons, sex, ﬁghting, inﬂammatory language. Others are more subtle; things that may be perfectly accessible outside of school, but are restricted inside because they disrupt learning or exceed a child’s readiness.   ?ese include certain topics (astrology, occultism), tools (drones, ﬁdget spinners), clothing (mini-skirts, ripped tees), movements (hall passes, exit restrictions), privileges (permission slips, equipment use), behaviors (outsourcing homework, selling goods), materials (modifying school software, accessing chemicals), forms of expression (protests, subversive political clubs), and personal choices (self-medicating, posting ﬂyers).Bans aren’t unusual—they’re foundational. ?ey exist to ensure growth,not to hinder it.  Still, when it comes to smartphones, many schools avoid hard bans in favor of soft policies: “We don’t want to see your phone during class, but you can use it during lunch, recess, or between periods.” ?e idea is to balance student freedom with classroom control.  Here’s the problem: we’ve known for nearly a decade that smartphone mitigation policies don’t work. Only strict, full-day, bell-to-bell bans produce consistent behavioral and academic improvements.  So why do mitigation policies fail? Why does allowing even a few minutes of phone use between classes still harm learning??ere are three key mechanisms.Mechanism #1: CravingA family friend recently installed a usage-tracking app on her teenage daughter’s smartphone to see how often she used it at school. ?e results shocked them both: “?roughout the week, she checked her phone an average of 14 times per class,” the mother told me. ?at’s about once every four minutes.Before banning the phone outright, the mother agreed to her daughter’splea for one last chance. ?e deal was simple: if she could go one week without checking her phone during class, she could keep it. Despite her best intentions, she couldn’t make it a single day. “I just get really jittery and uncomfortable,” she admitted. “It’s hard for me to focus unless I know I have my phone.”?at’s the problem: kids don’t usually reach for their phones because itbrings joy; they reach because it brings relief. And that shift—from wanting to needing—is the hallmark of a habit loop taking over.Every habit starts with a cue: something in the world grabs yourattention. Maybe the phone buzzes, or a notiﬁcation ﬂashes across the screen. Your brain registers this cue and waits to see what happens next.  You could do nothing, in which case the cue fades. Or you could take action—say, check your messages—to resolve it. Do that, and your brain will reward you with a warm chemical pat on the back: a hit of dopamine.CUE —> ACTION —> DOPAMINE  But the brain is a quick study. Repeat this loop often enough, and it rewrites the sequence. Now dopamine doesn’t wait for the action, it surges at the cue.CUE —> DOPAMINE —> ACTION   ?is is where the game changes. When dopamine comes after an action, it feels rewarding. But when it comes before, it creates tension. Suddenly, the mind becomes restless, hyper-focused on one thing: closing the loop. Now you’re driven to the action simply to make the agitation stop. ?at’s the deﬁnition of craving.Nearly half the developed world now lives in a low-grade state ofcompulsion with their phones. Billions of people have built habit loops that push them to reach for a device even when there’s no real desire to do so. We’re no longer picking up our phones out of interest; we’re doing it to quiet our own nervous system.Returning to the classroom, there are two important issues to consider:Not All Cues Are External?e phone doesn’t have to buzz to trigger craving. Internal cues—boredom, confusion, anxiety—can spark the loop just as easily. Unfortunately, as we saw in Chapter 1, good learning often feels uncomfortable. ?is means students who’ve linked that discomfort with phone use will reﬂexively reach for their device whenever a lesson gets hard.   ?at’s why ‘backpack-only’ phone policies rarely work. When the craving hits and the cure lies only a few feet away, most students can’t resist. In fact, research shows that in schools where phones must stay in bags, 25% of students still use them over an hour each day, and 10% use them more than three.To combat this, some schools insist students keep phones in theirlockers. Although this removes the physical distraction, it creates a second problem.Unmet Cravings Hijack AttentionOnce dopamine ﬁres, the brain expects action. If that action is delayed, dopamine release ramps up, craving intensiﬁes, and attention fractures. ?e brain starts pulling ever more focus away from the lesson and toward ﬁnding relief.  If a student knows they can get to their phone in 20 or 30 minutes, the craving builds minute by minute. ?is anticipation can be as distracting as having a phone in hand—and it produces the same negative learning outcomes.   ?e good news? Dopamine doesn’t climb forever. Once the brain realizes an action isn’t going to happen—say, because phones are locked away and truly inaccessible for hours—the craving peaks, then fades, and the habit loop unravels. ?at’s when focus returns and students can once again engage without that constant pull toward a device.Even better, when the pull is gone, something stronger can grow in itsplace. Instead of reaching for a phone at the ﬁrst sign of struggle, students can learn to self-regulate, seek support, or reﬂect—the same strategies we relied on before every ﬂicker of unease could be numbed by a screen.What to RememberOnce a habit loop forms, phones stop being tools for connection and start serving as regulators of the nervous system. When students know they’ll soon have screen access, dopamine surges and attention collapses. But when access is removed entirely, cravings fade and focus returns. Without full separation, harmful habits persist and learning su?ers. Banning phones isn’t about being strict—it’s about giving our children a chance to reset.Mechanism #2: ConsolidationImagine a fresh, snow-covered hill in the middle of a gentle blizzard.  Ski down it once, and you’ll carve a faint path. But with the snow still falling, that path will quickly vanish, as though you were never there. Ski the same route hundreds of times, though, and you’ll cut a lasting trail into the hillside—one that can withstand even the ﬁercest storm.Memory works much the same way.   In the brain, the hippocampus acts as the gateway to memory. In order for new information to be retained, it must ﬁrst pass through this neural region in a process called encoding. ?is is like your ﬁrst ski run—encoding marks the start of learning, but without reinforcement, memory traces rapidly fade. To prevent this, the hippocampus will replay newly formed memories hundreds of times in a process called consolidation. ?is high-frequency repetition etches learning deeply into the brain, making it stable, lasting,and easy to retrieve in the future.  We’ve long known that consolidation happens primarily during sleep. But one lingering question remains: how does the brain decide which memories are worth replaying overnight? By no means can we hold onto everything, so what separates the meaningful from the trivial?  A recent study o?ers a clue. Researchers asked participants to learn a simple piano piece while undergoing brain scans. As expected, the hippocampus lit up with encoding activity during active practice. But during rest breaks, something unexpected happened: the hippocampus spontaneously started replaying the notes they had just learned.?is suggests a key mechanism of memory: downtime. While we’reawake but relaxing, the brain enters into a unique form of waking consolidation. Although the details remain vague, it’s believed that this early replay process ‘tags’ certain memories for deeper processing during sleep. If your brain replays it during the day, it’s almost certain to lock it down at night.?e catch? ?e brain only enters waking consolidation during periods ofcalm, mentally undemanding rest. If a pause is ﬁlled with new, high-intensity input, this process will be blocked and memory formation will stall.?e implications are clear.  In schools with bell-to-bell phone bans, students are a?orded genuine downtime. As they walk between classes, talk with friends, or toss a ball at recess, their brains have the space for waking consolidation, reinforcing what they just learned.   But in schools that allow phone access between periods, many students jump on a device almost immediately. As they scroll through a rapid barrage of posts, videos, and messages, their brains are swamped with novelty and never have the chance to slow down. When these crucial pauses are missed, material from later lessons is likely to overwrite or muddle learning that should have been reinforced from earlier lessons.Without downtime, the brain can’t cut lasting trails. And when everypause is ﬁlled with a screen, even the best teaching is soon buried beneath fresh snow.What to RememberConsolidation is the brain’s way of converting short-term learning into long-term memory—and it begins immediately after learning.?e brain needs calm, quiet moments to replay and reinforce new material. When students spend their breaks scrolling, swiping, and gaming, their brains lose that crucial window for consolidation. As a result, learning fades or is overwritten entirely. Banning phones in school isn’t draconian—it simply gives our children the space they need to make learning stick.Mechanism #3: Cognitive DepletionWe’ve all felt it: that point in the day when your brain just quits. Focus slips, mistakes pile up, and even simple tasks feel exhausting.   ?is isn’t laziness—it’s mental fatigue. And it builds up faster than most people realize.  Just like a battery, the brain needs recharging in order to function at its best. Unfortunately, when students jump from one demanding task to the next without pause, their mental systems wear down rapidly: attention weakens, errors increase, and motivation fades.   ?is is another reason why low-stress breaks are essential for learning; they serve as the brain’s pit stop. While many biological systems contribute to mental fatigue, three are particularly important:?e Focus System (Norepinephrine)?is neurochemical helps lock attention in place. Unfortunately, when it’s inconstant use—like when students jump straight from classwork to a smartphone—it loses e?cacy and focus unravels.?e Fatigue Signal (Adenosine)?e longer we’re awake and mentally active, the more this chemical builds in the brain, creating that foggy, drained feeling. Interestingly, ca?eine works by blocking adenosine, which is why co?ee feels like a quick reset.?e Backup Fuel Tank (Astrocytic Glycogen)?e brain typically runs on sugar—but during periods of deep focus, it draws on a backup energy reserve stored in support cells. ?is reserve empties quickly and reﬁlls slowly, meaning the more its used, the weaker learning becomes.  In schools that allow genuine breaks—where students move, talk, or simply unwind with friends—these three systems get a chance to recalibrate. As a result, students return to class sharper, steadier, and better prepared to learn.  But when every pause is ﬁlled with media multitasking—scrolling, gaming, posting—the brain never gets a chance to recharge. Fatigue builds, fuel stores drain, and learning su?ers.   Protecting moments of calm isn’t indulgent—it’s what makes sustained learning possible over a six-hour school day.What to RememberWith prolonged mental stimulation, focus fades, energy slips, and the brain’s chemical balance falls out of balance. When every pause is ﬁlled with swipes and scrolls, fatigue builds quickly and dangerously. ?e good news: calm, phone-free breaks give students a chance to reset these systems so they return to class attentive, energized, and ready to learn. Banning phones isn’t about control—it’s about giving our children the chance to recalibrate and reengage with learning.So Now Then…Smartphones harm mental, physical, and cognitive wellbeing. Nobody can o?er a compelling reason why they should exist in schools, and permissive policies disrupt in-class attention, post-class memory, and future-class readiness.Given all this, the continued debate over bans confounds me. Here’s one ﬁnal data point to consider. It’s anecdotal, but telling.  Over the past decade, I’ve worked with dozens of schools that adopted ‘soft’ phone policies. Many eventually abandoned them and reverted back to unrestricted phone use after ﬁnding low compliance, inconsistent enforcement, and little beneﬁt.  I’ve also worked with schools that have implemented full bell-to-bell bans: no phones are allowed anywhere on campus, and any spotted devices are immediately conﬁscated. Among these schools, not one has ever reversed course. To my knowledge, no school has banned phones and later concluded students were better o? with them.Once the smartphones are gone, students are more focused, teachers aremore engaged, and everyone is more connected. Schools work better when everyone is fully present, not occasionally available.OceanofPDF.com| 6 |Artiﬁcial Intelligence: Part I?e Tool Nobody Asked For, Solving Problems Nobody HadLast year I had students who couldn’t write a paragraph. ?is year I’ve got kids handing in complete, polished essays—and I know they didn’t write a word of it. But God forbid I should say that out loud, because if I dare accuse anyone of cheating, I’ll be the one in trouble.?ere’s no point ﬁghting back, so I’ve given up. I love all the nonsense people spout about AI, like it’ll help students “reﬁne their ideas” or “think more deeply about feedback.” Anyone who says this has clearly never set foot in a real classroom or worked with real children. Kids use AI to cheat—end of story.–LUKE Teacher Since 2004 (Great Britain)  magine this: a pharmaceutical company develops a new drug. ?e problem? ?ey have no idea what it does—no clue what it treats, how e?ective it is, or what side e?ects it might have. Undeterred, they release it globally with no testing or usage guidelines. Rather than pushing back,governments, businesses, and schools all jump on board. Billions of doses are handed out, and patients are told to ﬁgure out for themselves what the drug is good for.  Sound absurd? It should. Yet that’s precisely what’s happening with generative artiﬁcial intelligence (AI).   For most of human history, tools were created to solve speciﬁc problems. Cranes were built to lift heavy objects; antibiotics were developed to ﬁght bacterial infections. But solving a problem was never enough—a tool also had to solve it well. Laundry detergents that almost removed stains failed; refrigerators that sort of cooled food were abandoned.?at logic began to change with the rise of digital technologies.Suddenly, tools no longer needed to address genuine challenges. Many developers stopped even pretending to address anything at all—they simply built technologies because they could, then trusted users to discover what (if any) function it served. As Stanford professor Mehran Sahami admits, “?ere is frequently no goal or purpose for a particular [digital] technology.”?is was the shift from problem-solving tools to solution-seekingtechnologies.   Besides fueling a throwaway tech culture that generates incredible wealth while delivering almost nothing of lasting value, this shift has created a deeper problem: when a new digital tool ultimately turns out to be harmful, it’s the public that pays the price. Companies walk away unscathed, having never promised anything speciﬁc to begin with.Nowhere has this been more obvious than with the rise of modern AI,especially large language models like ChatGPT.   ChatGPT began as an academic experiment—an intellectual exercise to see whether human language could be modeled at scale. ?e developers never set out to solve any speciﬁc issue, and they had no clear idea how theirtool might be useful. When ChatGPT was released to the public, the designers had one main goal: to learn from the crowd. ?ey asked the world to “have a play” with their new invention and tell them what purpose it served.   ?ere’s little evidence potential harms or broader disruptions were ever meaningfully considered. ?ey simply opened Pandora’s box and left the rest of us to deal with whatever came out.  Within months, schools around the world were told they needed to incorporate this tool—even though no one could clearly explain how. ?ere was no guidance, no clear outcomes, and few teachers were even sure what the tool actually was. Still, the warning was clear: adapt quickly or risk becoming obsolete.More than three years later, debate still rages over how best to use AItools in the classroom. But this debate misses the point. Asking how best to use these tools assumes they’re worth using in the ﬁrst place.What if they’re not?The DataAt the time of this writing, public access to ChatGPT is barely three years old. ?at means speciﬁc data on its impact within education is still very limited.  More concerning is that most of the research we do have comes from early adopters—people who genuinely want AI to revolutionize learning.?is kind of hyper-enthusiasm isn’t unique to artiﬁcial intelligence. Across many ﬁelds, the ﬁrst wave of research into a new theory or invention is usually dominated by die-hard believers.  Because of this, researchers often encounter what’s called the decline e?ect. Put simply, premature excitement tends to inﬂate early results. Butover time, as more independent and skeptical researchers begin testing the same tools, e?ect sizes start to drop—sometimes dramatically.  For example, in psychology, early studies reported the e?ects of ego depletion to sit well above +0.60. Today, estimates hover closer to +0.25—a 58% decline. In medicine, the relative mortality reduction due to Omega-3 Fatty Acids has dropped from +0.27 in early trials to +0.04 today—an 85% decline. Within education, the power of growth mindset has fallen from+0.66 to +0.05 in less than twenty years—a 92% decline.   ?is matters because over the next decade, you’re likely to hear numerous reports claiming that AI boosts learning. But with the decline e?ect in mind, you can take those claims with a grain of salt. It’s not that they’re necessarily wrong—it’s that far more research is needed before we can safely separate excitement from evidence.With that said, two meta-analyses have already been publishedsuggesting that ChatGPT can signiﬁcantly improve learning in K-12 schools. Needless to say, tech enthusiasts have been promoting these papers widely, so it’s worth looking at each more closely.   ?e ﬁrst analysis pooled data from ten studies and reported an e?ect size of +0.58—a value that, on the surface, sounds impressive. But a closer look reveals problems so basic they’d make a ﬁrst-year research student blush:• Two of the ten included studies were duplicates, meaning 20% of the analyzed data was a carbon copy. In research, that’s a glaring breach of standard methodology.• Two studies never explained how ChatGPT was actually utilized, making them impossible to replicate—the scientiﬁc equivalent of a magic trick.• One study didn’t compare AI to anything at all—it simply pitted two versions of ChatGPT against each other. With no control group, the result tells us exactly nothing.  When we strip away these ﬂawed studies—which should never have been included in the ﬁrst place—the e?ect size drops from +0.58 to a far less spectacular +0.27.   ?e second meta-analysis managed to be even thinner. It combined data from just ﬁve studies and reported an e?ect size of +0.43. Again, though, the problems jump o? the page:• One study measured only teacher use—no student ever touched ChatGPT. ?at’s not a study on AI and learning; that’s a study on teacher practice.• One study failed to report how students used the AI, so we have no idea what was actually accomplished.• One study showed ChatGPT harmed learning, but the meta-analysis accidentally ﬂipped the sign and reported it as a positive e?ect. Yes, really.Once those errors are corrected, the e?ect size plummets to -0.08.  Combine the cleaned results from both meta-analyses, and the overall e?ect size comes out to just +0.17, well below the +0.40 threshold for meaningful educational impact. Furthermore, as more rigorous, independent studies are conducted, the decline e?ect suggests this value is likely to fall even further.So, when it comes to learning, the early data suggests AI is not helping.In fact, it’s almost certainly harming learning compared to more traditional methods. As one professor from Cal State warns about AI’s inﬂuence in school, “Massive numbers of students are going to emerge from university with degrees, and into the workforce, who are essentially illiterate.”   But knowing that a tool harms learning is di?erent from understanding why. Until we better grasp the mechanisms, we can’t meaningfully counterthe negative impact of AI in the classroom. ?e damage can largely be traced to three mechanisms.Mechanism #1: OffloadingMy sister-in-law exercises regularly. Her husband does not.  A few months back, they rented a pair of e-bikes (bicycles with small motors that make pedaling easier) to tour around Melbourne. Unfortunately, after about an hour, the batteries on both bikes died, meaning they had to pedal home under their own power.  For my sister-in-law, this was no problem: thanks to her conditioning, she made it home without issue. Her husband, however, wasn’t so lucky. With no ﬁtness to fall back on, he quickly ran out of steam, ditched the bike, and was forced to call an Uber.   ?e moral here is obvious: capacity has to be built. If you train hard, you can rely on that training when needed. If you don’t, you’ll struggle the moment support disappears.  Why is something so obvious in the realm of physical exercise so often overlooked when it comes to learning?  Many digital tools are designed to support what psychologists call cognitive o?oading: the act of using external devices to perform mental tasks. ?ink of using spellcheck to avoid proofreading, or copy-and-paste to avoid rewriting. AI is the ultimate o?oading device. ChatGPT can read, write, edit, calculate, and even illustrate—all with minimal user input. ?at’s why it’s often hailed as the ultimate productivity booster across industries.But here’s the problem: productivity is not the same as learning.  A teacher in Chicago once told me about a student who turned in a ﬂawless lab report. Unfortunately, when she asked him to walk the class through his work, the boy froze. It turns out he’d never actually done the experiment; ChatGPT handled everything for him. Despite weeks oflearning the concepts and practicing the procedures in class, without his AI tool he was completely lost.   ?is illustrates an important issue: whenever people regularly o?oad a task, they naturally come to lean on the tool. Over time, this creates dependence: take the tool away, and performance collapses.  Take me as an example. ?ere was a time when I could recite every formula needed for statistical analyses. But after two decades of using stats software, I’ve forgotten most of them. Now, I rely on my computer for nearly every calculation.   We see the same thing in schools. Students who learn higher-order math (like geometry or trigonometry) with a graphing calculator often outperform their peers—as long as they have the calculator. Take it away, and their scores fall sharply, sometimes well below those of students who learned without one.Here’s the key di?erence: I learned statistics before I began o?oading.?at foundation means I can quickly relearn forgotten material with only a bit of review. If my computer crashes, I’d need only days to recover my prior skills. Like my sister-in-law, I can adapt when support vanishes.  But students who start with o?oading never develop that foundation. When the tool is removed, they can’t simply review and adapt—they have to start from scratch. ?ey’re stuck on a dead e-bike, hoping another machine will come to their rescue.When faced with this, tech advocates often shrug: So what? If studentscan “get the job done,” who cares if they require a machine to do it?   ?is is a classic case of expert blindness. Once a skill is mastered, it becomes incredibly di?cult to remember what it took to initially learn it. As a result, experts often assume that the same tools they use to perform taskse?ciently will be equally useful for novices. ?ey forget that what makes the tool e?ective is the deep expertise they bring to it.Which brings us to a deeper issue: vetting.   When experts o?oad, they are able to quickly assess whether the output makes sense. ?eir background lets them spot mistakes and course-correct instantly. If I ask AI what 2 + 2 equals and it says 15, I’ll recognize the error immediately and ﬁx it.  Novices can’t do that. Without background knowledge, they can’t tell whether an answer is right or wrong. If AI tells them 15, they’re likely to accept it because they have nothing deeper to compare it to. Later, when they fail a test or struggle in class, they often have no idea what went wrong or how to ﬁx it.Without expertise, o?oading short-circuits learning. Students don’t justfail to master the material, they learn to trust tools that they don’t understand and can’t meaningfully evaluate.  In the end, students using AI may “get the job done,” but that’s no guarantee they’ve done it correctly, can repeat it independently, or even understand what they’ve done.Mechanism #2: Higher-Order SkillsIn the age of artiﬁcial intelligence, memorizing facts is obsolete. With tools like ChatGPT able to access and organize vast amounts of information in seconds, students no longer need to waste time learning “stu?.” Rather, schools should focus on helping students ask better questions and let AI handle the rest.?is argument is, hands-down, one of the most pernicious in education.   ?e inescapable truth is this: higher-order skills are built on a foundation of lower-order knowledge. In other words, “stu? ” is the foundation of all deep thought. As educational psychologist Paul A. Kirschner notes, “Long-term memory is now viewed as the central, dominant structure of human cognition.” Without that foundation, so-called “critical thinking” collapses into guesswork.  Let me show you what this looks like in practice. Using your critical thinking skills, try to reason through the following:Recently, magnetoencephalographic data was published suggesting the human brain generates a very low-frequency wave (~6cph) detectable by SQUIDs. ?is signal cannot be source-localized, but it appears to spread rostrally from the lingula to Brodmann’s area 10. What might this data suggest about the process of ephaptic coupling within the brain?  Unless you’re a neuroscientist or an engineer, you probably had no idea where to begin.   Now ask ChatGPT to explain every term in that paragraph. Even after reading the deﬁnitions, you’ll still be no closer to applying your critical thinking skills to this scenario. Why? Because information remains almost entirely unusable until it’s integrated into your prior knowledge. In other words, access to information will never be a substitute for actually learning it.To be fair, you could paste the entire scenario into ChatGPT and copyits answer—but that’s about as far as things will go. Parroting back what AI spits out won’t help you ask better questions or reach deeper solutions. As discussed earlier in the section on vetting, if you don’t understand the output, you can’t critically evaluate it.  A parent once told me her son had used AI to “help” prepare a history presentation. It was detailed, well-structured, and conﬁdent. During a practice run at home, his family was impressed—his mother said it sounded incredible. But halfway through the actual presentation, the teacher began asking a few probing questions. It turns out the “facts” he’d memorized weren’t real. Without a foundation of genuine knowledge to draw upon,both the student and his parents had been fooled by what simply sounded good. Fortunately, the teacher wasn’t.?at’s critical thinking. But what about creativity?   At its core, creativity is the process of combining existing knowledge in novel ways to solve unfamiliar problems. You can think of it like tangrams, the game where you arrange small shapes to form a larger ﬁgure. In this case, the small pieces are your memories, and the larger image is the problem you’re trying to solve.   It may surprise you, but we all follow the same core process whenever we act creatively:Stage 1: Focused ?inkingYou sit down at your desk, pen in hand, staring at the problem in front of you. You jot down ideas, cross them out, shu?e them around. You test one possibility, then another, but nothing clicks. Your mind can only juggle a few ideas at a time, so progress is slow, almost plodding.Stage 2: Di?use ?inkingFrustrated, you step away. Maybe you walk the dog, stir a pot on the stove,or drift into a nap. Without realizing it, your brain starts playing matchmaker—pulling together the ideas you were just wrestling with and pairing them with memories, experiences, and knowledge you didn’t even know you remembered. ?is subconscious background process roams freely through your entire mental library, looking for patterns you missed.Stage 3: Focused ?inking (Again)And then, it happens: halfway through rinsing the dishes, a promising idea bubbles into awareness. ?e “aha” moment hits, and you rush back to your desk to test it. Now the conscious work begins again—reﬁning, building, and shaping the idea into something that just might work.   ?is is creativity for everyone: a continuous cycle of focused and di?use thinking, where ideas collide, combine, and slowly take new shape.  Here’s the crucial point: if this process only included focused thinking, then mere access to information would make everyone more creative. Heck, I’d be on ChatGPT right now folding proteins and curing cancer. But much of creativity happens when you’re not actively thinking—when your brain is silently reorganizing what it already knows.During di?use thinking, your brain can’t access the internet. It can onlywork with what it has stored in long-term memory. Internalizing knowledge is non-negotiable: any subconscious process involved in higher-order thinking can only use material you’ve already learned.   ?is is why creative geniuses train for years before producing original work: Picasso for 11 years, Frank Lloyd Wright for 14, Beethoven for 20. It’s also why the average age of Nobel Prize winners has climbed steadily for decades: from forty-ﬁve in 1920 to sixty-ﬁve today.
