Everyone must build deep knowledge before breaking new ground, and as ﬁelds grow morecomplex, it simply takes longer to master the fundamentals before pushing into the unknown.   Using AI to skip the slow, sometimes tedious work of learning isn’t the key to developing higher-order skills; it’s the surest way to prevent them from emerging at all. Without deep, internalized knowledge, critical thinking stays shallow, creativity remains imitation, and the real work of the mind never begins.Mechanism #3: IdentityNarcissus.  According to Greek mythology, this mortal hunter spurned the love o?ered by the god Echo. As punishment, he was led to a pool of water where he caught sight of his own reﬂection and fell deeply in love with it.Later, when he realized the image wasn’t real and never could be, he fell into deep despair and drowned himself in the pool.   ?is seemingly straightforward tale is one of the most misunderstood of all the ancient myths. It’s often interpreted as warning against conceit and self-centeredness—vanity cometh before the fall. But notice: Narcissus doesn’t fall in love with himself; he falls in love with his reﬂection.   ?is isn’t a story of egocentrism. It’s a story about the perils of externalizing one’s identity; about valuing public perception over personal understanding. Narcissus falls for an unreal image of himself; a vision created and reﬂected back to him by the outside world. Once he realizes that image is not his true identity, he chooses death over the daunting task of self-discovery.Hold onto this—we’ll return to it shortly.  Arguably the biggest problem with modern technology is its obsession with mimicking human beings. Rather than leaning into its unique digital capabilities, developers often ﬁxate on making the machine invisible: on tricking users into believing they are interacting with a real person.   Nowhere is this more apparent than with AI. Tools like ChatGPT are so committed to appearing human that they erase any trace of the real people required for them to function. ?e thousands of books, essays, and articles written by actual human beings that drive AI are deliberately hidden and never cited to make it feel as if the machine is speaking with original authority—as though its responses were conjured from genuine thought rather than statistical pattern matching.?e ability to trace ideas back to their source has long been one ofhumanity’s best defenses against deception. Consider this book: for any claim I make, you can ﬂip to the back and see exactly where it came from. Follow that citation, and you can track the idea back further, through layersof interpretation and context. ?is capacity to establish intellectual lineage not only reveals how ideas have evolved over time, but also how I’ve speciﬁcally chosen to interpret and apply them here.Unfortunately, the rise of the internet made this tracing vastly harder.?e sheer speed and volume of online content made it incredibly easy to copy or steal someone else’s work without fear of being caught. Over time, this didn’t just lead to widespread dishonesty—it led to widespread pathological dishonesty. Without accountability, plagiarists begin to believe their own lies.Artiﬁcial intelligence has only magniﬁed this problem.   When students use AI to write an essay, compose a song, or generate an image, all the underlying source material is concealed. Without any awareness of intellectual heritage, they take full credit for the output—wrongly believing they played a meaningful role in its creation. Don’t misunderstand: the problem isn’t using AI to make things. ?e problem is when students think they had anything to do with their creation.When Sir Isaac Newton wrote, “If I have seen further, it is by standing onthe shoulders of giants,” he was acknowledging the centuries of thought that came before him; the ideas he studied, theories he mastered, and equationshe built upon. He understood exactly why he was able to see so far, and he honored those who made his work possible.   AI acts like a helicopter that drops students straight onto the summit: no climbing, no struggle, no understanding of what it took to get there. A shroud is thrown over everyone who came before, leaving students to believe they’re special—that they must have simply been born twenty feet tall. ?ey mistakenly think they know far more than they really do and can accomplish far more than they actually can.And so we return to Narcissus.   When it’s said that children today are increasingly narcissistic, it doesn’t mean they love themselves deeply. It means they are outsourcing their self-development, relying ever more on external opinion to construct their identity. As the world says they are, so they believe themselves to be.  Admittedly, this tendency has always existed to some degree—but it became supercharged with the rise of the internet. ?e constant stream of likes, pokes, and thumbs-up taught children to chase external validation rather than seek internal truth. ?ey posted poems, songs, and paintings online, then waited for the verdict. No work was good or bad until the world voted. Anything that earned likes was deemed meaningful, internalized, and built-upon; anything that earned dislikes was discarded.But at least during this period, children were still creating their ownwork; there was at least some connection between the product and their actual abilities. But AI deprives even that.   I once spoke with a young girl who had posted an AI-generated poem to Instagram. She said she liked it well enough—until it only got a handful of likes. Her response caught me o?-guard: “I guess I’m not a good poet,” she shrugged. But she hadn’t written a single word of it.Today, children rarely produce the material they ask others to judge.Instead, they use a machine to generate artifacts entirely external to themselves, throw these false creations online, and wait for the votes to roll in. ?ey then absorb the verdict—good or bad—as though it were a direct measure of their own worth. In truth, they had no hand in the process at all. At no point do they seriously pause to consider who they actually are or what they genuinely value.And perhaps the most troubling part is that very few students see any ofthis as a problem. ?ey genuinely believe this is how identity is formed.My fear is that, before long, they’ll be right.So Now Then…When students o?oad the basics, they erode the foundation upon which higher-order abilities grow. And without deep knowledge or skills to drawupon, they soon come to depend on external sources to shape and validate their identity.  A college counselor recently told me about a freshman who openly admitted to using AI to write his admissions essay, to complete every major assignment in his ﬁrst semester, and even to generate funny stories to help him “stand out” socially. By spring, he’d built a GPA, a social life, and a personality almost entirely out of machine output. ?e counselor explained she genuinely struggled to go deeper with this student. “Every time I asked what he was passionate about or hoped to achieve at school,” she recalled, “he just stared at me, completely lost. ?at’s when I knew we had a real problem on our hands.”He couldn’t see past the next prompt. And to make matters worse, thisprocess is being actively encouraged. Tech evangelists insist that prompting is creating; that simply conceiving an idea fosters intellectual growth and personal development. But conception is easy. What really matters is transformation: the slow, di?cult work of turning thought into reality; of failing, adapting, and eventually discovering how your ideas ﬁt into the larger world. And when everyone turns to the same machines to bypass the hard work of transformation, convergence is inevitable.We have more “creators” today than at any point in history, yet whatthey’re producing is increasingly the same. When identity is built on reﬂection, originality gives way to virality. People no longer create because they have something to say, but because they believe their “followers” have something they want to hear.   Scaled across millions, the externalization of identity doesn’t just hollow out the self—it hollows out the culture. For much of modern history, each decade carried a distinct ﬁngerprint: ﬁlms of the 60s looked nothing like those of the 70s, and the music of the 80s sounded nothing like that of the90s. But with the rise of the internet, that rhythm faltered and culture began to recycle itself. Now, as AI becomes the default tool of creation, the process is calcifying.   ?is is the endpoint of externalized identity. When an entire generation chases trends instead of creating them, culture freezes—and with it, the expression of anything truly personal or unique.At that point, we haven’t just lost learning. We haven’t just lost creativity. We’ve lost ourselves.OceanofPDF.com| 7 |Artiﬁcial Intelligence: Part II?e Deeper ?reat of AI to EducationAI is changing how everyone thinks about everything. I’ve sat in sta? meetings where teachers have seriously proposed abandoning essay writing altogether and replacing it with exercises where students critique ChatGPT output. In those moments, I ﬁnd myself tightly gripping the table, doing everything I can to hold the line—because I still believe education is about building real understanding, not just producing polished artifacts. I’m not opposed to technology, I’m just terriﬁed that in all this talk about innovation, we are forgetting the true purpose of school.–JOSEPH, Head of School since 2017 (Australia)e’ve seen how AI harms individual learning, but now it’s time to zoom out. How might these tools alter the very narrative of schoolitself?   A warning upfront: this chapter will feel di?erent from the ones before it. Up to this point, we’ve focused on the concrete—data, mechanisms,pedagogy. Here, I’m going to ask you to step with me into deeper water. Together, let’s explore the underlying logic of artiﬁcial intelligence, and consider how this could recast the very meaning of education.   If you’re mainly interested in practical takeaways, feel free to skip ahead to Part 3, where I outline what parents, teachers, and schools can do to resist the growing EdTech incursion. But if you want to grasp the scale of the institutional shift now bearing down on us, keep reading. Because this shift isn’t merely pedagogical; it cuts to the heart of what school is for—and if we get this wrong, the damage may be irreparable.Technopoly: Revisited“Surely there is something perverse about schoolteachers being enthusiastic about [digital technology]. Such enthusiasm always calls to mind an image of some turn-of-the-century blacksmith who not only sings the praises of the automobile but also believes that his business will be enhanced by it.”–Neil Postman, TechnopolyIn 1993, media theorist Neil Postman published Technopoly: a landmark work exploring how new technologies subtly, but fundamentally, transform social institutions like education. I often wish I could hear Postman’s thoughts on artiﬁcial intelligence. Sadly, he passed away in 2003, two decades before the modern AI wave began.What follows, then, is my attempt to update and extend Postman’scentral argument to account for ChatGPT and other generative AI tools. Ri?ng alongside great thinkers of the past is always risky, so please: if anything in the pages ahead strikes you as meaningful or persuasive, the credit goes to Postman.The Deep QuestionSocrates.  One of history’s most inﬂuential thinkers will forever remain one of its greatest enigmas. ?is is because he despised writing and never set down a single word himself. As a result, everything we know about him and his ideas comes from second-hand sources.Why was he so opposed to writing?  A compelling clue comes from his most famous student, Plato. In Phaedrus, Plato recounts a myth about ?oth (the Egyptian God of language) and ?amus (the Pharaoh of Upper Egypt). In this tale, ?oth proudly presents what he calls his greatest invention: writing.“Writing is a tool, my lord the king, which will improve both the wisdom and memory of the Egyptians. I have discovered a sure receipt for memory and wisdom.”But ?amus is unimpressed. He responds:“?ose who acquire the tool of writing will cease to exercise their memory and become forgetful; they will rely on external signs instead of their own internal resources. And as for wisdom, your pupils will have the reputation for it without the reality: they will receive a quantity of information without proper instruction, and in consequence be thought very knowledgeable when they are for the most part quite ignorant.”  Notice that ?amus’s objection is not about what might be written; he doesn’t suggest that writing could be useful in some contexts but not in others. His concern is with the act of writing itself. ?e tool, he argues, demands scrutiny.Too often, we adopt new tools without considering how they shape ourperception, reasoning, and understanding. Yet every tool carries with it a worldview; a way of organizing reality. It dictates which ideas are defensible, which terms are meaningful, which questions can be asked, and which answers are acceptable.To use a tool is to adopt its worldview, whether we realize it or not.   ?is is what Langdon Winner alluded to when he said, “?e things we call ‘technologies’ are ways of building order in our world.”  It’s what Walter Ong meant when he remarked, “Technologies are not mere exterior aids but also interior transformations of consciousness.”  And it’s what Marshall McLuhan captured in his now-famous phrase, “?e medium is the message.”  Consider, for example, the grade. In 1792, Cambridge tutor William Farish invented the practice of assigning numerical values to student work in order to lighten his workload. But grading brought with it a logic of its own: it turned abstract traits like “intelligence” and “motivation” into measurable entities, assigned numbers to those entities, and then ranked students according to those numbers in a way that appeared objective.From there, the path was set. Grades opened the door to standardizedtesting and catalyzed the idea that K-12 schools exist primarily to sort children for university admission and job placement. Every time we use this tool, we assume its inherent worldview.  Returning to Socrates: he didn’t merely fear that writing would make people less capable. He feared it would quietly redeﬁne words like memory and wisdom into something lesser, and that no one would notice the change. He worried future generations would depend on writing in ways that dulled their thinking, never realizing that something essential had been lost. He feared writing would restructure the world so that “dumb” could pass for “smart,” and no one would think to question it.   ?is idea, that tools embed worldviews which change the world in subtle yet profound ways, lies at the heart of Postman’s Technopoly. In his words:“Tools alter the structure of our interests: the things we think about. ?ey alter the character of our symbols: the things we think with. And they alter the nature of community: the arena in which thoughts develop.”   Artiﬁcial intelligence is just a tool, which means the question of whether it can improve learning is almost trivial. ?e real question is: what ideology do these tools impose? How do they demand we redeﬁne ourselves and our abilities? And in so doing, how does it transform the very purpose of school?The Setting“We shape our tools and thereafter they shape us.”–John M. Culkin, ?e Saturday ReviewTo understand the worldview embedded within artiﬁcial intelligence, we ﬁrst need to consider the cultural context that produced it. Postman outlined three major stages of technological development that can help us situate AI within a broader historic arc. Across each stage, it’s not only the tools that evolve, but also our understanding of what it means to be human.Stage 1: Tool-Using CulturesIn this ﬁrst stage, tools are created to solve speciﬁc, practical problems. Roman aqueducts delivered clean water to a growing city. ?e astrolabe enabled accurate celestial navigation. ?ese tools were valuable precisely because they addressed clear, local needs.Here, tools are subordinate to human purpose. Man is seen as central—often divine—and tools are understood as extensions of human e?ort. ?e tool serves the user, not the other way around.Stage 2: Technocratic SocietiesIn the second stage, tools are created to solve a single, overarching problem: e?ciency. ?e power loom mechanized cloth production at ten times the speed of a handweaver. Watt’s steam engine vastly increased mechanical output while reducing fuel demands. Progress comes to be deﬁned as doing things faster and cheaper.In this world, humans are increasingly structured around the needs ofmachines. ?ink of the assembly line: man becomes a cog within a larger mechanical system. At the same time, the language of machinery begins to seep into how we describe ourselves. People are said to “blow o? steam,” “shut down,” or “get cranked up.” Importantly, this language remains metaphorical. Humans may act like machines, but everyone still understands they are not machines.Stage 3: TechnopolyIn the third stage, tools are no longer created to solve clear problems. ?ey simply arrive, and their justiﬁcation comes later. ?e laser was famously described as “a solution in search of a problem.” ChatGPT began as an academic experiment with no deﬁned purpose. In a Technopoly, tools without obvious functions aren’t seen as ﬂawed; they’re celebrated as signs of innovation.Whereas in earlier stages human work was supported by machines, nowit’s dependent upon and increasingly replaceable by them. Crucially, all sense of metaphor collapses. Man is no longer like a machine; he is a machine. Asthe boundaries between tool and user dissolve, humans themselves are redeﬁned as a form of technology.   To see Technopoly in action, consider how we now talk about the brain. It is routinely described as an “information processor” that “crunches bits” in order to “store and retrieve memories.” In the past, such language would have been recognized as metaphor; but now it’s taken as literal fact. ?e brain, we are told, is a biological computer.And herein lies the deeper problem: in the grand reductionism ofTechnopoly, man is not merely understood as a machine—he is understood as an incredibly weak and malfunctioning one. We are cast as ﬂawed and feeble versions of the very tools we create.AI is the pinnacle of this logic.  Emotion, perception, and lived experience—human elements long considered central to intelligence—are reframed as bugs to be ﬁxed. In this worldview, AI can think better than humans precisely because it has stripped away all that messy subjectivity, leaving behind what is treated as the purest distillation of intelligence.In a Technopoly, tools are assumed to be infallible. Whenever they provemore cumbersome or less e?ective than what came before, the blame never falls on the tool—it falls on the user. Have you ever received a meaningless answer from ChatGPT, only to be told it was your fault for not being speciﬁc enough with your input? ?e entire notion of “prompt engineering” serves to excuse the failures of AI by shifting responsibility onto inexperienced consumers. Objective tools cannot fail—only subjective people can.The Lead Character“We live in a world where there is more and more information, and less and less meaning.”-Jean Baudrillard, Simulacra and SimulationWe’ve set the stage: modern Technopoly has taken hold, and one of its most telling features is that we’ve reduced humans to lesser versions of the machines we build.Now it’s time to meet the primary player in our story: information.?e dangers of having too little information are immediate and obvious.?e spread of disease; failed harvests; military disasters. History is full of moments when a lack of data led to catastrophe.  But few acknowledge the opposite danger: having too much information.   A deﬁning feature of our modern Technopoly is information overwhelm. Every day, digital users post over 500 million tweets, upload more than 3 million videos, and generate an estimated 1.7 megabytes of data per person per second—the equivalent of an 850-page book for every person on earth, every second.When humans are confronted with this much information, coherencecollapses. Structure, order, and clarity are buried beneath an avalanche of data. Suddenly, almost everything starts to sound plausible, because we lose the frameworks needed to ﬁlter, evaluate, and prioritize what we encounter. Consider this: it’s long been known that the antennae of sea slugs are ten times stronger than spider silk. ?e only problem is scale—they’re too small to harvest. But scientists recently isolated the relevant DNA sequence and began growing miles of slug antennae in specialized laboratories. ?e U.S.Secret Service has been the ﬁrst to exploit this breakthrough, developinglightweight street clothing with the stopping power of three Kevlar vests.Sounds incredible, right??e problem is, I just made all of that up—not a single word was true.  When we are continuously bombarded with information, it becomes increasingly di?cult to distinguish sense from nonsense. Faced with overload, people tend to respond in one of two ways: either they lose the ability to think critically and blindly accept everything they hear, or they overcorrect and start trusting nothing at all. In both cases, expertise erodes, contradictions multiply, and belief systems collapse into jumbled collections of ill-ﬁtting ideas. Anything goes—because who can say otherwise?When too much information destroys coherence, meaning soon follows.And when meaning collapses, the world begins to feel chaotic.Piecing It Together“?e real danger of our technical age is not so much that machines will begin to think like man, but that man will begin to think like machines.”–attributed to Sydney J. HarrisSocial coherence and personal grounding depend on well-organized information. Without it, we lose any ability to stand on solid ground amidst a storm of chaos. And so a key question becomes: who has taken on the task of organizing information during each stage of our technological development?Stage 1: Tool-Using CulturesDuring our tool-using stage, which spanned most of human history, information was scarce. With so little data available, shaping it into a coherent worldview was relatively straightforward. ?at responsibility typically fell to village elders, tribal leaders, or religious institutions: ﬁgures entrusted with preserving, transmitting, and making sense of collective knowledge.Stage 2: Technocratic Societies?e West’s entry into a technocratic society can be traced to the invention of the printing press. ?is single tool unleashed the ﬁrst large-scale wave of information across the world. Books were published en masse, literacy surged, and suddenly the volume of available data outpaced what local patriarchs or religious o?cials could manage.?e result was a breakdown of existing coherence.   ?e church, long the central organizer of knowledge, began to lose its dominion. In fact, it was the printing press that catalyzed the Protestant Reformation: the movement that shattered Catholic authority and decentralized the interpretation of “truth.”  With traditional structures overwhelmed, new institutions arose to manage the growing deluge of information. Political parties, precedent-based legal systems, formalized medicine, even the nuclear family all o?ered people ways of determining what to prioritize, what to ignore, and how to construct a coherent worldview from competing inputs.?e most important organizing institution of this era, however, wasschool.  Roughly 150 years after the printing press, the ﬁrst formal academic curriculum was introduced in France. Its purpose was singular: to give students a structured understanding of the world, ensuring they could make meaningful sense of whatever was going on around them. Curricula remain the bedrock of education to this day, shaping not only what is taught, but also how knowledge is best framed and sequenced to produce coherence. In a world drowning in data, schools served as society’s ﬁlters. ?ey helped students discern what mattered—and what didn’t—to maintain a consistent and functional society.Stage 3: Technopoly?e printing press may have ﬂooded the world with data, but at least books maintained internal structure. Botany texts were about plants; geology texts were about rocks; even newspapers were mostly local in scope—curated to reﬂect the lives and needs of a particular community.   ?e invention of the telegraph shattered that stability and ushered in the modern Technopoly.   For the ﬁrst time in history, information could travel instantly across vast distances. With that speed came a stripping of context. ?e telegraph splintered knowledge into fragments severed from any clear narrative thread. Now, a single newspaper page might feature a war in the Middle East, a famine in Africa, a dog show in New York, and a regatta in Pittsburgh—each given the same weight; each completely disconnected from the reader’s lived experience.In this world, coherence unravels. Without a solid framework for sortingimportance, establishing nuance, or constructing meaning, information quickly becomes noise. As the saying goes: when everything is urgent, nothing is.   ?e internet, of course, supercharged this model. Every day, we are bombarded with headlines, updates, and alerts—all ﬂattened into the same feed, all demanding the same level of attention. Each swipe delivers a new, disconnected bit of “content” that feels important for a moment, then vanishes with the next ﬂick of the thumb.With decontextualized information now ubiquitous, the authority oflong-standing institutions is fraying. Political parties are polarizing, their platforms now riddled with contradiction. Precedent-based legal systems are straining, as each case demands unique interpretation. Formal medicine is weakening, challenged by alternative and esoteric therapies. Even parentsare struggling to instill coherent values, as their children encounter thousands of disconnected “facts” each day—facts that give the illusion of meaning but leave only a fragmented sense of reality.As for schools, the cracks are undeniable.Students are now being told they can build their own curricula, as if they—and their devices—know best how the world works and how to make sense of it. Teachers face overconﬁdent students who equate tweets with dissertations, while the digital tools meant to provide clarity instead generate the very confusion they claim to resolve.  Tech advocates see none of this as a problem. ?ey proudly declare that institutions like schools have become obsolete. Since anyone can access unlimited information on demand, why should we bother with structures to organize it? Each person, they argue, can create their own coherence. And this, they insist, is progress.But this logic is exactly backwards.   ?e more abundant and decontextualized information becomes, the more we need strong organizing structures. Without common coherence, people carry the illusion of knowledge without the substance. ?ey believe contradictory ideas, embrace unstable and ever-shifting ideologies, and still feel conﬁdent they understand reality better than anyone who came before.?is is precisely the moment when courts, hospitals, and families mustdouble down on their organizing roles—not retreat from them. And this is precisely the moment when schools must reassert themselves as society’s chief organizers of information.Which brings us to the million dollar question…The Ideolog y of AIWhat kind of worldview does AI assume? When we use this tool, what does it imply about how society functions, and about our place within it?AI rests on three core tenets:1) Human ?ought Is Best Represented in LanguageIn Chapter 2, we saw that you are not a brain trapped inside a body; you are a body. Far from being a miniature computer docked in a skull, the essential “you” emerges through the interaction of your whole being with the wider world. From this perspective, it’s no surprise that for most of human history cognition was understood as multimodal and often indescribable. Emotion, intuition, imagery, spatial awareness, sensory patterning, even dream states—all were seen as central to how humans think.  Within this tradition, language was a medium: a tool for expressing thought or sca?olding it into new forms. It was important, yes, but never comprehensive. ?is is why artists have so often struggled to “put their thoughts into words,” turning instead to paint, music, or movement to capture dimensions of thought that language could never hold.Large language models like ChatGPT invert this understanding.Because they tra?c only in words, their ideology begins with a basic reduction: thinking is language. If thought is best (or only) captured in words, then everything essential about the human mind is available on the page or screen. Anything that resists verbalization—embodiment, perception, insight—is dismissed as noise.In this worldview, what cannot be said cannot be thought.2) Human Language Can Be Statistically ModeledOnce thinking is equated with language, the next step is to treat words as data points—tokens to be counted, sequenced, and recombined. In this worldview, language is not expression but information, and the task becomes to uncover the hidden patterns that govern it.  From here, the leap is profound: if human thinking can be reduced to patterns of language, then modelling language is the same as modelling thought. ?is is precisely what ChatGPT and its counterparts claim to do. By mapping statistical relationships across billions of disconnected and decontextualized utterances, they present themselves not as modeling speech, but as modeling thought. ?is is why developers resist calling their tools “pattern matchers” and insist on calling them “intelligent.”In this worldview, what cannot be modeled does not exist.3) Only AI Can Find the Hidden Patterns of Human ?ought?e creed culminates with exclusivity. ?e human mind, we are told, is too slow, too narrow, and too biased to perceive the deep patterns buried in language. Only AI, with its speed and scale, can uncover the truths supposedly hidden within our thoughts—truths that promise solutions to our greatest challenges. ?e answers were always there; we were simply too limited to ﬁnd them.?e machine now becomes the true interpreter of human thought. Andif its output ever strikes us as strange or incoherent, that only conﬁrms our blindness: the system sees what we cannot and understands what we never will. Opacity becomes wisdom, and we trust the machine not because we comprehend it, but precisely because we do not.In this worldview, what cannot be understood belongs to the machine.The Impact on SchoolsWe’ve come a long way to reach a simple but signiﬁcant conclusion: AI undermines the very function of school.  Schools exist to help students build a coherent, shared worldview; to organize knowledge in ways that allow culture to grow, evolve, and endure.?eir job is not only to transmit facts, but also to curate meaning—weavingfragments into stories, sequencing ideas into disciplines, and guiding students toward collective understanding.   But the ideology of AI denies this. By reducing thought to language and coherence to patterns, it strips schools of their broader role as meaning-makers.   When schools embrace AI, they signal institutional surrender and tacitly declare the end of their role as organizers of knowledge. ?is shouldn’t be surprising. In the worldview of AI, coherence is not a human achievement but a machine discovery. And so the transfer of authority from teacher to tool begins.When teachers outsource planning, assessment, and feedback to AI, theyimply that meaning-making is beyond human capacity. And if teachers can’t fulﬁll their core function, why should we expect any more from students? If human thinking is subordinate to AI, then the future of school isn’t learning—it’s training. Students no longer need to learn how to think; they need only learn how to prompt the tools that will do the thinking for them. And lest anyone feel uneasy about this shift, fear not: we’ll simply redeﬁne the term “thinking” so that no one notices what’s been lost.Sound far-fetched? It’s already happening.  As we saw in Chapter 3, reading comprehension has already been redeﬁned to mean “extracting a single idea from a short passage”—exactly like parsing a snippet of AI output. Likewise, the writing section of this year’s SAT exam reveals the same drift. Instead of asking students to construct and defend an argument, the test now asks them to spot typos and correct grammatical errors in isolated sentences. In other words, writing is being redeﬁned as “editing decontextualized output”—the same task students would perform if ChatGPT drafted their essay.?is is the AI worldview made tangible: break information intofragments and train humans to operate at the micro-level. Context, sequencing, and originality vanish—because in a world built around machine output, the ability to create or sustain a coherent argument from scratch is irrelevant.Welcome to Technopoly.   To embrace AI is to declare that teachers are in over their heads and that only machines can make school coherent. It is to claim that students are best served not by collective exploration, but by “personalized” fragments tailored by algorithm.Which brings us to the greatest danger: the erosion of shared reality.  In the face of informational overload, the glue that holds cultures together is a shared reference point; a uniﬁed framework for sifting experience and assigning common meaning. ?at is what schools have long provided: a structured narrative that unites individuals from all backgrounds into a coherent functioning society.But when AI o?ers each student their own “truth,” there can be nocommon curriculum, no shared canon, no collective conversation. And as many countries have experienced in recent years, when citizens fragment, democratic discourse and civic trust begin to collapse.  One fear is that this is precisely the outcome tech companies seek. In a world of manufactured chaos and incoherence, meaning can only be found in the very machines that caused the disruption. What better way to secure devoted customers than by o?ering the only semblance of clarity?  But a greater fear is that none of this is intentional—that developers never paused to consider the consequences of their inventions, and that we are all passengers on a speeding train with no one at the helm.   ?is is why schools must reclaim their primary role—not as content providers, but as meaning-makers. School may be the only consistent (ifimperfect) conductor: human in design, clear in intention, and grounded in purpose. If schools surrender this role to machines, they will not survive in the world AI is working to create—and I fear neither will any remaining sense of coherence.So Now Then…One of the ﬁrst points made in this book was that education is not broken. But our modern Technopoly is doing everything it can to make it appear otherwise.  Schools are under pressure from all sides: to stop focusing on human development and start prioritizing job preparation; to stop building transferable skills and start teaching trendy tools; to stop cultivating a society and start serving individuals. As information overwhelm works to fracture education, schools are faced with a choice.Choice one: allow AI to seep into the classroom and reshape pedagogyin its own image. If we do this, the small cracks we see today will widen into giant chasms until, within a few decades, school as we know it will be all but gone.   ?is isn’t hyperbole—we’re already seeing it in the university sector. Institutions that have stood strong for centuries are scrambling to digitize—and in the process, eroding both student and public trust. Seemingly impervious colleges are now on the verge of collapse, and there’s no reason to believe K-12 education cannot su?er the same fate. If that happens, an entire generation (or more) will grow up without clarity, coherence, or a stable cultural foundation.Choice two: batten down the hatches. Reject the push from corporateinterests and information engines. Schools have an incredible immune system—one that has allowed them to survive countless incursions in thepast. ?ey have absorbed the radio, the television, and early teaching machines without losing their soul. If we stay grounded in the core purpose of coherent learning, we can ﬁght o? this incursion well.?e choice is ours.   In my experience working with thousands of schools, most teachers are ready to resist. Like Joseph, whose words opened this chapter, they genuinely want to hold the line.  Unfortunately, the small minority enamored with AI currently has the loudest voice. ?ey dominate the conversation and insist that if children are struggling, the fault lies with us and our outdated thinking. ?ey claim we’re simply using ChatGPT incorrectly, all while holding up questionable stories and weak anecdotes as proof that this technology is both transformative and infallible.It feels safer to stay quiet—after all, who wants to rock the boat? But ifwe continue to let tech enthusiasts construct the narrative, there will soon be little left to ﬁght for.It’s time to ﬁnd our voice. It’s time to speak up.It’s time to disrupt the disruptors.OceanofPDF.comPART T HREEPushing Back“Do not wait to strike till the iron is hot; but make it hot by striking.”–W. B. YEATS, Ideas of Good and EvilOceanofPDF.com| 8 |What Can We Do?Parents and TeachersEvery day my daughter came home from school exhausted, then begged for her iPad because “everyone else gets to.” I ﬁnally stopped saying yes. We started small—no screens after dinner, then none on weekends. At ﬁrst it was awful, but within weeks I saw a di?erent child. She was talking to us again, drawing, making comics. It sounds cliché, but we got our daughter back. Now I’m that parent who sends in paper forms and asks for printed homework. I don’t think we realize how much these devices take from our kids until we take them back.–MARY, Mother of 12-Year-Old (United States)ary isn’t alone. ?ere’s no shortage of parents who see the problem clearly, yet few speak up. Why? A 1968 psychological study byLatane and Darley o?ers a clue.   For this study, college students were seated in a waiting room and asked to ﬁll out a brief questionnaire. As they worked, researchers quietly pumpedwhite, odorless stage smoke into the room through a wall vent. When students were alone, 75% took action—most left to report the problem. But when students were seated with groups of two or three strangers, only 12% responded. ?e majority simply sat in the smoke.   ?is experiment shows that when we face an ambiguous situation, it’s normal to look to others for cues on how to act. If those around us seem calm, we assume everything must be ﬁne. If others are concerned, we follow suit.  In his 2024 book ?e Anxious Generation, Jonathan Haidt uses this experiment to frame what’s happening with parents today. We see our children growing more distracted, restless, and disengaged, and we can’t shake the sense that something is wrong—yet we immediately second-guess ourselves. Are we just overreacting?Unsure what to think, we look to others. And what do we see? Schoolshanding out devices. Teachers assigning digital homework. Other parents giving smartphones to eight-year-olds. No one else seems alarmed.So we sit idly in the smoke.  But make no mistake: something real and dangerous is happening. We are losing our children to screens and watching as deep learning gives way to shallow thinking.  It’s time to speak up. And the more of us who do, the more we’ll normalize a new response—one rooted in responsibility rather than resignation.   For those ready to make a change, this chapter o?ers practical steps you can take—both in school and at home—to push back against the digital tide and restore healthy learning and development in our children’s lives.Parents: At School1) Form a CoalitionHere’s an interesting question: how do schools of ﬁsh change direction? When hundreds of animals are swimming in perfect synchrony, what causes the entire group to shift?   Researchers studying this phenomenon discovered something surprising. If only one or two ﬁsh break away, the rest keep going as usual. But once a small threshold changes direction—typically less than ﬁve percent of the group—the entire school will alter its course.   Schools can easily dismiss a lone parent raising concerns about EdTech. But when a small coalition begins moving together—speaking the same language, sharing the same concerns, advocating the same changes—the culture of the school starts to shift. It doesn’t take hundreds of families; a coordinated few can create real momentum.So, where do you begin?  Start by attending PTA meetings, school board sessions, or anywhere else technology decisions are being discussed. Chances are, you’ll likely hear similar concerns voiced by other parents.  From there, build connection. Create a WhatsApp or Facebook group for like-minded allies to share research, stories, and strategies. Trust and momentum grow when people can see—and speak—the same truth together.  To make it easier, you’ll ﬁnd a Coalition Invitation Letter at the end of this chapter.  Once you’ve formed a core group, work together to develop a Learning First Charter: a values-based guide that clearly states what you stand for,what you support (not just what you oppose), and what boundaries you expect the school to honor.?is charter might include: Guiding Principles: What core beliefs shape your approach? Consider statements like “We believe learning requires focus and e?ort,” or “We believe personal interaction matters more than screen time.” Supported Tools & Conditions: Which technologies are acceptable, and under what circumstances? For example, “Typing tools may be used for ﬁnal writing, but not for drafting,” or “Adaptive tutors may be used for practice, but not for introducing new concepts.”  Restrictions: Which devices, programs, or platforms should be removed or restricted? For instance, “No smartphones during the school day,” or “No gamiﬁed learning apps for academic subjects.” Expectations: What EdTech-related policies do you expect from your school? For instance, “Homework should be printable and accessible o?ine,” or “Screens use should be limited to a dedicated computer lab.”   For inspiration, you’ll ﬁnd a sample Learning First Charter at the end of this chapter. Once you’ve drafted your own, print it, share it, and let it serve as your anchor when negotiating with board members or administrators.  You might also consider developing a companion set of shared family norms that reinforce your groups values outside of school. For example: Delay Phones Until High School: Families agree to postpone smartphone ownership in order to preserve face-to-face interaction during formative years.Mandatory Social Time After School: Children regularly engage with in-person activities (sports, choir, scouts) to support connection, identity formation, and conﬁdence building.Structured Device-Free Socialization: Families host phone-free hangoutsso children can build real friendships without devices.  When families act in concert, no one stands alone. Any child worried about being left out for not having a phone will now have a group of friends walking the same path.  Fight as one, and little will change. But ﬁght together—even in small numbers—and others will follow.2) Ally with TeachersOver the last decade, I’ve had the privilege of working with hundreds of schools around the world. Based on that experience, I’ve come to an admittedly unscientiﬁc conclusion: 10% of teachers genuinely love EdTech, 20% are indi?erent, and 70% are quietly fed up with screens in schools.   Many parents see educators as part of the problem. But in truth, teachers may be our strongest allies in the ﬁght against digital overreach. ?ey are ready to push back—especially those who’ve spent more than a decade in the classroom and witnessed ﬁrsthand the steady decline in students’ ability to perform even basic academic tasks. But most feel trapped by administrative pressure and district mandates. When your livelihood is on the line, it’s safer to keep your head down.Start by sharing your Learning First Charter with teachers. A few maysco?, but many will nod along. ?ese conﬁdants can o?er valuable insight into how the system works—and how best to drive change. It’s likely that many will remain silent supporters at ﬁrst. But as your coalition gains traction, the silent majority will ﬁnd its voice.  Teachers rarely feel safe leading the charge. But if parents take the ﬁrst step, teachers will follow. And when families and teachers stand together, administrators have no choice but to take notice.3) Request Opt-In / Opt-Out?anks largely to the COVID pandemic, an estimated 88% of U.S. public school districts now provide laptops to students. One-to-one computing is no longer a futuristic dream; it’s the everyday reality for most of our kids.   ?at might be ﬁne if this trend actually improved learning. Unfortunately, as we saw in Chapter 2, the average e?ect size of one-to-one devices is just +0.12; far below the +0.40 threshold that deﬁnes meaningful educational beneﬁt.  So why do laptops persist in schools? Largely for bureaucratic convenience.  Personal devices make it easier for teachers to collect homework, mark assignments, and generate reports. ?ese are genuine advantages, but they raise a deeper question: what is the purpose of school? If the answer has anything to do with ‘learning’, then administrative e?ciency should never come at the cost of student development.If your school has adopted a one-to-one device program, express yourdesire to opt-out. To make this easier, you’ll ﬁnd a sample 1-to-1 Device Opt-Out Letter at the end of this chapter, ready to adapt for your own use.  One parent in Oregon used a similar letter to refuse a district-issued Chromebook in her home. Within a week, the school agreed to provide all relevant work on paper instead. One-to-one computing may have served a speciﬁc purpose during lockdowns, but it should never be compulsory in a physical classroom.Just a few years ago, many insisted that smartphone bans in school wereimpossible—yet today they’re becoming law around the world. ?e next step is clear: families deserve legally binding opt-out protections from academic screen use. No parent should be told their child can’t attend public schoolsimply because they refuse to sit them in front of a screen for six hours a day.  Public education should serve students, not screens— It’s time the law caught up.4) Demand Evidence, Not AnecdotesSchools often justify tech purchases by sharing compelling stories of success—usually provided by the vendors themselves. You might hear about how a class in Charlotte used iPads to clear trash from a river, or how students in Topeka used a coding app to rescue a struggling local business.Unfortunately, anecdotes aren’t evidence.  Whenever your school announces a new digital tool, request independently replicated research showing the tool improves learning—not engagement, not motivation, not time-on-task. If that data doesn’t exist, or comes only from studies funded or conducted by the developer, the tool isn’t ready for classrooms.Next, ask for internal data. Even if a tool works elsewhere, has it beentested in your school, with your students, under your teaching conditions? One high school in Pennsylvania I worked with discovered that a highly-rated math app actually lowered test scores when piloted locally—an outcome the vendors will surely never mention. Internal evidence takes time to gather (often a full school year), but no program should be scaled schoolwide until it ﬁrst proves e?ective in small, local trials.Finally, if no real data exists (as is the case with many emerging AItools), ask for a clear, evidence-based rationale that addresses three questions:What speciﬁc problem is this tool solving in our school? How will this tool improve learning, not just logistics?Why is this the best available solution?  If the rationale sounds anything like the myths from Chapter 1 (“Kids love choice”) or the apologies from Chapter 4 (“School isn’t the same as you remember”), it’s not good enough.   To make this easier, you’ll ﬁnd a sample Agenda Request Letter at the end of this chapter that you can bring to your school board or PTA.   Remember, you’re not asking for miracles—just evidence and reasoning. Any tool that belongs in a classroom should be able to withstand basic scrutiny. If it can’t, say no.5) Avoid “Digital Citizenship” CurriculaMany tech companies are selling “Digital Citizenship” programs to schools that promise to teach students how to use technology responsibly: how to avoid social media ﬁghts, spot AI fakery, and manage screen time with blockers.   ?e problem? Evidence shows these programs rarely change long-term behavior. Kids still bully online, still misuse digital information, and still spend far too much time on screens. And even if they did work, the platforms they’re built around—TikTok, WhatsApp, ChatGPT—may not even exist in a decade. Training students to navigate today’s digital landscape is like teaching them how to rent a VHS from Blockbuster in 1999.A better approach is to teach timeless human principles. Don’t focus on“cyberbullying prevention”—teach real-world conﬂict resolution. Don’t emphasize “digital etiquette”—emphasize face-to-face social etiquette. Don’t narrow the lens to “online ethics”—widen it to ethical reasoning as it’s been understood across centuries and cultures. Strong o?ine skills transfer easily to the digital realm, but the reverse is almost never true.   Our goal shouldn’t be to raise good “digital citizens.” It should be to raise good “citizens,” full stop. If students later extend those values online, great. But if we tether their morality to today’s apps, we leave them unprepared for tomorrow’s world.Parents: At Home6) Do a Tech AuditBuilding better habits at school often begins with building better habits at home. Unfortunately, we can’t ﬁx what we don’t see clearly—and when it comes to screen use at home, most of us are ﬂying blind.   To that end, consider performing a tech audit with your child. Over the course of a typical week, track how often they use devices and to what ends. Useful questions include:What tech is being used?For what purpose and for how long?Are they using the device alone or with others?  Additional notes: What emotions were involved? Did you have to intervene? Would you consider this time well spent?   Once complete, perform a similar audit on yourself. A key consideration here is not just how you use tech, but what message it might be sending to your child:What tech did I use?For what purpose and for how long?Did my child witness this use? What message might they have received fromit?Was this time well spent?To guide you through this process, you’ll ﬁnd At-Home Tech-Audit Formsat the end of this chapter—one for children, one for parents. Once you havea clear picture of screen-time patterns, you’ll be in a far stronger position to make purposeful, tailored changes—ones that reﬂect your family’s values, not merely your habits.7) Buy a PrinterIn nearly every school I work with, students ask me the same question: “What’s the smartest thing I can do to study better?” My answer, without hesitation, is always the same: “Buy a printer.”?is is one of the simplest ways parents can boost learning at home.  As we saw in Chapter 2, the medium matters: reading on paper leads to deeper comprehension and stronger memory than reading on a screen. Most modern textbooks are digital—but just because something arrives on a screen doesn’t mean it has to stay there. Print it out, and you transform passive skimming into active reading.?e same goes for writing. Students who complete work by handunderstand and retain more than those who type. Printing worksheets and encouraging handwritten responses at home helps build a habit that carries into the classroom. Now, when it comes time to take notes or annotate at school, handwriting will be their default – and that habit delivers a lasting cognitive edge.8) Implement Tech-Free Weekends?ere was a time when Sundays felt di?erent. Whether rooted in religious tradition or secular routine, the day o?ered a natural pause: stores closed, streets quieted, and the world seemed to slow down.   Today, Sunday is just another ‘screen-day’. Kids game on laptops, parents catch up on emails, and everyone retreats to separate rooms to stream a fewepisodes of their favorite show. ?e workweek has bled into the weekend, leaving little space to pause and reset.   But as we’ve seen throughout this book, real learning and growth doesn’t happen under constant stimulation. Creativity, critical thinking, and deep understanding emerge in quiet moments of reﬂection. ?is means downtime isn’t wasted time—it’s fuel for growth.  One of the simplest, most powerful changes any family can make is to reclaim the weekend with Tech-Free Sundays. ?is isn’t just about limiting your child’s screen time—it’s about the whole family logging o?. No phones, no laptops, no passive entertainment.   One family I know started this practice after realizing that ‘family time’ on Sundays usually meant four people in four rooms staring at four di?erent screens. ?ey admit their ﬁrst tech-free Sunday felt awkward, but by the third week they were playing cards, cooking together, and actually looking forward to the next weekend.If this sounds daunting, remember you don’t have to go all-in overnight.Start small—maybe one Sunday a month, or no tech before noon. You can also ease in with softer rules—maybe schoolwork is allowed, or a shared movie at day’s end. ?e goal isn’t perfection; it’s consistency. ?e point is to carve out regular time to disconnect from screens and reconnect with each other.To avoid this feeling like punishment, involve your children from thestart. Invite their input and plan your Sundays together. Maybe the family goes hiking, visits grandparents, or hosts a boardgame competition. Perhaps you try something new each week to build excitement and make the day feel special.   Over time, small rituals can become sacred. Tech-Free Sundays can grow into a weekly reminder that what matters most can’t be streamed.9) Ban AI From Your House (At Least for School Work)Over the past few years, one message has been echoed endlessly from the rooftops of Silicon Valley: artiﬁcial intelligence is the future of education. You can’t turn on the television or open a browser without being told that AI is revolutionizing learning and closing performance gaps.Don’t believe the hype.  As we explored in Chapter 6, AI is well-suited to expert production. When skilled adults use these tools, they can streamline workﬂow and enhance output. But AI is poorly suited to student learning. When novice children use these tools, they short-circuit e?ort and skip the thinking process entirely.?at’s why you may want to consider banning AI from your home—atleast when it comes to schoolwork. Real learning requires children to wrestle with ideas and gradually work their way toward understanding. By o?ering ready-made answers, AI interrupts that process and robs students of the very struggle intellectual growth demands.   Some argue that, if used correctly, AI can promote deep thinking. With the right prompts and carefully structured inputs, students could—at least in theory—use these tools to guide reﬂection and extend their learning.  Even if we set aside the vetting problem outlined in Chapter 6, this is just another version of the familiar ‘Tech Has So Much Potential’ apology. Yes, students could use AI thoughtfully—just as I could improve my ﬁtness by putting my car in neutral and pushing it to work every morning. But of course, I don’t—and the overwhelming majority of students won’t either.?e best safeguard is to make your expectations explicit from the start.Draw a clear line in your household:   No AI for Writing, Editing, or Annotating: Essays should be planned and drafted o?ine.   No AI for Problem-Solving: If your child gets stuck, encourage them to ask you, a teacher, or a peer for help.  No AI-Generated Study Material: Have your child make their own ﬂashcards, study guides, and summaries instead of outsourcing the process to a chatbot.  If your child complains that this makes school harder, remind them: that’s the point. ?e more e?ort they invest, the deeper and more adaptable their learning will become.10) Put It to the Test?roughout this book, we’ve explored how learning works and how digital technology gets in the way. But you don’t need to take my word for any of this—you can see the e?ects for yourself.   Treat your own home like a laboratory: pick one strategy and run a short trial. Maybe it’s a week of daily outdoor play after school, two weeks of printed-out homework, or one month of screen-free Sundays. To help your family choose the right collaborative experiment, you’ll ﬁnd a Talking with Kids About Tech guide at the end of this chapter. Whatever you choose, involve your children in the set-up, agree on the rules, and execute it together as a shared project.One mother I know tried a single change: no phones in bedrooms forone week. She expected pushback—and at ﬁrst, she got it. But by day four, her kids were talking more at dinner, sleeping longer, and waking up in better moods. By the end of the week, the rule stuck.Over the course of your own trial, you might track:  Schoolwork—Did it get easier or harder? Any changes in focus or performance?Mood and Focus—Was there more energy? Fewer arguments? Improvedsleep?Next Steps—Would you do this again? How could it work better?   ?e goal is to build awareness. When children observe their own minds in action, they begin to notice how habits shape what they think, how they feel, and how they connect. And when families experiment together, change stops being something done to them—it becomes something done with them.?ese aren’t acts of control, they’re acts of care.Children/Students1) Scrap the ScreenOne Year 10 student I worked with was struggling with algebra despite studying for hours every week—on his laptop. ?e issue wasn’t e?ort, but approach. ?e laptop made everything too easy: autoﬁll diagrams, copy/paste formulas, AI summaries. Because it all felt so e?ortless, he often drifted into gaming, scrolling, and streaming. So we made one simple change: he printed out his worksheets and completed them by hand. Within two months, his quiz scores jumped from barely passing to the top third of his class.?at’s the power of moving learning o?ine. Screens are brilliant forspeed, but they strip away the friction that makes learning truly stick. Reading on paper forces your brain to slow down and focus. Writing by hand deepens processing and recall. Solving problems without a calculator strengthens mental pathways you’ll need under pressure.At school, this might mean:• Taking notes in a notebook instead of a laptop.• Working math problems with pen and paper.• Using physical textbooks and manipulatives for science.• Speaking up during class discussions rather than typing in a chat box.At home, this might look like:• Printing readings instead of scrolling a screen.• Making physical ﬂashcards and study guides.• Handwriting lesson summaries from memory.• Walking to a friend’s house to work on a project together.  Will this take more time and e?ort? Absolutely. But e?ort isn’t the enemy of learning—it’s the secret ingredient. Once you’ve mastered a skill o?ine, you can easily apply it online. But if you only ever practice on screens, you’ll ﬁnd that you’re lost when the context changes—especially in high-pressure moments like exams or presentations.School is about more than ﬁnishing tasks. It’s about discovering how youlearn, how you think, and how you build lasting understanding. Screens may make the process easier, but easy rarely builds strength.   ?ink of it this way: if someone invented a machine that lifted weights for you, you wouldn’t expect to build muscle by using it. Learning works the same way. Your brain only develops when you do the heavy lifting yourself. Skip the shortcuts and train your mind to handle the real work.2) Choose Active Recall over Passive ReviewWhen I was a student, most of my study time was spent doing the same thing over and over again. I’d re-read my notes, re-listen to class recordings, re-highlight textbook chapters. My thinking was simple: if I just kept cramming the same information into my brain, eventually it would stick.   What I didn’t realize at the time is that memory doesn’t deepen through passive repetition—it strengthens only through active recall. ?e act ofpulling information out of your brain is what solidiﬁes knowledge and makes it easier to retrieve later.   ?is is why I can still remember details from the ﬁlm Toy Story even though I’ve only seen it once. I’ve talked about this movie so often with my nieces that each act of recall has reinforced my memory. By contrast, I’ve watched certain episodes of the cartoon Peppa Pig dozens of times with my daughter, yet I couldn’t meaningfully summarize a single one. ?ose memories have never been recalled—just absorbed passively—so they remain weak.In short: the secret to learning is recall. ?e problem is, most digital toolsdiscourage recall. Google, Wikipedia and AI make it easy to review information passively, but they rarely demand you mentally retrieve anything yourself.So if you want to study more e?ectively, move o?ine and embrace recall:• Rewrite notes from memory• Quiz yourself with physical ﬂashcards• Explain a concept to a parent• Debate a topic with a friend• Work through closed-book problems  But there’s a catch: every act of recall deepens a memory—but it also risks slightly distorting it. ?at’s why it’s vital to always pair recall with feedback. After rewriting your notes, compare them to the originals and correct any errors. After taking a practice test, review your mistakes and ﬁgure out why each one happened.Recall makes memories deep; feedback keeps memories accurate.Together, they make the most powerful learning loop we know.3) Set a Timer and Go All InYou sit down to study—and ten minutes later you’re watching YouTube, texting three friends, and doom scrolling Reddit.  Sound familiar? You’re not alone. More than two-thirds of students admit to multitasking during revision.   ?e problem is, multitasking is one of the worst things you can do for learning. As we saw in Chapter 3, research shows that students who multitask while studying consistently score lower on exams and earn lower grades than students who focus.   ?e best way to resist distraction at home is to add structure. Set a clear time block in advance and commit to staying o?ine and fully engaged with study during that period. ?is removes the burden from willpower and weakens the pull of digital distraction.   You may need to start small—maybe just 10 minutes—then take a short break. But over time, you’ll build stamina and be able to sustain 15-, 20-, even 30-minute blocks of deep, focused study.To make it easier:   Work O?ine: Screens are designed to encourage multitasking. Paper keeps you grounded.   Create a Study Zone: A consistent, tech-free space signals to your brain—this is where I focus.  Use a Physical Timer: Phones are too tempting. A kitchen timer or wristwatch keeps your study blocks on track without hijacking attention.  Several short study sprints without distraction are almost always more powerful than hours of multitasked study. Start training your attention now, and you won’t just improve your grades—you’ll develop a skill that pays o? for life.4) Do a Little Bit Every NightWhen it comes to studying, most students assume more is always better. If one hour is good, two must be great. And if two hours are great, three must be incredible.But that’s not how learning works.   ?ink about cramming. Chances are you’ve stayed up late in the past, trying to load your head with as much information as possible. ?e next day you probably did ﬁne on the exam, but two weeks later most of that knowledge was gone.   ?e reason is simple: your brain doesn’t store everything it learns right away. Rather, it waits until you sleep to really lock down new knowledge. Unfortunately, there’s a limit to how much information your brain can consolidate each night. If you cram too much in one sitting, a lot of that learning will simply vanish. But spread the same work across shorter sessions with sleep in between, and you give your brain multiple chances to strengthen and stabilize memory.?is is why, when it comes to study, frequency beats quantity. ?irtyminutes of revision each night for a week is far more e?ective than three hours all at once.  When students ﬁrst hear about short, o?ine study blocks, many sco?. How could 15 minutes lead to real learning? ?e truth is, one block per week won’t do much. But if you make this a nightly habit, it aligns perfectly with how the brain actually forms lasting memories.   In learning, consistency always beats duration. Study a little each night, and you’ll retain more, stress less, and walk into exams far more conﬁdent than if you’d crammed everything into a single session.5) What About Music?For many students, study time doesn’t begin until the headphones go on. Textbook open, playlist queued, volume set—now the work can commence. Adults often roll their eyes at this ritual, insisting that music during study is nothing but a distraction. But that’s not entirely true. Under certainconditions, music can actually sharpen focus and make studying easier.   ?e scientiﬁc idea behind this is called stochastic resonance. ?e term may sound intimidating, but the principle is simple: sometimes, adding a steady layer of background noise into a system can actually make that system work better. Engineers use this principles to stabilize machines, and neuroscientists have found that our brains respond in much the same way. A predictable stream of noise can nudge attention networks into a more stable rhythm—making it easier to stay focused.  Music, when used properly, can act as that noise. ?e key, however, is subtlety: the music must dissolve into the background, like faded wallpaper for the mind—present, but unnoticed. ?e moment you catch yourself humming along or drumming on your desk, it’s no longer noise; it’s competition. ?at’s when music shifts from ally to saboteur, and your learning will su?er.?is doesn’t mean you need bland or boring tracks. ?e best study musicis anything so familiar to you that your brain can predict every note. Personally, I keep a Pearl Jam album on repeat. It’s loud, abrasive, and full of lyrics—but I’ve heard it so many times it fades into the background, providing rhythm without distraction. When I shu?e through a newplaylist, though, every unfamiliar song hooks my attention and fractures my focus.  Here’s the test: if your playlist ends and you didn’t even realize it had begun, that’s stochastic resonance at work. But if you’re singing or tapping your toes, you’ve crossed into multitasking and the music is no longer helping.   It’s also worth noting that everyone’s threshold is di?erent. Some people thrive with background noise, while others need silence. And even for the same person, the e?ect can change depending on the task. For instance, I often use music when drafting emails, but need silence for editing videos or writing complex passages.?ere’s no universal formula here: just pay attention to yourself. ?e goalisn’t to ﬁll the silence—it’s to discover the conditions under which your mind works best, and use them to your advantage.So Now Then…In 1968, psychologists showed that people will sit quietly in a room ﬁlling with smoke—as long as no one else reacts. Today, we’re surrounded by digital smoke, yet the message continues to be “trust the tech.”  But now you know the truth: screens aren’t neutral. ?ey shape how our children learn, how our families connect, and how our culture evolves.  So will you keep sitting idly—or will you be the one to react? Will you be the signal that gives others permission to push back?  If you speak up, you may be labeled a nuisance or a problem parent. But ask yourself this: would you rather be called “di?cult,” or watch your child struggle to focus and learn?   You have a choice. You can shy away from action, or you can carry your troublemaker card with pride.I’ve made my decision.PARENT ACT ION TO OLKITLetters, Charters, and Checklists?e resources that follow are designed to help parents move from awareness to action. Start with the Coalition Action Menu as a quick touchpoint for planning your approach. From there, adapt the sample letters, charters, and checklists to help transform your ideas into real change.DRAFT COALITION ACTION MENUCoalition Action MenuEvery journey starts with a single step. Here are concrete ways parents can push back—from easy wins at home to big changes in school culture.Low Commitment (Easy Wins)Request Printable Homework: Ask teachers for paper versions of digital-only homework (and thank them when they provide it).Academic Tech Audit: Keep a 1-week screen diary recording your child’s daily school-related screen use at home.Non-Academic Tech Audit: Keep a 1-week screen diary of all non-academic screen use for both you and your child. Compare patterns and reﬂect.Ask Questions at School: Attend a PTA or board meeting and raise one speciﬁc, research-backed concern about academic tech use.Medium Commitment (Collective Action)• Start a Parent Group: Create a safe space to compare notes and share stories. Consider extending to parents beyond your immediate school community.• Print and Share: Distribute your Learning First Charter to three other families.• Request an Agenda Item: Formally request your school board or PTA to review screen use policies.• Partner with a Teacher: Approach a teacher you trust and ask if they’d support optional analog assignments—and let them know parents will back them.• Model Healthy Norms: Share how your family employs tech-free weekends or tech-free dinners with other families.High Commitment (System Change)• Opt-Out Together: Submit device opt-out letters as a group so no family feels singled out.• Coalition	Petition:	Collect	signatures	for	policies	such	as	“no smartphones during school hours.”• School Tech Philosophy: Work with teachers and leaders to create a values-based framework for your school.• Public Forum: Organize a community event or town hall with local experts on healthy learning.• Media Spotlight: Write a joint op-ed or letter to the editor to amplify parent voices in your community.DRAFT 1-TO-1 DEVICE OPT OUT LETTER[Date]__________[Student Name]	,[Date of Birth/Student I.D.] __________Re: Withdrawal of Consent for My Child’s Participation in EdTechProgramsDear [Principal / Administrator / Superintendent]I am writing to formally withdraw consent for my child, [Child’s Full Name], to participate in the use of school-issued digital devices and non-essential electronic programs, whether currently provided or adopted in the future.?is decision is based on well-documented concerns regarding:1. Academic Impact: Large-scale assessments (PISA, TIMSS, PIRLS) and academic research consistently show declines in learning outcomes with increased screen use for both in-school and at-home academic work.2. Cognitive Development: Analog practices (such as reading, notetaking, and studying on paper) reliably produce stronger comprehension and memory than digital methods.3. Mental and Physical Health: Excessive device use is linked to higher rates of distraction, anxiety, depression, and sleep disruption in children and adolescents.To be clear:• My child should not be required to use school-issued devices for classwork or homework.• All assignments should be provided in printable/o?ine formats upon request.• My child should not be penalized academically for working in analog formats.I recognize the school may be legally obligated to administer certain standardized assessments digitally. I will not oppose those instances. I also acknowledge that certain subjects (e.g. coding, robotics, design) may require computer use. I do not oppose those contexts, so long as digital work is performed on a school-mediated computer with a teacher present. However,in all other instructional contexts, I respectfully require my child to be exempted from compulsory EdTech use.Please provide written conﬁrmation that this opt-out request will behonored and that my child will be o?ered appropriate o?ine alternatives.  ?ank you for your understanding and cooperation in supporting my child’s learning, health, and wellbeing.Sincerely, [Signature][Printed Parent/Guardian Name]DRAFT AGENDA REQUEST LETTER(FOR SCHOOL BOARD / PTA / PRINCIPAL MEETINGS)Subject line: Request for Agenda Item – Review of School Digital Device PolicyDear [Board Chair/Principal],On behalf of a group of concerned parents, I respectfully request that the upcoming [School Board/PTA] meeting on [date] include an agenda item titled:“Review of Digital Device and Screen Use Policy.”Parents across our community are noticing the growing negative impact of school-based technology on our children’s learning and wellbeing. But research shows that when even a small group of parents speak up, schools listen. Change happens when we work together.  ?at’s why we’re forming a Parent Coalition for Healthy Learning. Instead, we want to partner with sta? and leaders to make sure school policies put learning and wellbeing ﬁrst.What we stand for:• Learning First: Technology should support real learning, not replace it.• Choice for Families: Parents should always have the right to opt-out of compulsory screen use.• Balance: O?ine practices (handwriting, printed homework, book reading) must remain central.• Healthy Norms: No child should feel left out simply because their family sets screen boundaries.Our ﬁrst steps:1. By speaking up together, we can protect focus, restore balance, and make sure our children’s education isn’t being outsourced to screens.We hope you’ll join us.Warmly,[Your Name] [Contact]DRAFT LEARNING FIRST CHARTERLearning First CharterOur children deserve an education that puts learning and wellbeing ﬁrst. Guiding Principles• We believe real learning requires focus, e?ort, and time—not shortcuts.• We believe human connection, conversation, and play matter more than screen time.• We believe technology should support genuine learning goals, never replace them.Supported Tools & Conditions• Typing tools may be used for ﬁnal drafts only: ideas should be planned and drafted o?ine.• Adaptive tutors may be used for practice and review, not for introducing new concepts.• Projectors or videos may be used to enrich, never to replace, live teaching or discussions.• Digital research tools may be used on school-run devices under teacher guidance, provided time is devoted to critical evaluation of digital output.Restrictions• Smartphones should not be permitted during the school day.• Gamiﬁed learning apps should not be used in core academic subjects.• No student should be required to use digital platforms when proven o?ine alternatives exist.Expectations for Schools• Homework must always be printable and accessible o?ine.• Non-essential screen use during school hours should be limited to no more than one hour per day (essential use, like mandatory state-wide exams, should not contribute to this limit).• Families retain the right to opt-out of device programs without penalty to their child’s education.• Teachers should have the autonomy to teach without being forced to rely on digital platforms.• Schools should work in partnership with parents and the wider community to prioritize health, wellbeing, and deep learning.By sharing this Charter, we commit to working with teachers and leaders to restore balance and protect our children’s ability to focus, learn deeply, and thrive.DRAFT AT-HOME TECH AUDIT FORMSType of TechFor What
