Start With Curiosity• “What’s your favorite part about using devices at school?”• “When do you feel screens help you learn? When do they make learning harder or distracting?”  • “How do you feel when you have to do lots of work on a screen?” (Goal: Listen before you lecture—kids need to know you care about their perspective.)2. Share What You Value• “I believe learning is easier when you can focus.”• “I want you to have time for reading, writing, and real conversations.”• “Screens can be useful, but I don’t want them to crowd out other important things.”(Goal: Frame it as family values, not just rules.)3. Problem-Solve Together• “What would make schoolwork feel calmer for you?”• “Would it help if homework was printed instead of online?”• “Would it help if you put your phone in another room while you study?• “Should we try a tech-free study block at home?”(Goal: Invite them into building the solution, so it feels like teamwork.)4. Acknowledge the Social Side• “Do you feel left out if other kids get more screen freedom?”  • “What do you wish teachers understood about how screens a?ect you?” (Goal: Name the pressures, so kids don’t feel alone.)5. Make a Family Agreement• “Together, let’s pick one small experiment to try this week…”° No devices after 8pm / Print assignments when possible / 30 minutes daily reading• Check in: “How did it feel?”OceanofPDF.com| 9 |What Can We Do?Teachers and EducatorsLast year I spent more time monitoring devices than actually teaching. So this year I ditched the tech. Oh my goodness—it’s been night and day. I thought the kids would resist, but they’ve been great. Many have even said they like this class better than their others because it feels calmer and less pressured. We talk, we laugh, and we LEARN!!! For the ﬁrst time in years, I feel like a teacher again.ALISON, Teacher Since 2011 (New Zealand)  n 1970, NASA’s Apollo 13 mission su?ered a catastrophic failure en route to the moon. An oxygen tank exploded, crippling the spacecraft. With limited power and failing systems, the astronauts’ survival depended entirely on ground control—engineers forced to work with whateverdamaged tools and spare parts were already onboard.   ?ere was no time to redesign the system, no chance to shuttle in new technology—no option but to think. Every move was improvised, but grounded in years of training and deep knowledge. Against all odds, theastronauts were safely brought home—not because of the tools, but because of the people using them.?at’s what teaching often feels like today.   ?e digital ecosystem we’ve been handed is ﬂawed, the devices are underdeveloped, and the tools more likely to harm than to help. But classrooms still have one thing that matters: a human expert at the center.  As teachers, we may not have the power to reject the system-wide tech we’ve been burdened with, but we absolutely can control how, when, and under what conditions it’s used (if we choose to use it at all). ?e classroom is our domain—when that door closes, we are the last line of defense against the slow erosion of real learning.Primar y MottoAny digital tool you choose to use must rest on a solid, independently veriﬁed base of e?ectiveness. ?is is non-negotiable. But e?cacy alone isn’t enough. Every time you turn on a screen, you should be able to answer three questions:   Is It Deliberate? You should know exactly why a device is in use and how it supports learning. Never use a tool without a clear purpose. Identify the cognitive process you’re targeting, the routine you’ll follow, and the outcome you’re aiming for.   Is It Time-Bound? You should know how long a device will be in use. Never turn on a screen without a ﬁrm limit. Whether it’s ten minutes for quick research or a three-hour weekly cap, set a timer and stick to it.   Is It Justiﬁed? You should know why a device is better than other options. Never use techsimply because it’s there.Digital tools should be reserved only for those things that are impossible, not merely inconvenient, to do o?ine. As a good rule of thumb: if you can do something live, then do it live.To help you assess and plan your digital application, you’ll ﬁnd a sampleTech-Prep Form at the end of this chapter. It’s a quick template you can use to decide whether a digital tool truly earns its place in your practice.  Your classroom is not a testing laboratory for unproven devices. Your responsibility is to your students—not to some distant company pitching the next big thing. ?is means digital tools must meet your standards, not the other way around.Stuck with Tech?Not every teacher can ditch their digital tools overnight. Many of us inherit systems we didn’t choose, with programs so deeply embedded into daily routines that they can’t be easily uprooted. So if you’re stuck with tech—at least for now—what principles can help you use it wisely while minimizing harm?1) Audit the ScreenMeaningful change begins with clarity. Before you can make better choices, you ﬁrst need to understand how you and your students are currently using digital tools. To this end, start by collecting some simple data. Over the course of a few weeks, use a short survey or tracking sheet to monitor all in-class tech use:What tools are being used?By whom?For what purpose? For how long?  To make this easier, you’ll ﬁnd a sample Tech-Audit Form at the end of this chapter. ?is will give you a clear snapshot of your digital environment so you can make informed, context-speciﬁc decisions about what to change, when to change it, and how best to proceed. For instance, one teacher I worked with discovered nearly 20% of student screen time in her class wentto administrative tasks (logging on, downloading programs, de-bugging platforms) rather than learning. ?at single audit shifted how she used devices for the rest of the year.  And remember: go slow. Tech reform is a journey. You needn’t ﬁx everything in a semester, or even a year. ?e goal is to gradually chip away at old habits and steadily reshape routines that better support learning.2) Recall, Don’t O?oadHere’s a quick experiment that will only work for readers of a certain age.Question 1: What was your childhood best friend’s phone number?  Chances are you rattled it o? without hesitation, even though you haven’t dialed those digits in decades.  Question 2: What is your current best friend’s (or closest colleague’s) phone number?  Chances are you have no idea. Sure, you know how to ﬁnd it on your phone—but the actual numbers? Nothing.   ?is reveals one of learning’s best-kept secrets: memory is constructive. Put simply, the more you actively recall something, the stronger that memory becomes.   ?at’s why we remember countless details from our favorite TV shows, even if we’ve only seen them once. Every time we talk about them, quote them, or debate them, we reinforce those memories through recall. ?at’s also why we remember almost nothing from last week’s Zoom meeting. Without follow-up, there’s no recall—and without recall, there’s no memory.Digital technologies are designed to o?oad information so users don’thave to recall it. To be clear, we still form memories when using these tools—but usually not of the information itself. Instead, we remember how to access it: which buttons to press, which menus to open, which apps to use.  Unfortunately, there’s a massive di?erence between knowing something and merely knowing where to ﬁnd it. ?ink back to our discussion of critical thinking and creativity in Chapter 6: which matters more—the knowledge or the shortcut?   If we’re stuck with digital tools, then we have to ﬁnd ways to use them in a manner that reinforces recall rather than bypasses it.   Turn o? multiple-choice quizzes. Use open-ended questions that require students to retrieve information from memory.   Turn o? simple search, scan, and summary functions. Have students read full texts and process ideas through follow-up discussions.   Turn o? auto-correct. Have students apply grammar rules from memory when editing their work.  If we want students to retain and apply what they’ve learned, we must shift tech use away from easy access and toward e?ortful recall.3) Stretch the ContextHuman declarative memory comes in two distinct forms: Type 1: Episodic Memories.   ?ese are facts or ideas tied to a speciﬁc time and place. For example, I remember accidentally popping my daughter’s Peppa Pig balloon on her second birthday.Type 2: Semantic Memories.   ?ese are facts or ideas independent of any particular time and place. For example, I know the word birthday refers to the anniversary of a person’s birth.Why does this distinction matter?   In a recent study, researchers assessed the creative abilities of two groups of patients using well-established measures of general creativity. One group had frontotemporal dementia, which impairs sustained, focused thought. ?e other had semantic dementia, which erodes general knowledge but leaves personal memories intact?e results were striking. ?e frontotemporal group showed creativeperformance scores nearly 45% lower than healthy controls. But in the semantic group, scores were nearly 80% lower. Despite having ample time, structure, and support, patients without access to semantic memories struggled to generate new ideas. Instead, they recycled fragments of past personal experiences over and over again.?is tells us something critical: semantic memories are our useableknowledge. ?ey allow us to move beyond our own lived experiences and apply ideas in new ways across di?erent situations.  So where do semantic memories come from? ?ey grow out of episodic memories.  Each time we learn something new, that information is tied to the speciﬁc context in which it was encountered—that’s our ﬁrst episodic memory. As we encounter the same idea in di?erent settings, we form additional episodic memories. Over time, our brain will compare these episodes, identify what’s consistent, and extract any common thread. ?at extraction process—of distilling the consistent features from varied episodic memories—is how semantic memories are born.Imagine a child practices math in three di?erent locations: a classroom, alibrary, and at home. Each session creates a distinct episodic memory—but because the only consistent element is the math itself, that’s what the brain extracts into a semantic memory. “?is is a math equation.”   Now imagine that same child practices math three times, but always in the same location: her classroom. Each session still creates an episodic memory, but because everything stays the same, everything will be extracted. “?is is a math equation that belongs on a blackboard in a gray-walled classroom.”   Put simply, the context of our episodic memories ultimately determines the content of our semantic memories.   ?is is where digital technology becomes a problem. Screens impose an extremely narrow context. When all learning happens on a device, that device becomes part of every episodic memory—and eventually, every semantic one. Over time, the screen itself begins to dictate how, when, and even if students can access learning later. In other words, digital uniformity limits the development of useable knowledge.Sowhat can teachers do?   Alternate O?ine Formats: If a concept is ﬁrst introduced digitally, revisit it later in a variety of o?ine settings. Class discussions, physical practice,and hands-on activities can help decouple knowledge from any single tool or environment.  Encourage Screen Breaks: If o?ine teaching isn’t an option (such as in remote learning), build in tasks that require students to step away from the device. Have them complete printed readings, handwrite problem sets, or summarize lessons on paper before returning to the device to share and discuss.Vary the Digital Context: If all learning must remain online, diversify theformat. Present the same concept through videos, PDFs, digital games, typed discussions, and interactive tools. Even small changes in program or layout can reduce over-contextualization and promote more ﬂexible knowledge.  Our goal is to break the tight bond between content and device so that students can carry what they’ve learned beyond the screen.4) Find the Story Behind the FactsHere’s something fun to try with your students. At the start of class, display a list of 15 random words—things like motorcycle, glasses, and lizard. Give students 60 seconds to memorize as many as they can.  But here’s the twist: ask half the class to spend those 60 seconds visualizing each word in detail (imagine the shape, texture, and weight of a pair of glasses). Ask the other half to weave the words into a short story (the glasses-wearing lizard’s eyesight was so bad, he failed his motorcycle license exam).At the end of class, ask everyone to write down as many words as theycan remember.  Who do you think will perform better? Many assume it’ll be the visualization group—after all, images are incredibly powerful. But nearlyevery time, the narrative group wins. ?e reason for this is simple: human beings think in stories.   Facts only become meaningful once they are connected to other facts in a coherent, cause-and-e?ect chain. As cognitive psychologist Steven Pinker puts it, “Disconnected [memories] in the mind are like unlinked pages on the web: they might as well not exist.”  In other words, narrative is the brain’s default mode for understanding the world. Because of this, whenever information is presented in the form of a story, it becomes much easier to make sense of, remember, and apply later. As discussed in Chapter 7, digital technologies largely kill sustained narrative. ?ey ﬂood us with disconnected, context-free fragments—swipe after swipe, scroll after scroll—with no coherent thread linking one idea to the next. ?is consistent stream of randomness doesn’t just weaken memory (which is why most of us remember almost nothing after an hour on YouTube), it also disrupts comprehension, deep thinking, and the ability tobuild useable knowledge.  EdTech has largely failed to account for this issue. Many emerging classroom tools actively embrace short-form videos, rapid-ﬁre content sprints, and micro-learning bursts. Developers assume that since this is how people consume tech at home, it must be how they learn best at school. But nothing could be further from the narrative-driven truth of human cognition.So when digital tools are in play, how can we restore story?   Begin With a Frame: Open class with a compelling o?ine story that gives students a meaningful context for what they’re about to encounter online.  Connect to Lived Experience: Invite students to share personal stories related to digital content, helping them pull ideas o? the screen and organize them through their own lives.   Turn Fragments into Flow: Have students create a hard-copy concept map or ﬂow chart based on the information they’ve gathered online, turning scattered pieces into structured understanding.   If the brain thinks in stories, and digital tools strip stories away, then our job is to help students reestablish the narrative. Without it, they’ll almost certainly forget the facts.Further Classroom Considerations5) O?er Analog AlternativesDue to administrative pressure, teachers may not be able to reduce their own tech use as quickly as they’d like. But there’s no policy dictating students must always use a screen. In fact, mandating digital engagement can raise equity issues for students with limited access or poor connectivity at home. So whenever possible, o?er analog alternatives: print out articles, have students handwrite assignments, and use pen and paper to solve practice problems.O?ine tools consistently foster deeper processing, sharper focus, andstronger memory. And when students who work on paper begin outperforming their digital peers, others take notice. With a bit of luck, peer inﬂuence kicks in, and screen-free work begins to spread organically throughout the classroom.   One English teacher I worked with asked one group to draft their essays on computers and another to do all their work by hand. Within two weeks, it was clear the handwritten papers were sharper, and the revisions more thoughtful. Seeing the di?erence, several of the digital-ﬁrst kids jumped ship and swapped to notebooks for the rest of the year. ?at teacher never returned to laptops in class.  Going o?ine pays dividends for teachers too. While creating analog materials may take longer than clicking ‘assign’ on a digital platform, that extra e?ort almost always produces more intentional, better-targeted resources. Many teachers discover that the more they invest in analog design, the more they and their students come to prefer the results.Screens may sometimes be required, but they don’t need to be thedefault. Keep pencils sharpened, printers stocked, and notebooks close at hand. For practical swaps you can make right away, you’ll ﬁnd a list of Lesson Alternatives and Analog Examples at the end of this chapter. And if you’re ready to push for structural change beyond your own classroom, you’ll also ﬁnd a draft Teacher Coalition Letter meant to help teachers pools their voices and advocate for analog practices across their school.Sometimes the oldest tools are also the most powerful.6) Embrace Handwritten NotesNotetaking isn’t just about recording words—it’s about thinking through them. When done correctly, it helps students wrestle with new information, organize ideas, and connect them into coherence. As we saw in Chapter 2, pen and paper encourage the kind of notetaking that promotes this deep learning and comprehension. Accordingly, whenever possible, prioritize handwritten notes in your classroom.But there’s a wrinkle: taking notes while listening to someone speak isharder than it seems. Most students subvocalize as they write—silently ‘saying’ each word to themselves as they scribe it on the page. Unfortunately, the brain processes this inner speech in the same way it processes an external voice. Just as we can’t follow two people talking at once, many students struggle to take meaningful notes while simultaneously attending to a teacher’s voice.  To address this, it’s worth rethinking structure. ?at might mean teaching for ten minutes, then giving students two minutes of silent notetaking time. It might mean pausing mid-class for students to take notes in pairs. Or it might mean waiting until the end of class to co-construct a shared set of group notes. If you’d like a structured format designed to support recall and review, you’ll ﬁnd a Handwritten Notetaking Form at the end of this chapter.Notetaking isn’t passive transcription—it’s an active cognitive task thatdeserves the same time, structure, and intention as any other classroom activity.7) Let Students See Real ProgressEdTech loves to simulate progress with badges, leaderboards, and digital rewards. However, while these may motivate some students (certainly not those stuck at the bottom), they deliver limited useful insight into performance.  In order for feedback to actually shape learning, it must be speciﬁc, personal, and actionable. ?ink about those digital roadside speed signs that ﬂash how fast you’re driving. If one lit up with “?ree Gold Stars,” you might smile—but you’d have no idea how fast you were going or whether you needed to adjust your speed. But when it says, “You’re Going: 45mph – Slow Down!” that’s feedback you can use. It gives you real information and a clear indication of what to do next.We should aim to supplement digital rewards with e?ective analogfeedback systems in our classrooms. Self-quizzes, learning journals, progress logs, and rubrics can all help students track their learning in concrete, meaningful ways. ?ese tools make progress visible, strengthen memorythrough active recall, and build a learning narrative through guided reﬂection.   Let students see their growth in real terms—not merely their rank on a leaderboard.8) Designate One Tech-Free Period per WeekWhen starting a new ﬁtness regimen, beginners are often encouraged to start small—typically two days a week. ?e aim is to inspire change without overwhelming. Once they start seeing results, two days becomes three, three becomes four, and soon a new habit takes hold.  We can take the same approach in the classroom by setting aside just one tech-free period each week. ?at small shift creates space for deeper, more focused lessons and gives students a chance to feel the analog di?erence. To help you track the impact on both students and teachers, you’ll ﬁnd several Tech-Free Reﬂection Checklists at the end of this chapter.Over time, you might add more days—or you may ﬁnd you don’t needto. Once students experience the beneﬁts, some will begin reducing tech use on their own. Small changes can create big momentum. Start with one class, and let the results do the convincing.9) Create Space for Slow ?inkingDigital technologies thrive on speed—the endless stream of tweets, images, and videos designed to keep us moving without pause. While this rapid pace boosts proﬁts in the attention economy, it wreaks havoc in the classroom.   As we saw in Chapter 6, our deepest insights typically emerge only after we step away from a problem and allow the brain to enter a calm, reﬂectivestate known as the default mode. But this state cannot surface unless we slow down—a nearly impossible task with a device nearby.  So can we explicitly build moments of stillness into the school day? Maybe we open class with ﬁve minutes of silent reﬂection. Maybe we o?er a ten-minute quiet interval between periods. Maybe we extend creative task deadlines to allow students overnight time for consolidation and insight.  EdTech is the hare: fast and ﬂashy, but quick to fatigue. Higher-order thinking is the tortoise: slow and steady, but ultimately more enduring. If we want the latter to win the learning race, we must limit unfettered access to the former.10) Avoid Artiﬁcial IntelligenceAI is a tool for expert o?oading, not novice learning. When students use it, they bypass memory, suppress creativity, and short-circuit personal identity formation. Worse still, its presence in education signals to the world that schools no longer believe they can fulﬁll one of their primary purposes: to organize information in ways that brings coherence and meaning.Put simply, AI is not a friend of schools.  Still, many teachers feel tempted to employ it behind the scenes. ?ey may forbid students from using AI, yet rely on it themselves to build assessments, generate feedback, and design lessons—usually in the name of saving time. But using AI in this manner undermines the very work it’s supposed to support.Feedback, assessment, and planning aren’t just for students—they’re alsofor us. ?ey reveal how our students are thinking, where our teaching needs to adapt, and what we must do to help everyone move forward.  Imagine you personally create a short quiz and half the class struggles with a concept you thought they’d mastered. ?at’s not a grading burden—it’s a red ﬂag for tomorrow’s plan. Maybe you need to reteach an idea, slow the pace, or ﬁnd a clearer example. But if AI writes and marks the quiz, there’s no guarantee the necessary questions needed to assess understanding will be asked or that the feedback will be anything more than a few numbers on a screen.Lesson planning works the same way. Drafting by hand forces us tothink through the ﬂow of a class, anticipate where students might struggle, and connect tomorrow’s teaching with yesterday’s performance. If we let AI spit out a generic plan, we skip that essential thinking—and our students pay the price.  O?oading to AI may save time, but it severs our connection with the core work of teaching. ?e less we engage and the less we understand our students, the less we can support their growth. By all means, use AI to format permission slips or draft parent emails. But the moment it stands between us and our craft, we surrender our expertise and deprofessionalize the ﬁeld—handing ammunition to those eager to see teachers replaced by screens.Whenever people ask me if AI can replace teachers, I always give thesame answer: only if teachers let it happen. Every time we use AI to mediate between us and our students, we are letting it happen.Beyond the Classroom11) Reconsider Homework PracticesFor both learning and equity reasons, all assigned homework should be o?ine. ?at is the only way to guarantee every student can access and beneﬁt from the material. Explaining this low-tech shift to families can be tricky, so to support those conversations you’ll ﬁnd a Draft Parent Letter at the end of this chapter.  Of course, when students are handed paper worksheets, many will roll their eyes—which is why analog homework must be short and sharp to sustain engagement. Fortunately, research shows that’s exactly the kind of homework that works best.   SHORT: ?ere is a clear ceiling on how much homework is useful. ?e relationship between homework time and learning impact follows an inverted-U curve: the biggest gains come in the ﬁrst 60-90 minutes of work per night, after which performance plateaus and may even decline. In practical terms, that translates to about 10-15 minutes of homework per class, per evening.   SHARP: Practice-based assignments consistently outperform discovery-based ones. When students review content they’ve already encountered, or practice skills they’re actively developing, homework supports learning. But when they’re sent home to watch a video lecture, complete an online lab, ortackle new concepts alone, it often backﬁres—largely because fewer than 30% of students actually follow through.   Perhaps the most robust ﬁnding in homework research is that frequency matters more than quantity. Ten minutes of focused practice every night produces far greater beneﬁts than a single two-hour session once a week.?e goal is rhythm, not overload: keep homework short, sharp, and consistent—and the gains will follow.  As a ﬁnal note, if your students vary widely in skill or conﬁdence, consider pairing problems with worked examples. In this format, every other question is already solved, complete with a step-by-step explanation. Stronger students can skip these, but those still building ﬂuency can use them as models.  Some worry this approach o?ers too much sca?olding—but that’s precisely what many students need when working at home without a teacher present. Hundreds of studies have shown that worked examples are powerful learning aids. In one experiment, students who completed ﬁve problems paired with ﬁve worked examples scored 35% higher on a follow-up test one day later than students who completed ten unsolved problems alone.One third more learning for half the e?ort. Not bad.12) Foundations over ToolsIn 2016, an augmented reality game called Pokémon GO was released to an unprepared public. Using their smartphones, players roamed real-world locations in search of virtual creatures projected onto the environment through their screens. Within four months, over 230 million people were playing each month.Almost immediately, educators were urged to bring this game into theirclassrooms. ?at year alone, I attended no fewer than ﬁve conferences featuring sessions on “Pokémon GO for Teachers.” Needless to say, those sessions aged poorly. Within ﬁve years, the game’s user base had collapsed by nearly 70%, and those who still played were overwhelmingly adults—no longer school-aged.?e same cycle repeats every year. Professional development programsspring up around the “tool of the moment”: how to harness AI, how to master Google Classroom, how to plug into the latest app. But tools quickly come and go, and the training tied to them becomes obsolete just as fast.   Rather than focusing on new programs or devices, teacher professional development would be better served if it focused on the foundations of learning: How does memory work? How does attention ﬂuctuate? How is deep understanding formed? As this book has shown, when you understand why learning happens, you’re far better equipped to evaluate what works—no matter which tool may come along.When students are trained on tools, their skills become locked to thatspeciﬁc platform. When teachers are trained on tools, their pedagogybecomes similarly locked. Tethering teachers to devices may serve developers, but it does little to serve students.So Now Then…When the Nazis established the Warsaw Ghetto in 1940, they banned all formal education for Jews. Schools were closed and children were forbidden from studying beyond the barest vocational level.  In response, Jewish families organized a vast network of underground schools. Amid starvation, surveillance, and the constant threat of death, educators in the Warsaw Ghetto kept learning alive. Without permission, without funding, and without supplies—they continued to teach.   ?is incredible story reveals something simple yet profound: the only essential ingredients for learning are a curious child and a willing teacher. Everything else is just decoration.  Today, teachers are constantly told they’re failing—that to be e?ective they must embrace devices, and to stay relevant they must learn to love screens. But nothing could be further from the truth.  What teachers need is what they’ve always had: an understanding of children, an understanding of learning, and a commitment to show up every day and teach.   Let’s stop trying to replace them. Let’s give teachers back the authority, trust, and freedom to put our kids ﬁrst.T EACHER ACT ION TO OLKITPractical Steps and Templates?e resources that follow are designed to help teachers take concrete steps toward reducing unnecessary tech use. ?ink of this section as a starting kit: you don’t have to use everything at once (or at all), but you can refer back to and adapt these tools as your EdTech journey unfolds.1. Presentations  LESSON ALTERNATIVES TEACHING WITHOUT TECH• Instead of: Students making PowerPoint slides or Prezi presentations. | Try: Poster boards, storyboards, or oral presentations with hand-drawn visuals.• Why: Prioritizes substance over design, builds speaking conﬁdence, and strengthens recall through drawing/writing.2. Research Projects• Instead of: Endless Googling. | Try: Curated print packets, encyclopedia sets, or assigning roles to small groups who pool their ﬁndings.• Why: Reduces distraction, teaches students to work with limited but reliable sources, and models the value of depth over breadth.3. Notetaking• Instead of: Typing notes in Docs or OneNote. | Try: Structured handwritten notes (Cornell method, mind maps, or sketch notes).• Why: Handwriting deepens encoding and encourages synthesis rather than transcription.4. Practice & Review• Instead of: Gamiﬁed quiz apps. | Try: Paper ﬂashcards, retrieval games (e.g., “Brain Dump,” “Teach It Back”), or team-based review on the whiteboard.• Why: Builds retrieval strength without distraction and keeps focus on knowledge over ‘scores’.5. Writing• Instead of: Drafting everything on a laptop. | Try: Handwritten ﬁrst drafts, peer editing on paper, then typing only for the polished ﬁnal version.• Why: Supports idea generation, avoids premature editing, and balances analog/digital skills.6. Collaboration• Instead of: Online discussion boards or shared docs. | Try: Fishbowl discussions, rotating peer groups, or physical “idea walls” with sticky notes.• Why: Builds real-time listening, empathy, and negotiation skills.7. Assessment• Instead of: Online quizzes. | Try: Paper-based short-answer or essay questions, oral exams, or portfolio reviews.• Why: Encourages depth of knowledge and allows teachers to assess true understanding rather than test-taking tricks.ANALOG EXAMPLESRetrieval Practice: Brain Dump• How: At the end of a lesson, students open a fresh page in their notebook and spend three minutes writing everything they can recall from that day’s lesson. ?ey then pair up to compare and add missing ideas, followed by a teacher-led whole-class debrief to surface and clarify key points.• Why It Beats Tech: Unlike Kahoot or Quizlet, which reward recognition, brain dumps demand free recall—a process that produces far strongerlong-term learning as knowledge must be actively reconstructed. Peer-sharing and teacher debriefs ensure recall is broad and remains accurate.• Variations: For ELEMENTARY students, keep recall brief (1-2min) using drawings or single words. For SECONDARY students, extend to ‘brain storm maps’ where students sketch links between recalled concepts. As an UPGRADE, run ‘progressive dumps’ across a unit so students can see the growing narrative and revisit earlier material.Collaboration: One-Stay, Others-Stray• How: Divide the class into small groups. Each group creates a poster or graphic organizer that explains one key concept. One student remains with the poster to present and answer questions, while the others rotate between groups to learn from di?erent presenters. Continue until all students have both explained and listened.• Why It Beats Tech: Google Docs and online boards often reducecollaboration to parallel typing with minimal dialogue. ?is routine requires verbal explanation, questioning, and negotiation in real-time. Teaching peers activates the protégé e?ect, deepening the explainer’sunderstanding, while the rotation fosters empathy and active listening—skills digital tools rarely develop.• Variations: For ELEMENTARY students, use drawings, manipulatives, or simple diagrams to support explanations. For SECONDARY students, require each group to pose at least one discussion question for visiting peers. As an UPGRADE, have all groups tackle the same topic rather than di?erent ones. Comparing perspectives highlights multiple ways to interpret content.Reviewing Content: Team Whiteboard Challenge• How: Split the class into small groups. Give each group a mini-whiteboard (or scrap paper). Pose a question or problem for all groups to solve. After the set time, groups simultaneously reveal their answers, allowing comparison and discussion.• Why It Beats Tech: Online review games often emphasize graphics, points, or badges which distract from content. ?e whiteboard challenge keeps attention squarely on knowledge generation, as students must produce rather than select an answer. ?e low-stakes competition element maintains engagement without diluting rigor.• Variations: For ELEMENTARY students, use visuals (pictures,numbers, drawing) or manipulatives. For SECONDARY students, pose multi-step problems (math proofs, essay outlines, case analyses) that demand sustained reasoning. As an UPGRADE, add a ‘defense’ round where teams must justify their answers, further strengthening reasoning and explanatory skills.HANDWRITTEN NOTETAKING FORM?is notetaking form is designed for deep comprehension and long-term retention.1. During Class: Students take handwritten ‘deep notes,’ capturing key ideas in their own words. ?ese can be completed either during brief note-taking breaks or after each lesson segment.2. After 24 Hours: From memory, students spend a few minutes writing key ideas, themes, or questions in the margin. After attempting recall, they should review their original deep notes and amend or expand as needed.3. After 48 Hours: Again from memory, students write a short summary at the bottom of the page. Afterwards, they can compare to their deep notes and reﬁne as needed.Why It Works: ?is approach combines four of the most powerful principles in learning science—handwriting (stronger encoding than typing), free recall (more potent than recognition), distributed practice (better than single exposure or cramming), and feedback (checking memory against notes to maintain accuracy). Together, these ensure each page of notetaking has a built-in study routine.CLASSROOM TECH FORMSTECH PREP FORMWill I UseTech?What SpecificTech?For WhatPurpose?For HowLong?Why NotAnalog?MONDAYPERIOD 1PERIOD 2PERIOD 3PERIOD 4PERIOD 5TUESDAYPERIOD 1PERIOD 2PERIOD 3PERIOD 4PERIOD 5WEDNESDAYPERIOD 1TECH AUDIT FORMDid lEmploy Tech?What type/ for whatpurpose?For how long(inmin)?Did mystudentsWhat type/ for whatpurpose?For how long (inmin)?employ tech?MONDAYPERIOD 1PERIOD 2PERIOD 3PERIOD 4PERIOD 5TUESDAYPERIOD 1PERIOD 2PERIOD 3PERIOD 4PERIOD 5WEDNESDAYPERIOD 1Purpose:DRAFT CLASSROOM PHONE POLICY?is policy is not about punishment—it’s about protecting learning. Research shows that phones (even when turned o? but nearby) reduce attention, memory, and performance. In this classroom, we prioritize focus, relationships, and genuine learning.?e Rules1. Phones O? and Away: Before the start of class (in lockers or outside of classroom)2. No ‘Silent Use’: Checking the time or ‘just a quick look’ still breaks focus.3. Medical / Health Needs: Talk with me privately and we’ll make a plan together.Why ?is Matters• Research shows students learn 20-30% less when their phone is visible or available.• Class feels calmer, more respectful, and more human.• Protects every student’s right to focus without distraction.Consequences (Clear & Consistent)• First Time O?ense: Phone placed in teacher’s secure storage until end of class.• Second Time O?ense: Phone placed in teacher’s secure storage until end of school day.• Repeated Issues: Parent contact and referral to school administration.A Note to ParentsWe ask for your partnership in this policy. Please avoid calling/texting yourchild during school hours. Important messages can (and should) be relayed through the school o?ce.Student AcknowledgmentI understand and agree to follow the classroom phone policy.
