year you may notice that our classroom looks a little di?erent: fewerscreens, more books, more pens, and more direct interaction. I want to explain why. Over the past decade, schools everywhere have adopted more and more digital tools. While some can be useful, the evidence is now clear: more screen time in school does not automatically mean more learning. In fact, many studies (from international testing agencies to cognitive science labs) show that too much reliance on screens can reduce focus, weaken comprehension, and replace deeper skills with quick clicks and shallow responses.Here’s what we know about how children learn best:• Attention Matters: Screens, by design, divide focus. Real concentration—the kind needed for problem-solving, writing, or deep reading—grows when students practice working without constant digital interruption.• Memory Matters: Taking notes by hand, drawing diagrams, and reading printed text all strengthen retention in ways digital formats often do not.• Relationships Matter: Students learn not just from content, but from teachers and peers. Face-to-face discussion builds empathy, listening skills, and the ability to think critically together.With this in mind, my approach will be simple:• Screens Will Be Limited and Purposeful: Devices may be used forspeciﬁc tasks (like ﬁnal drafts or occasional research), but will never be the default.• O?ine Skills Will Be Prioritized: Handwriting, printed readings, and real conversations will be at the center of our work.• Balance Will Be Modeled: Students will see that technology can be a learning tool—but not the only learning tool (and rarely the best one).I want you to know this is not about rejecting the future or making life harder. It is about giving your child the best chance to develop the habits of mind that truly lead to long-term success: focus, persistence, memory, and human connection.  I welcome your questions, concerns, or suggestions. Together, we can help your children thrive not just in this classroom, but in life.Warmly, [Your Name][Your Contact Information]DRAFT TEACHER COALITION LETTERTo: [Principal/School Board]From: Concerned Teachers at [School Name] Subject: Ensuring Technology Serves LearningDear [Principal/Board Members],As teachers committed to the growth and wellbeing of our students, we are writing together to share our concerns about the current use of technology in our classrooms. While digital tools can serve a role in modern education, our daily experience has shown that mandatory, unrestricted, or poorly structured use often undermines focus, deep learning, and healthy classroom relationships.Every day we see:• Students unable to sustain attention long enough to grasp new ideas.• Class discussions replaced by silent scrolling.• Work	completed	on	screens	but	with	little	evidence	of	deep understanding.?ese are not isolated frustrations, but recurring patterns conﬁrmed by international research and echoed by parents in our community.We believe:• Learning requires sustained attention, discussion, and practice.• Human interaction and teacher guidance are irreplaceable.• Technology should support, not substitute, the core processes of teaching and learning.We envision classrooms where students read deeply, write thoughtfully, and engage in open discussion. Technology has a place, but it should nevereclipse the essential human work of education.We respectfully request the following steps:1. Establish clear limits on personal devices (e.g., phones away during lessons).2. Prioritize analog options alongside digital assignments (e.g., printable homework).3. Support teacher autonomy by allowing us to choose when and how technology is used in our classrooms.4. Engage in open dialogue with teachers before adopting new platforms or programs.As a starting point, we ask to meet with you this month to discuss piloting [a phone-free week] and reviewing the results together. We are united in our commitment to help every student succeed. Together, we can create a balanced approach that reﬂects evidence-based practice, equity, and the values of our community.Sincerely,[Names of teachers / Coalition group]OceanofPDF.com| 10 |What Can We Do?Schools and LeadersTwo terms ago we instituted a full phone ban during the school day. Within weeks, the change was undeniable: students were more focused, behavioural incidents dropped dramatically, and children were once again playing at recess. I only wish we had done it sooner. Of all the interventions I can recall, this has had the greatest impact—and it required no new resources. ?e only resistance has come from parents. Students adapted almost immediately, but some parents still worry about being able to reach their children. In time, I hope they’ll come to trust that our priority is the wellbeing of their kids.–HAROLD, Head of School since 2015 (Australia)yms exist to build physical strength through hard work and struggle. Imagine if a gym invested in machines that did the work for you:treadmills that ran on their own while you ﬂipped through a magazine; weights that lifted themselves while you sipped a smoothie. No one wouldmistake that for exercise, and no one would expect to grow stronger or healthier. ?e mismatch between tool and purpose is obvious.  Schools exist to build cognitive strength through hard work and struggle.   Imagine if a school invested in machines that did the work for students: programs that wrote essays while they scrolled social media; devices that solved equations while they took selﬁes. No one would mistake that for learning—yet this reﬂects much of modern education.  If we want to reclaim our schools for learning, then we must reclaim e?ort and rigor. Any tool that eliminates the need to think does not belong in education.Two Organizing PrinciplesBefore leaders act, two foundational guidelines are worth considering:Start YoungOlder students have spent their entire school lives immersed in digital technology. Removing these tools abruptly can provoke resistance, especially if essential academic habits like handwriting, sustained attention, and patience have never fully been developed. Younger students, on the other hand, have yet to form a deep reliance on devices. ?is makes the primary years the ideal entry point for reform. Begin there, and your digital strategy can grow alongside them. As these students progress into middle and upper grades, policy can scale accordingly—eventually evolving into a coherent school-wide (or even district-wide) approach.Start SmallSweeping educational reforms rarely go smoothly. Any abrupt shift to the status quo risks confusion and deﬁance—especially when the rationale andexpected outcomes are unclear. Rather than rushing in headﬁrst, try adopting a phased, iterative approach. Small, manageable steps allow leaders to assess impact, reﬁne strategies, and build lasting support among sta? and families.   With these principles in mind, schools can begin a deliberate journey of digital reform. What follows is one possible three-phase system. It does not prescribe which technologies should stay or go—rather, it o?ers a framework that each school can adapt to its own context, goals, and values.Phase 1: Stabilize and AssessWhenever I teach students about time management, we begin by performing a time audit. ?e logic is simple: if you want to make better use of your time, you ﬁrst need a clear picture of how you’re currently spending it. ?e same principle applies to EdTech. If schools wish to make deliberate, e?ective choices about digital tools, the ﬁrst step is to confront the reality of how those tools are currently being used. You’ll ﬁnd a Leader Reﬂection Checklist at the end of this chapter to help pause and begin the process of comparing current practices against core values.1) Pause New Tech Adoption for 12 MonthsTo treat a wound, you must stop the bleeding. As long as schools are scrambling to adopt the latest apps and programs, they cannot properly assess the impact of those tools already in use. Getting ahead of the curve begins by pressing pause. A one-year moratorium on new tech adoption gives schools the space (both cognitively and operationally) to determine what’s currently working, what’s not, and what should come next.2) Ban Data Harvesting by DefaultMany people still assume platforms like Facebook and TikTok exist to sharephotos or connect friends. In reality, they were designed to harvest data. Every swipe, glance, and click feeds into a user proﬁle intended to make advertising more precise. Developers justify this by claiming they’re helping people connect with what they like most.   EdTech often follows this same playbook. Many adaptive and interactive learning tools collect far more data than schools realize—including facial expressions, voice recordings, typing cadence, and engagement patterns. Vendors justify this by claiming they’re personalizing learning for each student.But as we’ve seen, personalized learning delivers only limited gains inhighly speciﬁc circumstances. Do those narrow beneﬁts excuse collecting sensitive data from children—oftentimes without explicit consent? Worse still, several companies that once promised never to sell user data to third parties have since been caught doing exactly that. Why would EdTech ﬁrms be any di?erent?If a learning tool adapts only to immediate student input (for example,incorrect answers or hesitation time), then it may be acceptable. But if it constructs a proﬁle using longitudinal biometric, emotional, or behavioral data, it should be scrapped immediately. At minimum, review every system currently in use and disable all data-sharing features. Assume nothing—double check everything.3) Perform a Tech Audit?is is where implementation truly begins.  Begin with a hypothesis: how much screen time do you think students accumulate per day? Be sure to include both academic and between-class use.Next, collect data. Ask teachers and students to track their tech use forone week. Use whichever method best ﬁts your context: paper checklists, short daily surveys, classroom observations—so long as everyone uses the same system, consistency will ensure results are comparable and analyzable.  Teachers should track in-class use: which tools are used, by whom, for what purposes, and for how long? Students should track between-class use: which tools are used, for what purposes, for how long, and with whom (if anyone)? To support this process, you’ll ﬁnd Tech Use templates at the end of this chapter.Once the data is collected, compare it to your initial hypothesis. In myexperience, most leaders underestimate just how much screen time students actually accumulate. For reﬂection, consider:Where is tech use most concentrated?What is the primary purpose of most tech use?  Is in-class tech most often used for support, instruction, substitution, or distraction?Are the highest-usage periods also the most productive?  Remind everyone that this is a non-judgmental process. ?e goal isn’t to shame or assign blame; it’s to build clarity. Once you have an honest picture of current tech use, you’ll be in a much stronger position to decide what’s worth keeping, cutting, or rethinking moving forward.4) Conduct a Cost/Beneﬁt AnalysisEvery dollar spent on technology is a dollar not spent on books, professional development, or classroom resources. Fortunately, it’s relatively straightforward to determine whether or not digital tools are delivering a meaningful return.   Start by looking back at least ten years and charting your school’s annual expenditure on digital technologies. Don’t worry about the speciﬁcprograms just yet; the aim is simply to identify broad trends. Has spending remained ﬂat over time? Has it steadily increased or decreased? By how much each year, or by what percentage?  Next, chart academic performance over the same period. You might focus on a speciﬁc cohort (for example, ninth grade), a recurring assessment (such as ﬁfth grade reading results), or whole-school outcomes. Avoid relying on internal grades for this process, as two-decades of grade inﬂation have made this metric increasingly unreliable.Once both charts are complete, compare the trajectories.• If performance has improved in step with increased tech spending, you may be in a position to maintain—or cautiously expand—your digital infrastructure.• If performance has remained ﬂat while spending has risen, it may be time to reconsider your investment strategy.• If performance has declined despite higher spending, then it’s likely time to scale back and redirect funds toward more e?ective learning tools.  As a point of reference, consider the relationship between national E-Rate spending on digital technologies and National Assessment of Educational Progress scores (often called ‘?e Nation’s Report Card’) across the U.S. from 2018 to today. A simple pattern emerges: as EdTech spending goes up, performance goes down—a clear sign that a digital rethink is in order.  Once long-term value has been assessed, return to your tech audit and dig into the details. Which tools are used most? Which are used least? Which aren’t used at all? Discontinue any tools or subscriptions that are inactive, and reallocate those funds toward professional development or other resources with a proven impact on learning.A head of school I worked with in Melbourne once argued thatreturning to paper-based resources was ﬁnancially impossible—the printing costs would be too high. Yet when they conducted a proper audit, they discovered they were spending nearly one million dollars each year maintaining technology that went unused. By eliminating those expenses and shifting back to paper, the school not only covered its printing costs but saved a substantial amount. What looked like waste turned out to be e?ciency.Finally, identify where each tool is being used:• Are certain tools consistently used in high-performing subjects but absent in low-performing ones (or vice versa)? What’s the price tag on these tools? Can usage be reduced to only the contexts where impact is clear? In some cases, it may be better to limit use rather than apply a single school-wide standard.• Do the same tools appear across both high- and low-performingsubjects? If so, can we really say the tool is contributing to learning, or are other variables at play (e.g. teacher expertise, student background, time of day)? If the tool isn’t consistently linked to improved outcomes, is it time to reconsider its use?   ?is isn’t about scrapping tech—it’s about boosting learning. ?e goal is to ensure your tech budget supports real student growth. If it doesn’t, then it’s time to rethink and reinvest. To help throughout this process, you’ll ﬁnd School Tech Audit documents at the end of this chapter.5) Create a Tech-Use Task ForceOnce your audit and cost-beneﬁt analysis are complete, share the results with sta? and invite volunteers to form a dedicated task force. ?is group will help shape and support your school’s digital direction going forward. Include representatives from across the school (primary, middle, and secondary) to ensure decisions are context-relevant and cohesive. You may also wish to host focus groups with students and parents—their insights are often surprising and insightful. You’ll ﬁnd a Community Engagement Framework at the end of this chapter.?e ﬁrst goal of this task force should be to develop and publish yourschool’s EdTech philosophy. ?is document must go well beyond vague slogans like “we believe in innovation” or “technology is just a tool.” It should o?er clear, defensible guidance on when, why, and how digital tools will be used. ?is philosophy should be grounded in your school’s values, supported by established cognitive mechanisms, and aligned with observed student outcomes. It should address:• Primary Purpose: What core function will tech use—or tech avoidance—serve in your school?• Pedagogical Boundaries: How will technology support teaching andlearning, and what practices (e.g. handwriting, feedback, deep thinking) will it never replace?• Developmental Sca?olding: How will tech use evolve by age or year level?• Data & Privacy Standards: What data will—and will not—be collected, stored, or shared?• Equity Concerns: Will technology be used to address inclusion? If so, when and how?• Purchasing & Expenditure: How will tech-buying decisions be made, and by what criteria will tools be evaluated?• Sunset Clause: How often will each tool be re-evaluated (e.g. every three years), and what’s the process for removal if it proves ine?ective?  To assist, you’ll ﬁnd an adaptable Schoolwide Tech Philosophy document at the end of this chapter. With a clear philosophy in place, your school can begin the process of change with coherence, purpose, and transparency.Phase 2: Begin the Journey?is phase is all about reframing how your community sees technology and building buy-in from your two most important allies: teachers and parents. It marks the ﬁrst step toward reducing overuse and rallying those people you’ll need to help rebuild a healthy learning culture.6) Demand DataEarly in my neuroscience training, my mentor treated a Parkinson’s patient with a novel therapy. After only one session, the patient’s tremors and freezing stopped completely for nearly thirty minutes. It felt like abreakthrough, and I was convinced we had all the evidence we needed to go public and change the world.I was wrong.   Future treatments never replicated the e?ect. In fact, it’s been nearly two decades and I’ve never seen another patient respond like that. It was a total ﬂuke.I’m sharing this story to make one point clear: anecdote is not evidence. EdTech companies often rely on glowing testimonials to sell their tools—uplifting stories of improved grades, inspired teachers, and classrooms brought back to life. While these anecdotes may well be true, they are not proof of consistent or widespread e?ectiveness. Once your tech procurement policy is in place, make rigorous data a non-negotiable requirement. Proper evidence should:• Come from Independent Sources: not the vendor or a?liatedresearchers.• Be Collected at Scale: involving thousands of students across diverse contexts.• Deﬁne Boundary Conditions: clarify when the tool works, for whom, and under what circumstances.  Be especially cautious of the researcher allegiance e?ect: when a tool appears e?ective only when demonstrated by the developer. If impact depends on a charismatic salesman or a specially trained evangelist, it will vanish the moment that tool reaches a typical classroom. For this reason, always insist on trial periods before committing fully to any tool.Finally, don’t simply install a digital tool and walk away. Be sure tomonitor its usage and impact at regular intervals. Many platforms produce a short-term bump in performance, but fail to maintain a meaningful impact after six, nine, or twelve months. Be prepared to pull the plug if you startseeing signs of this decline e?ect in your school. Good tools will stand the test of time.7) Isolate Tech to a Lab or a CartIn the early days of television, changing the channel required e?ort. You had to stand up, walk to the set, and turn a physical knob. Because of that friction, most viewers picked a single program and stuck with it.   ?en came the remote control. Suddenly, switching channels required nothing more than the ﬂick of a ﬁnger. With all friction removed, channel surﬁng exploded and sustained focus declined.   ?e same principle applies in schools. When digital devices are immediately accessible—in desks, on shelves, in backpacks—teachers and students are far more likely to use them by default. Screens get used not because they’re necessary, but merely because they’re there.?e solution? Reintroduce friction.  Move devices to a designated computer lab, or store them in a shared cart that must be booked in advance. When teachers must plan ahead, reserve time, and escort students to a remote location, tech use becomes a deliberate act rather than a mindless reﬂex. By reintroducing e?ort, schools can shift technology from habitual to intentional.8) Formalize an ‘Opt-Out’ Choice for FamiliesRoughly 12 million students in the United States lack reliable internet access at home, and millions more face unstable electricity or housing conditions. For those students, digital literacy remains limited and online learning is a constant frustration. ?at fact alone is reason enough to formalize every family’s right to Opt-Out of school-mandated technology use—both during and after school hours.  Beyond infrastructure, many families have made a conscious choice to limit their children’s exposure to screens. Some do so for religious reasons, others for health concerns, and still others simply because they want to give their children the best chance at a grounded, socially rich childhood. ?ese parents work hard to preserve o?ine routines and minimize digital intrusion.To override that work with compulsory one-to-one computing is notmerely technological overreach—it’s moral overreach. “We chose fewer screens at home,” one parent shared with me. “It would be respectful for the school to honor that choice, but they’d rather mandate something our pediatrician told us to avoid.” Schools can certainly provide devices for those who want or need them. But no child should be denied a public education because their parents refuse to have them staring at a screen for hours each day.Denying families the right to Opt-Out is coercion, not progress.9) Give Teachers a No-Tech VetoMany schools adopt digital tools primarily to streamline administrative tasks: learning management systems for reporting, dashboards for data analysis, online portals for collecting and grading homework. Unfortunately, while such tools may help schools run more e?ciently, they can also interfere with genuine learning.If teachers judge that handwritten assignments are more e?ective thandigital submissions, or that face-to-face feedback is more meaningful than automated comments (and can support those judgments with evidence or sound reasoning), then they should be empowered to veto tech use within their own classrooms.Technology can indeed be a powerful productivity tool for adults. Butschool is not about optimizing adult workﬂows—it’s about optimizing student learning. Any tool that negatively impacts student performance doesn’t belong in school, regardless of how useful it may be for teachers. And every teacher should have the authority to say so.10) Recenter the Teacher?ere’s a street in Melbourne I call Academic Row. In just a few short miles, you pass more than two dozen public, private, and religious schools. At the start of each school year, this street is ﬁlled with banners and billboards, all selling the same pitch:“Explore our New Virtual Reality Lab.” “Make Sure Your Child Is Future Ready.” “Education Without Boundaries.”  Inevitably, these slogans are paired with images of students grinning in AR goggles, laughing over laptops, or staring in awe at a 3D printer. In every case, technology takes center stage.  You know one thing I’ve never seen on Academic Row? A school celebrating its teachers.  If you choose to scale back technology, it’s not enough to emphasize what’s being removed (“No More One-to-One Devices!”)—you must also highlight what’s being restored. Rebrand ‘low-tech’ as ‘high-rigor’, and shine a spotlight on your school’s greatest asset: knowledgeable, caring, and dedicated teachers. Let your billboards show students grinning over books, laughing during lively class discussions, or staring in awe as a teacher performs a daring scientiﬁc experiment. ?e message should be unmistakable: education at your school is about people, not products.Phase 3: Shift the CultureOnce early reforms are underway, you can begin the process of reshapingyour school’s culture. ?is means building shared rituals, shared expectations, and shared norms around how students and teachers will engage with technology—and with each other. ?e overall goal is to reclaim those habits, environments, and relationships that foster deep, meaningful learning.11) Implement a Bell-to-Bell Smartphone BanAs Harold highlighted at the start of this chapter, banning smartphones is one of the simplest and most cost-e?ective ways to improve student health, wellbeing, and learning. But half-measures don’t work—if phones are allowed ‘sometimes,’ the beneﬁts tend to vanish.Several countries have analyzed the e?ects of full school-day phone bans—and the results are consistently positive. In the UK, low-achieving students gained 0.14 standard deviations; in Spain, 0.12; in Norway, 0.22. In fact, every credible study using international testing data points in the same direction: bans boost learning.   In response, many governments are implementing nationwide bans. But you don’t need to wait for legislation—you have the power to ban phones in your school today. For support, you’ll ﬁnd a draft Phone Policy at the end of this chapter, ready to adapt for your community.  With that said, it’s important to be realistic: the ﬁrst few weeks are bound to be tough. Whether it’s quitting smoking, drinking, or any other deeply ingrained habit, withdrawal takes time. Expect pushback, and allow four to six weeks before conducting your ﬁrst evaluation. Once the adjustment period has passed, check in with everyone. Consider asking:STUDENTS:& How often have you used a smartphone at school in the past month?& How often have you seen others using smartphones?& Has school felt di?erent in the past month? How?& Has lunchtime or recess felt di?erent? How?& Have classes felt di?erent? How?& Have you personally felt di?erent? How?   & Have your relationships with teachers or peers changed? How? TEACHERS:& How often have you seen smartphone use at school in the past month?& Have you noticed a shift in classroom culture?& Has student interaction changed—within and between classes?& Have the number and severity of behavioral problems changed?& Have classroom interruptions increased or decreased?& Have you noticed changes in learning behaviors or outcomes?& Have you observed any change in deep thinking or understanding?   ?is is not a judgmental exercise. ?e goal is simply to take the school’s temperature and assess general levels of compliance and acceptance. Repeat this survey each term to track qualitative trends. ?en, at year’s end (once ﬁnal testing data is available), conduct your ﬁrst formal quantitative evaluation of learning impact.Finally, evidence suggests academic performance continues to improveyear-on-year as a phone-free environment evolves from a short-term adjustment to a deﬁning feature of school culture. Stick with it.12) Introduce Tech-Free DaysFamilies across the world are reclaiming time for genuine connection by embracing “Tech-Free Sundays”—a weekly ritual where everyone unplugs for twenty-four hours. Without digital distractions, families can share meals, play games, and rediscover the simple pleasure of being together.Schools can borrow this idea. Designate one day as fully tech free foreveryone—students and teachers alike. No laptops, no smartboards, not even projectors. When teachers cannot rely on slides or online content, they turn to discussions, demonstrations, and hands-on strategies long shown to outperform screens. When students cannot swipe or scroll, they turn to one another—boosting connection, conversation, and comprehension.Without screens, schools feel louder, more alive, and more human. Asone physics teacher in the UK told me, “I planned for silence but got noise—the good kind.” His students argued over ideas, built a cardboard prototype, and asked for a second tech-free day to ﬁnish. “I didn’t touch my slides once,” he said. “It was like 2010 again.”   A single day won’t undo a culture of tech reliance, but it can disrupt it. It sends a powerful message about what truly matters, and gives everyone a chance to rediscover the richness of analog engagement. Sta? should retain access to emergency-use phones, stored privately and accessed only when necessary. Beyond that, let the silencing of screens make space for the noise of real life.13) Host an Annual “Analog Week”Once tech-free days have gained traction, consider going bigger: host a full week without screens. Importantly, this shouldn’t be about subtraction—it should be about replacement. Analog Week should revolve around a unifying theme, planned well in advance with input from teachers, students and families. For example:Health Week: Host a ﬁeld day, organize a basketball tournament, o?eryoga or dance classes—anything that promotes movement and physical activity.Self-Expression Week: Celebrate creativity with student art shows, talentperformances, writing workshops—anything that nurtures non-digital expression.  Science Week: Build hands-on labs, stage a school-wide science fair, undertake laboratory walks—anything that encourages exploration and discovery.   Connection Week: Play team-building games, run storytelling circles, create large group scavenger hunts or escape rooms—anything that strengthens human relationships.   ?emes could center on food, music, service, nature, world cultures, or anything else that showcases the richness of analog life. ?e goal is to make screen-free learning and socialization visible, joyful, and memorable. You might run this week entirely in-house, or partner with nearby schools to create shared events, campus swaps, or cross-district student conferences. Let technology take a back seat for one week each year, and bring community and curiosity back to the center of school life14) Cap Device Use TimeWhen I was a child, my parents imposed a hard limit: eight hours of television per week. ?at meant TV became an event; I knew exactly which shows I’d watch, when I’d watch them, and when to walk away to save precious minutes for later.   Schools can adopt a similar strategy with EdTech by capping device use according to year level, subject area, or overall daily limits. When teachers know there’s a hard screen-time ceiling (say, one hour per day), digital use becomes far more intentional. It encourages careful planning, coordination with colleagues, and a preference for deliberate use over default reliance.Limited screen time also prompts greater experimentation with analogmethods—some teachers may even use ‘tech time’ as a bargaining chip toorganize larger projects or assignments. “With a 60-minute cap, we started trading minutes across classes,” one Canadian languages teacher told me. “?e funny thing is, we kept the best digital pieces, and replaced the rest with whiteboards and notebooks. It made me realize how mindless I had become with screens.”One note of caution: don’t attempt a cap too early in your EdTechreform journey. Coordinating limited screen time requires school-wide alignment and a baseline of trust. Hold this strategy in reserve until sta? has bought into the broader vision and seen success with simpler changes.15) Make Long-Form Reading a Core School Value?e beneﬁts of long-form reading are hard to overstate. When students engage deeply with books, they develop attentional endurance, cognitive patience, and essential literacy skills like inference, evaluation, and synthesis. More than that, long-form reading remains one of the most powerful developmental tools we possess.When you perform an action (say, kicking a ball), your brain activates aspeciﬁc neural pattern. Remarkably, simply imagining that action triggers the same pattern. In other words, the human brain doesn’t strongly di?erentiate between action and thought.  Now the surprise: reading about someone performing that action also activates the same neural sequence. But this only occurs when the act is embedded in a longer, immersive narrative. Short-form texts like tweets, summaries, and headlines rarely provide enough context for the brain to simulate experience. And this e?ect extends beyond physical movement: emotional and social experiences are also mentally rehearsed through story. In this sense, books are the original virtual reality device. Just as ﬂightsimulators help pilots prepare for the sky, long-form narratives help children prepare for life.So, how can we bring long-form reading back?  Institute a daily reading block (say, 20–30 minutes) in which every student across every year level reads independently. To make this time e?ective, add light structure and accountability:Set clear expectations for reading time.O?er a curated list of age-appropriate book choices. Have students track progress with simple reading logs. Use guided journal prompts to support reﬂection.Host informal discussions to share insights.Encourage book presentations, perhaps during tech-free week.  One important caveat: do not grade this. ?e goal isn’t performance—it’s process. If students see reading as just another task to be assessed, engagement will dwindle. ?e aim is to rebuild a culture of sustained, immersive reading—not to chase marks.So Now Then…Most school leaders didn’t sign up to become tech auditors, policy writers, or cultural critics. ?ey signed up to support students. But in today’s climate, doing right by students means stepping into these larger roles—at least for a while.   If schools don’t reclaim education from digital proliferation, no one else will. Vendors won’t, lawmakers won’t, even parents are unlikely to speak up until they see schools taking the lead. ?is chapter o?ers a model, not a mandate. Whether you adopt all, some, or none of these suggestions, the goal remains the same: to help students think deeply and connect meaningfully. ?at means protecting and elevating what matters most fordevelopment—empowered teachers, coherent narratives, and deep human relationships.   ?e future of education will be written by leaders bold enough to say ‘?is isn’t working. We can do better.’So, let’s do better.LEADER ACT ION TO OLKITPractical Steps and Templates?e resources that follow are designed to equip principals, superintendents, and other administrators with practical ways to align school policy, practice, and culture around healthier tech use.?ese aren’t static blueprints, but adaptable strategies—tools you can use to set direction, empower teachers, and safeguard student learning.LEADER REFLECTION CHECKLISTUse this self-audit to pause and examine whether your school’s technology practices align with your values, your evidence, and your community.1: Evidence & E?ectiveness& Have we adopted technology only after reviewing independent, replicated research (not vendor claims)?& Do we regularly reassess tools against student outcomes and remove those that don’t deliver?& Have we conducted a cost–beneﬁt analysis comparing learning gains vs. ﬁnancial and human costs?2: Teacher Autonomy & Professionalism& Do teachers have genuine choice in how (or whether) to use tech, or are they locked into systems?& Are we providing professional development rooted in cognitive science and pedagogy, not just platform training?& Do we honor teachers’ professional judgment over the mandates of vendors?3: Student Learning & Development& Do students spend more time learning from humans than from screens?& Are foundational practices (handwriting, discussion, recall, extended reading) protected and prioritized?& Do we sca?old tech use by age, limiting early exposure and expanding only when developmentally appropriate?4: Communication & Transparency& Have we shared our school tech philosophy publicly with parents and sta??& Do families have a clear opt-out pathway for digital tools they ﬁnd harmful?& Are students, parents, and teachers regularly invited to feedback forums about technology use?5: Values & Culture& Is our school culture built around focus, relationships, and depth of learning rather than convenience and novelty?& Do our policies (e.g., phone bans, screen time limits) actively protect attention and community?& Do we treat technology as a support for learning, not the driver of it?6: Equity & Inclusion& Are we ensuring that tech policies do not disadvantage students without access to reliable internet or devices at home?Are we addressing the needs of students with learning di?erences in ways that are evidence-based (not just “there’s an app for that”)?(?is ensures “inclusion” is not misused as an excuse for more screen time)7: Financial Stewardship& Have we calculated the total cost of ownership (hardware, software, training, IT sta?, replacements) before adopting a tool?& Could funds spent on technology be better invested in teachers, books, or physical learning resources?8: Sustainability & Exit Strategy& Do we have a sunset clause for each platform, ensuring it will be re-evaluated (e.g., every three years)?& Do we have a clear exit plan if a platform fails (including data deletion, teacher retraining, and replacement processes)?Final Reﬂection& If most of your answers are “yes,” your school is aligned with evidence and values.& If many answers are “no” or “not sure,” pause new adoptions, engage your community, and recalibrate before moving forward.DRAFT SCHOOL-WIDE TECH PHILOSOPHYPrimary PurposeAt [School Name], we believe education is about people, not programs. Technology is not an end in itself—it’s a means to support focus, deepen learning, preserve human connection, and ensure equity of access. If a digital tool does not demonstrably advance these purposes, it will not be adopted.Guiding Principles• Teacher Autonomy is Essential: Educators are trusted to use professional judgment around tech.• Learning Requires Focus: Attention is our most valuable resource; we protect it.• Human Relationships Matter: Teachers and peers—not screens—are the heart of education.• Technology Serves, It Does Not Lead: Tech is welcome only when it clearly strengthens learning.• Community	Shapes	School: Families, teachers, and students share responsibility for aligning technology use with our values.Supported Tools & Conditions• Typing: Allowed for ﬁnal drafts or accessibility needs; drafting and note-taking remain by hand.• Adaptive Tutors: Permitted for practice and review; not for introducing new concepts.• Presentations: Used selectively, with teacher oversight, to support communication and inquiry.• Research Tools: Used only when hardcopy is unavailable; will print most readings.Restrictions• Smartphones: Not used during the school day.• Gamiﬁed Apps: Not used for core academic subjects; not used as a substitute for authentic practice or instruction.• Social Media: Never part of instructional practice.Expectations for School Practice• Handwriting and personal feedback remain foundational practices.• Drafting, notetaking, and revision are conducted o?ine unless speciﬁc accessibility needs apply.• Tech may sca?old practice, but not replace instruction, dialogue, or critical discussion.• Homework must be printable and accessible o?ine.• Screen-based classwork is limited to: none in the early years, no more than 3 hours per week in the middle years, and no more than one hour per day in the upper years. ?is limit includes both individual and group screen use during academic time.• All new EdTech tools must undergo a TECH AUDIT (evidence of learning beneﬁt, cost-e?ectiveness, data privacy).• Parents and students will be consulted before major EdTech adoptions.Developmental Sca?olding• Early Years (K–5): Minimal tech, prioritizing handwriting, play, and face-to-face learning.• Middle Years (6–8): Introduction of supervised practice tools; limited screen exposure. Tech use cannot be mandatory and must be guided.• Secondary (9–12): Gradual increase in purposeful digital research and production tools, always under teacher guidance and always optional for families.Data & Privacy Standards• No student data is collected, stored, or shared without explicit parental consent.• ?ird-party apps must comply with privacy law and only the minimum data required for functionality may be collected.• No data may ever be used for commercial purposes.• Student behavioral/engagement metrics (e.g., ‘time-on-task’) will not be used as academic assessments or grading inputs.Equity & Inclusion• Devices will never be mandated as a condition of enrollment.• School-provided devices will be available for students who require them.• Tech use will be adapted to support—not segregate—students with learning needs.Purchasing & Expenditure• All procurement decisions undergo a TECH AUDIT, evaluating:1. Independent evidence of learning beneﬁt2. Cost e?ectiveness vs. analog alternatives3. Privacy and security standards• All programs must be piloted (with opt-in participation) before whole-school consideration.• Final approval rests with a committee of school leaders, teachers, and parents.Sunset Clause• Every tool must be re-evaluated every 3 YEARS. Tools failing to meet standards will be retired, regardless of sunk cost.Our Commitment?is charter is not anti-technology. It is PRO-LEARNING. We commit to shaping a school environment where every digital choice strengthens attention, preserves relationships, and prepares students for a lifetime of deep, human learning.DRAFT SCHOOL PHONE POLICYReclaiming Attention & CommunityAt [School Name], we believe learning thrives when students are fully present—focused on their work, connected to their peers, and engaged with their teachers. Phones disrupt that focus, which is why we’re committing to a school culture that prioritizes attention, community, and wellbeing.Core Policy• Phones O? & Away: From the ﬁrst bell to the last, phones must remain turned o? and stored in a locker or a phone pouch.• Tech for Learning Only: School-provided devices (when used) are for class purposes only. Personal phones are not part of instruction.• Emergency Access: Students can always be reached through the main o?ce. Parents who need to contact children during school hours will be supported by sta?.• Before & After School: We will not police students o?-campus or during non-academic times. However, we strongly encourage zero-use while on campus outside of class, so the environment remains consistent and focused.Why ?is Matters• Focus & Attention: Students can give their full energy to lessons and discussions without distraction.• Community & Connection: Lunchtimes and recess become times for play, conversation, and friendship.• Wellbeing & Calm: Freeing students from constant alerts and messages reduces stress and anxiety.Enforcement• Hold For a Period: If a phone is visible or in use, sta? will remand the phone for the duration of the class.• Hold For the Day: Repeated use will result in sta? remanding the phone for the duration of the school day.• Family Contact: Continual issues will warrant a discussion between teachers and family, and may result in punitive processes.Our Commitments• We will provide alternative, non-tech ways for students to access resources.• We will ensure parents can always contact their child quickly through the school o?ce.• We will review this policy annually with input from students, sta?, and families.?is policy reﬂects who we are as a school: a community that values curiosity, connection, and calm focus. By reclaiming our attention, we strengthen not just learning, but the relationships and rhythms that make our school thrive.TECH USE TEMPLATESTEACHER USE FORMDid lEmployWhat type/for whatFor howlong (inDid mystudentsWhat type/for whatFor howlong (inTech?purpose?min)?employ tech?purpose?min)?MONDAYPERIOD 1PERIOD 2PERIOD 3 PERIOD4 PERIOD 5TUESDAYSTUDENT USE FORM Did 1Employ Tech?What Type/ for What Purpose?For How Long (in min)?Alone or With Others?MONDAYBEFORE PERIOD 1BREAK 1LUNCHBREAK 2RECESSBREAK 3AFTER PERIOD 6TUESDAYBEFORE PERIOD 1BREAK 1LUNCHSCHOOL TECH AUDIT TEMPLATEPurpose & Alignment• What was this tool originally purchased/introduced for?• Does it clearly align with our stated school philosophy (e.g., learning requires focus & e?ort)?• Is it used primarily for teacher administration, student engagement, or student learning?• Could the same outcome be achieved just as well (or better) without technology?• Is this tool developmentally appropriate for all age groups, or only some?• Are teachers forced to use this tool, or is it optional?Evidence• Do we have independent research (not vendor claims) that shows this tool improves learning?• Do we have our own school data showing positive e?ects?• Has it been formally reviewed or trialed since adoption?• What’s our plan to re-evaluate its e?ectiveness (e.g., every 3 years)?Cost• What is the annual cost (licensing, training, hardware upkeep)?• What is the time cost for teachers (setup, troubleshooting, learning curve)?• What is the student cost (screen hours, distraction risk, privacy trade-o?s)?• What valuable non-tech experiences are being replaced (handwriting, discussion, reading time)?Impact• How often is the tool actually used in practice? By whom? For what?• What do teachers say about it? (supportive / neutral / burdensome)• What do students say about it? (helpful / distracting / irrelevant)• Does this tool support equity (e.g., accessibility features, fair access) or does it widen gaps?• Net E?ect: Does this tool strengthen focus, memory, and relationships, or does it fragment them?Rating & Decision For each tool, mark:• Keep: Aligned with values, supported by evidence, low cost, positiveimpact.• Fix: Has potential, but needs tighter limits, better training, or periodic review.• Cut: Doesn’t serve learning, too costly, or consistently harmful.At the end of the audit, schools can publish a TECH SCORECARD: a simple one-pager that shows parents and sta? which tools passed, which are on probation, and which are being phased out. ?at’s where transparency earns trust.DRAFT COMMUNITY ENGAGEMENT FRAMEWORKHosting a “Tech & Learning Forum”Purpose: To bring parents, teachers, and students together in a structured, respectful space to discuss how technology should (and should not) shape learning at [School Name]. ?e forum’s goal is to align community values with school practice and produce concrete next steps for policy.Step 1: Invitation & FramingSend a warm, collaborative invitation:• “We want to hear from you. Technology touches every student, every day — but we know it works best when families, students, and teachers help shape how it’s used. Please join us for a Tech & Learning Forum to share your experiences, your concerns, and your hopes. Together, we’ll create a community vision for technology in our school.”• When & Where: Schedule an evening session at the school hall orlibrary. Provide childcare (if possible) to encourage attendance.• Who Facilitates: Neutral moderator (e.g., assistant principal, trusted teacher, or outside facilitator).• Framing Message: “Tonight is not about debating gadgets. It’s about clarifying what kind of learning we value, and ensuring our technology use supports — not undermines — those goals.”• ~90 minute session: 10min Brieﬁng | 15min Student Panel | 30min breakout | 30min report.Step 2: Setting the ToneProvide a short 10-minute brieﬁng on:• Share Ground Rules: respect, equal airtime, focus on ideas not individuals.• Name the Goal: “We’re not here to sell a program. We’re here to shape our school’s future together.”• Current school tech practices (hours per day, devices used, homework requirements).• What research says about tech and learning (keep it parent-friendly).• Key challenges families and teachers are experiencing.Forum Goals (to keep visible)• Align community values with school practice• Give students and families a real voice in tech decisions• Build trust and transparency• Ensure technology serves learning, not the other way aroundStep 3: Student VoiceBegin with a student panel (10–15 minutes).• Invite 3–5 students (di?erent ages) to share candid experiences: “What helps you learn at school?”; “What helps you learn on a screen?”; “What gets in the way of learning?”• ?is centers the conversation on lived reality, not abstract arguments.Step 4: Breakout DiscussionsDivide into mixed parent/teacher/student groups. Give each group a facilitator and 3–4 prompts. —Encourage groups to capture 3 key priorities each.1. Values: What do we believe learning requires? (e.g., focus, interaction, deep thinking)2. Supports:	Which	technologies	actually	help,	and	under	what conditions?3. Boundaries: What practices/devices harm learning, and how should welimit them?4. Equity: How can we ensure all students succeed, regardless of access?Step 5: Sharing & Synthesis• Each group reports back.• Facilitators record responses on a board/wall for everyone to see.• Identify overlap, themes, and consensus areas.Step 6: Draft Community Priorities• From the discussion, identify 3–5 shared priorities. Example:• Protect handwriting in early years | Limit daily screen use in classrooms | Ensure homework is o?ine-accessible | Safeguard recess and play.Step 7: Next Steps & Accountability Close with a clear action path:• Establish a small task force (parents + teachers + leadership) to turnthese priorities into a draft School Tech Philosophy / Charter. Circulate the draft for community feedback.• Schedule a follow-up forum in 3–6 months to review progress. Provide an ongoing feedback channel (anonymous survey, email).End the Evening with a Clear, Positive Message:• “We may not all agree on every detail, but tonight we a?rmed what matters most: focused learning, healthy kids, and a strong community.?at’s our anchor as we move forward.”?is format ensures the forum feels collaborative, student-centered, and action-driven, rather than a top-down PR event.OceanofPDF.comOceanofPDF.comOutro“You may call the people a mob, but do not forget that a mob too often speaks the sentiments of the people.”—LORD BYRON  n the prologue, we met the Luddites: a group of 19th-century revolutionaries who smashed textile machines in protest against the growing incursion of technology into their way of life. Today, they are mocked		as	ignorant	peasants		raging	against	progress	they	couldn’t understand. But this is a distortion. ?e Luddites were never ﬁghting against	machines—they	were	ﬁghting		for	the		human	 rhythms	andrelationships those machines threatened to erase.  Although they ultimately lost and culture shifted around them, their warning was clear: beware adopting tools designed to reshape the world without ﬁrst reckoning with their ultimate cost.  Two centuries later, we face the same dilemma. ?e arena has shifted from factories to schools, and the tools are no longer looms but laptops, tablets, and smartphones. But the question remains unchanged: will we allow technology to hollow out the human practices that education has always rested upon without ever collectively considering what we are losing in this exchange?   ?ere is no denying that EdTech has already reshaped classrooms. Glowing screens have replaced dog-eared textbooks. Personalized learning platforms have replaced back-and-forth discussion. Uniform keyboards have replaced scribbled notebooks. And the result is not progress but decline: weaker memory, fragmented attention, and signiﬁcantly diminished learning. ?ese are not minor side-e?ects of a novel tool; they are symptoms of a deeper redeﬁnition of education itself.Multitasking is now praised as a virtue, detachment is dressed up asresilience, and impulsivity is rewarded as creativity. Reading has been reduced to skimming. Writing has been reduced to editing. None of this is neutral—each shift ﬂatters the tool while reducing the human.  EdTech advocates believe that resisting the inevitable march from one device to the next is quaint, backwards, or even dangerous. But as Marshall McLuhan once reminded us: “?ere is absolutely no inevitability as long as there’s a willingness to contemplate what is happening.”   ?at is all I have hoped to do in these pages: inspire you to pause, to contemplate, and to reckon with what is happening inside our schools. Because the story of humanity is not a tale of gadgets; it’s a tale of values.   ?is was never a book about rejecting technology; it was about reclaiming education as a deeply meaningful and human endeavor. It wasn’t about moving away from screens; it was about reorienting toward deep thought and true understanding. It wasn’t about scrapping devices; it was about reclaiming rigor.I am not anti-tech. I am pro-learning.I am a Luddite.But what about you? What are your core values?  Do you believe schools exist to serve students, or developers? Do you believe that hard work, struggle, and sustained e?ort are essential forwisdom? Do you believe that physical books, face-to-face conversations, and silent reﬂection remain meaningful ingredients for growth?   If your answer to any of these is ‘yes,’ then you already know what ideals are worth defending. ?e only question left is: will you act?Will you stand up? Will you push back? Will you be a Luddite with me?OceanofPDF.comAfterwordBy Sophie Winklemanhitney Houston’s “Greatest Love of All” opens with the unforgettable line: I believe the children are the future; teach themwell and let them lead the way.   ?is sentiment strikes an alarming and uncomfortable note in our current era.   ?e decision-makers behind the contagion of screens in today’s classrooms clearly do not believe that children are the future; they believe AI and algorithms will carry the burden of human progress. And so, they do not teach our children well—and these children will not be able to “lead the way” to the local bus stop, let alone toward new paths in human greatness.All of this is laid bare in ?e Digital Delusion—the book so many of ushave been waiting for.   Disa?ected teachers heartbroken by what’s happened to the classroom—their view from the front now a sea of Apple logos instead of children’s faces. Employers, ba?ed by their youngest recruits’ hemorrhaging attention spans and inability to think deeply. And thousands of parents like me, who watched what online learning did to their children during lockdown, only to see those “emergency” screens remain long after the crisis had passed.  Money has trumped matter. EdTech is now a multibillion-dollar industry, while the disastrous impact on children’s learning is plain to see. Jared has done all the hard work here, and we owe him a debt of profound gratitude. He has shown, both clinically and practically, that learning from screens is a wildly, crazily inferior form of education compared to human teachers, books, and analog strategies.For those who think school ends with the ﬁnal bell, remember: aninferior education shapes a child’s mind for life. Surely a brain that has never truly focused, wrestled with di?cult material, or built genuine intellectual stamina will struggle to handle the complexities and road bumps that mark a full and ﬁbrous life. Shallow learning paves the way to shallow living, paper-thin resilience, and a life shot through with vacillation.And here’s the devastating truth: in ?e Digital Delusion, Jared hasn’teven touched on the other horrors of digital learning—the mass data breaches, the Mao-esque surveillance, the ruined sleep, the disrupted hormone production, the degeneration of both eye and spinal health, the gruesome content a click away, the relegation of beloved teachers to heartless chatbots. But he didn’t need to, because EdTech’s single, towering defense against all this negative impact—“but it improves your child’s learning”—collapses under scrutiny.Some parents might be willing to tolerate privacy violations, addictivedesign, and behavioural proﬁling from these tools if they genuinely led to better learning. But this book makes it clear that there is no better learning. And once that pillar falls, the entire EdTech ediﬁce follows. What parent would accept surveillance and worse education? Addiction and diminished cognition? ?e single supposed beneﬁt vanishes, EdTech has no leg to stand on.Give this book to teachers, heads, parents, governors, policy makers,politicians—anyone and everyone connected with children.If they act, it’s a sign they care about children.If they stay the digital course, it’s a sign they care about money.?at’s a harsh truth, but I honestly can’t see it any other way.OceanofPDF.comREFERENCESPROLOGUE“…grew up, on average, healthier, happier, and better educated…” HealthierCole, T. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences, 117(28), 16096-16099.Note: In the ﬁeld of psychology, this di?erentiation between exposure-based and directed learning is typically called Biologically Primary (neuronal reﬁning) and Biologically Secondary (neuronal recycling) learning. Biologically primary learning refers to skills or knowledge that are acquired naturally with little conscious cognitive input or e?ort. ?ey are skills that human beings have evolved to naturally acquire over millennia (think facial recognition or social bonding). Biologically secondary learning, on the other hand, refers to skills or knowledge that are acquired via intense cognitive input and e?ort. ?ese are skills that human beings have only relatively recently invented (think calculus or heart surgery). Biologically primary learning principally occurs during the ﬁrst ﬁve years of life. During this period, the brain naturally moves from processing a broad array of general stimuli into processing only those speciﬁc stimuli relevant to a child’s immediate environment. Of importance, this process of neuronal reﬁning does not require deliberate exertion; mere exposure to stimuli is enough for the brain to adapt. Unfortunately, on-balance the brain shifts from primarily synaptogenesis to primarily synaptic pruning by around age of 5. ?is coincides with the shift into biologically secondary learning. Now, mere exposure is no longer su?cient: rather, in order for the brain to adapt, sustained focus and cognitive e?ort is required. ?is demanding process of “neuronal recycling” is the primary reasonschools exist: to facilitate the process of learning culturally mediated (rather than biologically mediated) knowledge. ?e push for self-directed learning confuses biologically primary and secondary learning. ?ere is little question that the vast majority of students who employ EdTech tools have progressed into biologically secondary learning. ?is means when they are confronted with pure autonomy, they are relegated to undertaking guidance-free trial and error experimentation. Although this form of learning can prove beneﬁcial for adults and experts, research has long shown it is very di?cult and ponderous for children and novices, leading many to lose motivation and quit. As educational psychologist Richard E. Mayer has simply stated, “?e debate about discovery [learning] has been replayed many times in education, but each time, the evidence has favored a guided approach.” It is perhaps worth pointing out that none of this speaks directly to pedagogy; it does, however, speak to the importance of guidance. Every signiﬁcant pedagogy—from play to inquiry to direct instruction to ﬂipping the classroom—confers better learning when it is mediated by expert guidance. Indeed, the ability to lead and support students through the acquisition of new knowledge is arguably one of the most powerful skills human beings have evolved.Geary, D. C. (2012). Evolutionary educational psychology.Sweller, J., Kirschner, P. A., & Clark, R. E. (2007). Why minimally guided teaching techniques do not work: A reply to commentaries. Educational psychologist, 42(2), 115-121.“Trial-and-error can work, but it’s incredibly slow...”Clark, R. E. (2009). How much and what type of guidance is optimal for learning from instruction?. In Constructivist instruction (pp. 170-195). Routledge.Sweller, J., Kirschner, P. A., & Clark, R. E. (2007). Why minimally guided teaching techniques do not work: A reply to commentaries. Harvard University Press.NOTE: ?ere is a sixth myth that supports EdTech, but it was too tangential to this text to keep within the main text. ?at myth is that digital learning—speciﬁcally Massive Open Online Communities (MOOCs)—will democratize learning and close worldwide learning gaps. In the ﬁrst 10 years of their existence, over 35 signiﬁcant global MOOC platforms were created serving over 220 million learners from all 195 countries. It is certainly impressive that millions of people from all around the world have engaged with MOOCs, but this statistic is ultimately meaningless. As any educator can attest, what matters is not how many students enroll for a program; what matters is how many students successfully complete a program. Of the 160,000 people who enrolled in the ﬁrst MOOC, only 23,000 completed the course; that’s a completion rate of about 14%. Unfortunately, that proved to be the high-water mark; more recent analyses put MOOC completion rates consistently under 10%. Of greater importance, however, is the commonalities amongst graduates. Individuals who successfully complete MOOCs and similar online learning programs typically share three characteristics: ﬁrst, they come from a?uent regions (an estimated 80% of MOOC completers come from the world’s most a?uent countries); second, they have already earned a graduate degree (an estimated 80% of MOOC completers have a bachelor’s degree with 44% boasting a master’s degree); and third, they possess strong self-regulated learning (SLR) skills. Self-regulated learning refers to an individual’s ability to take agency over and meaningfully control his/her own learning process. Importantly, SLR is not an innate ability; rather it is a set of skills that must be developed through modelling and explicit practice. Which leads to an important question: where do people primarily experience relevant modelling and explicit practice? You guessed it: formal education. A long history of research has demonstrated that SLR skills are best developed in a group learning setting with structured guidance from an experienced teacher. Furthermore, attempting to embed training and prompts within digital courses like MOOCs appears to have little impact on SLR development. ?is is the dagger for the assumption that EdTech will close learning gaps. If e?ective digital learning requires pre-existing and well developed SLR skills, and most people develop these skills via an apprenticeship model within formal education, then online learning is most likely to serve the already educated rather than those in need of an education—thereby widening rather than reducing the gap. When the majority of MOOC graduates already have a graduate degree, this is a clear sign that we are witnessing a Matthew E?ect (To those who have, more will be given—to those who don’t have, more will be taken away). Perhaps the most obvious example of EdTech’s inability to close learning gaps came during COVID-19. When brick-and-mortar schools shut down and global learning moved online, this should have been the breakout moment for EdTech: ﬁnally, the world would bear witness to the powers of purely online learning. Unfortunately, COVID-19 lifted the veil and revealed just how feeble and ill-distributed digital technology truly is. UNESCO estimates that at least half ofstudents worldwide expected to access remote learning were unable to do so due to divides in technological access; this included not only computers, but also television and radios in less developed countries. Within the US speciﬁcally, an estimated 16 million students were pushed out of education due to a lack of internet connectivity or a reliable digital device. ?is ultimately led to a widening of the already present socioeconomic achievement gap within the US by ~20% in mathematics and ~15% in reading. As education specialist Mark West has noted, “?e evidence overwhelmingly [suggests] education became less accessible, less e?ective, and less engaging when it pivoted away from physical schools and teachers and toward technology exclusively.”Chen, Z., Liu, M., & Zhou, R. (2025). ?e more the better? How excessive content and online interaction hinder the learning e?ectiveness of high-quality MOOCs. He is the creator of ?e Learning Blueprint, an international award-winning program helping educators and students understand how learning actually works. Jared has conducted research and taught at Harvard University, Harvard Medical School, and the University of Melbourne, and has worked with more than 1,000 schools around the world. He is the author of six books, has published over sixty research articles, and his work has appeared in ?e New Yorker, ?e Atlantic, ?e Economist, Harvard Business Review, and ABC’s Catalyst. He currently serves as Director of LME Global, an organization dedicated to bringing cutting-edge brain and behavioral science to educators, students, and communities.OceanofPDF.com
